HIS
MAJESTY KING GEORGE VI.
THE SENATE.
VOLUME XXVI.

---

Tuesday, 16th February, 1943.
The Senate met. at Half-past Two of
the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in the Chair.

Prorogation
Prorogation
THE SENATE

PROROGATION OF PARLIAMENT.
Mr. SPEAKER: I have it in command
from His Grace the Governor to read
forthwith in this Chamber to the Senators
and Commons of Northern Ireland therein
assembled His Grace's Proclamation proroguing this present Session of Parliament.
com-
Resolved: "That the Gentleman
Usher of the Black Rod be
manded to inform the Commons that the
Proclamation proroguing the present
Session of Parliament is to be read
forthwith, and inviting the Commons to
attend in order to hear the same.”—(Mr.
Robb.)
The Commons, being at the Bar, with
their Speaker, the Proclamation was read
to both Houses of Parliament by the
Speaker of the Senate (in pursuance of
His Grace's command), as followeth :-
BY HIS GRACE THE GOVERNOR
OF NORTHERN IRELAND.
A PROCLAMATION.
For Proroguing the present Parliament of
Northern Ireland.
WHEREAS it is provided by section
eleven of the Government of Ireland Act,
1920, as modified by and in pursuance of
certain enactments amending the said
Act, that the Governor of Northern Ireland shall, in His Majesty's name,
summon, prorogue and dissolve the Parliament of Northern Ireland:
AND WHEREAS it is desirable that
this present Parliament of Northern
Ireland should be prorogued to the
twenty-third day of February, one thousand nine hundred and forty-three:
NOW I, JAMES ALBERT EDWARD,
Duke of Abercorn, Knight of the Most
Noble Order of the Garter, Knight of the
Most Illustrious Order of Saint Patrick,
Governor of Northern Ireland, by this
Proclamation do publish and declare His
Majesty's Pleasure that the said Parliament be, as from the reading of this
Proclamation in the Parliament of
Northern Ireland, prorogued to Tuesday,
the twenty-third day of February, one
thousand nine hundred and forty-three.
one
AND I do hereby further declare His
Majesty's Royal Will and Pleasure to be
that the said Parliament shall on the said
twenty-third day of February,
thousand nine hundred and forty-three,
assemble and be holden at Stormont for
the despatch of divers
divers urgent and
important affairs, and the Senators and
Members of the House of Commons of
Northern Ireland are hereby required to
give their attendance accordingly.
Given this sixteenth day of February,
one thousand nine hundred and fortythree.
(Signed by his own hand),
ABERCORN.
GOD SAVE THE KING.
End of the Fifth Session (opened

Tuesday, 23rd February, 1943.
The Sixth Session of the Fifth Parliament of Northern Ireland appointed to
meet on 23rd February, 1943, in the
Seventh year of the Reign of King George
VI., was opened by the Governor (His
Grace the Duke of Abercorn, K.G.,
K.P.) in person.
The House met at Five Minutes before
Twelve of the Clock noon, Mr. SPEAKER
(the Right Hon. Viscount Bangor,
O.B.E., D.L.) in the Chair.
KING'S SPEECH.
His Grace the Governor, being seated
on the Throne, commanded the acting
Gentleman Usher of the Black Rod
(Brigadier-General H. G. Young, C.I.E.,
D.S.O.) to let the Commons know "It
is His Grace's pleasure that they attend
him immediately in this House."
The Commons being at the Bar with
their Speaker, his Grace delivered the
following speech from the Throne:—
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
The people of Northern Ireland, who
have followed with pride and admiration the gallant exploits of the armed
forces of the United Nations in every
theatre of war, face the tasks which
lie ahead with renewed determination
and courage. My Ministers are COoperating to the fullest extent with His
Majesty's Government at Westminster,
inspired by the common purpose of
freeing the world from the tyranny of
an aggressive and brutal foe.
The continued happy relations between my Government and the various
branches of His Majesty's Forces, and
the cordial spirit of comradeship between the civil population and members
of the British, United States and other
Allied units in Northern Ireland,
afford me the greatest satisfaction.
Although many thousands of our
young men and women have volunteered
for the fighting services, many more are
still required, and I earnestly hope that
the call to duty will be answered to an
even fuller extent than in the past.
The Ulster Home Guard, which, I am
pleased to record, has reached a high
state of efficiency, reflecting the greatest
credit on all ranks, is also in need of
recruits, and I trust that those whose
duties permit will enrol in this Force,
which is so vitally necessary for the
protection of the Home Front.
My Ministers continue to attach the
utmost importance to all measures of
Civil Defence. I am glad to know that
the period of relief from air raids has
been occupied by the Civil Defence
Services in perfecting their organisation
and training their personnel in the light
of the lessons learned by grim experience, and I am confident that all
A
King's Speech
King's Speech
THE SENATE
will be ready to meet any emergency
which may arise. I am pleased to
learn that good progress has also been
made with the unification of our local
fire brigades into the new National Fire
Service.
I have been gratified by the magnificent response made by the whole
agricultural community to the appeal
by my Government to increase the
production of food and flax during the
past year. Notwithstanding unfavourable weather and many other difficulties
only a very small proportion of the
harvest was lost, and the valuable
assistance rendered by members of the
Forces and by many of our people has
earned the gratitude of our farmers.
This season even greater efforts must be
made by the civilian population to save
the harvest. It is also essential that
there should be a further substantial
increase this year in our ploughed land
and a still larger proportion devoted to
barley, oats and potatoes. My Ministers
hope that at least one hundred thousand
acres of flax will be grown and that not
less than eighty. per cent. of the crop
will be dam retted.
When I last addressed you certain
industries were facing a difficult period
because their normal products could
not by their nature be regarded as
essential under war-time conditions. On
the principle that it must be our aim to
use all available machinery and man
power for war purposes, strenuous
efforts have been made during the past
year to adapt these industries to the
use of alternative raw materials and to
new types of production that have their
definite place in our war economy. It
is satisfactory to know that considerable progress has been achieved
particularly in branches of the textile
industry.
During the past year the output of
our shipbuilding, engineering and
textile industries has steadily increased,
and the growing demands for labour in
these industries and also in connection
with an extensive and important series
of works of construction have resulted
in a reduction in the number of persons
unemployed, during the year which
ended in January last, by nearly eight
thousand. Large numbers of women
workers, also, are now engaged on the
manufacture of munitions, which,
although new to them,
them, they are
performing with a high standard of
efficiency and zeal. It is the earnest
desire of my Government to ensure
that, strenuous though the activities of
the workers and managements in our
factories have been, an even greater
effort will be made to achieve our
maximum production of essential arms
and war materials.
Closely associated with the production
of munitions and other war requirements is the provision of electricity for
power purposes. I am glad to learn
that the scheme for providing additional
supplies of electrical energy, upon
which work has been proceeding during
the last eighteen months, is nearing
completion, and that auxiliary plants
are now available to meet
emergency.
any
My Government has been concerned
about the restraining effect of derating
on social developments in our area and
a proposal will be laid before you at an
early date which will alleviate the
difficulties which Local Authorities
have experienced in carrying out essential improvements owing to the operation of the derating system.
The arrangements which are being
made, with financial assistance from
Exchequer funds, for the provision of
additional Hospital and Medical
Services under emergency conditions
will, when complete, effect an increase
of sixty per cent. in the total hospital
accommodation available for the people
of Northern Ireland.
Among the measures which you will
be invited to consider will be Bills
designed to strengthen the Civil
Authorities (Special Powers) Acts; for
the amendment of the law relating to
workmen's compensation, and for
making further provision for the protection and welfare of young persons
and the treatment of young offenders.
Members of the House of Commons.
The Estimates for the Public
Services will be laid before you in due
course.
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
It is a source of much satisfaction to
me to learn that my Government has
been actively engaged in making
preparations for dealing with the
problems which are likely to arise on the
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
23 FEBRUARY 1943
conclusion of hostilities, and that
considerable progress has already been
made in connection with post-war
physical reconstruction in relation to
town and country planning, and with
the provision of additional housing,
water supplies and sewage disposal
facilities throughout Northern Ireland.
The whole area will be included in a
planning scheme which is being prepared with the co-operation of the
various local planning authorities; and
the Planning Advisory Board and
Planning Commission, which have been
established, are proceeding expeditiously
with the work allotted to them.
Educational reforms must take an
important place in any plans for postwar reconstruction and it is the
intention of my Ministers to ensure that
our young people will be given every
opportunity to share in the developments to which we confidently look
forward after the war.
I am glad to know that effective steps
have been taken during the past year
to ameliorate the lot of old-age
pensioners and widows over sixty years
of age by substantial additions to the
rates of Supplementary Pensions where
required and also by the distribution of
grants for the provision of clothing and
blankets to the value of forty thousand
pounds.
I am gratified to learn that the
scheme for the provision of milk, either
free or at reduced rates, to schoolchildren and also to expectant mothers
and children up to five years, has proved
of substantial benefit.
It is satisfactory to note that, notwithstanding the strain of war conditions, the public transport undertakings
in Northern Ireland, both road and
rail, have been able to meet the greatly
increased demands made upon them for
the carriage of passengers and merchandise.
During the past year grave outrages
have been committed by those elements
who are striving to undermine our
Constitution; and all ranks of the Royal
Ulster Constabulary and the Class "B"
Special Constabulary deserve the highest
commendation for their devotion to
duty and bearing in dealing with the
situation.
My Government has been in consultation with the Imperial Authorities in
regard to the necessity for exercising
control over persons entering Northern
Ireland who were not ordinarily resident
in Northern Ireland on 1st January,
1940. A scheme came into operation
on the 1st January last whereby, with
certain exceptions, all such immigrants
who wish to reside in Northern Ireland
for more than six weeks are required to
be in possession of residence permits.
It is intended that the scheme will be
kept in operation for a reasonable time
after the war to facilitate the reinstatement in employment of demobilised men
from Northern Ireland who joined His
Majesty's Forces as volunteers.
Public interest has been aroused by
the issue of the Report on social security
by Sir William Beveridge. Whatever
improvements are effected by the
Imperial Government in connection
with the social services in Great Britain
will be carried out on a similar scale in
Northern Ireland.
I pray that the blessing of Almighty
God may rest upon your deliberations.
Then his Grace was pleased to retire,
and the Commons withdrew.
House adjourned during pleasure.
House resumed.
Papers Presented to Parliament.
Ministry of Labour:
Factories (Operations at Unfenced
Machinery, Amended Schedule) Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1942.
National Health Insurance and Contributory Pensions (Internees) Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1942.
Ministry of Home Affairs:

for
Inspectors' Reports for
Mental Hospital in Northern Ireland.
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lie on the Table.
Member Sworn.
Major John Robert Perceval-Maxwell
took and subscribed the Oath.
Address in Reply to His Majesty's Most
Gracious Speech.
SPEAKER having read
Mr.
King's Speech,
the
The LORD MAYOR OF BELFAST
(Sir Crawford McCullagh) (in morning
Address in Reply
King's Speech-
THE SENATE
[Sir Crawford McCullagh]
dress): I beg to move that an humble
Address be presented to His Grace in
the following words:
Your Grace,
We, His Majesty's dutiful and loyal
subjects the Senate of the Parliament
of Northern Ireland beg leave to offer
our humble thanks to your Grace for
the speech which your Grace has addressed to both Houses of Parliament.
I appreciate the honour paid to the city
of Belfast, and to me, by the Government
in asking me to propose this Motion. We
in Northern Ireland, in common with
Great Britain, the Empire and our Allies,
find ourselves still engaged in war against
the forces of aggression. We are proud
to be in this partnership and to take our
share, so far as it is in our power, in the
struggle. We look forward in the future
-perhaps in the near future to see the
complete defeat of our foes, the enemies
of all civilised mankind. The great victories of our Russian allies and those of
our own Eighth Army, the events of this
winter in North Africa, with the continued efforts of our other Allies, give us
every right to confidence.
We are gratified to learn that the efforts
made by the people of Northern Ireland
to show hospitality and comradeship not
only to members of the British Forces but
to those of the United States and our other
Allies have given your Grace satisfaction.
The Gracious Speech expressed the hope
that many more men and women would
join the fighting services from this area.
With this we agree. We would have preferred that equality of service could have
been ensured by the extension of conscription to Northern Ireland, but this does
not lie in the power of the Government of
Northern Ireland.
The gracious reference to the Ulster
Home Guard will, I am sure, be greatly
appreciated, and I hope the request for
additional volunteers for this very necessary service for our country's protection
will be readily responded to.
The Civil Defence Services, as has been
said, are continuing their training in the
light of experiences gained in actual air
raids, and to the many citizens who are
giving voluntary and enthusiastic service
we are under a debt of gratitude.
The great efforts of the agricultural
community, which is so important, are
commended in the Gracious Speech. This
will give real satisfaction to those engaged
in that industry, and encourage them to
even greater efforts. The appeal for
volunteers to help in the harvesting of this
year's crops is one which I am sure will
be responded to generously by the citizens
of Northern Ireland.
The fall at January last in the number
of unemployed is a matter for satisfaction.
We hope that workers in trades classified
as "non-essential" will be more rapidly
absorbed into war work, and that the
number of unemployed may be reduced
still further.
We cannot but deplore the labour
disputes which have, occurred in Northern
Ireland. It seems a pity that the
machinery which exists for negotiation is
not always availed of.
As Lord Mayor of Belfast, I am
specially pleased by the reference in the.
Gracious Speech to derating. While I
agree with the. principle of derating, that
is that industry should be helped in
times of trade depression-I have always
felt that once the business or firm
emerges from depression to prosperity, it
should bear its full share of rates; and
further, that the "little man" in business
and trade is the one who should benefit
by derating or some other form of assistance and encouragement. In other words,
transfer it from the wealthy firms to the
poor, struggling traders.
The arrangements being made to provide greater hospital and medical services.
will commend itself to all who know of
the long waiting lists for admission to our
voluntary hospitals.
So
Reference is made in the Gracious
Speech to preparations being made by the
Government to deal with problems which
will arise on the. conclusion of hostilities
in connection with town-planning.
far as the cities and towns of Northern
Ireland, especially Belfast, are concerned,
it is hoped that full advantage will be
taken of the various schemes being prepared. The City of Belfast, in particular,
has suffered damage by enemy action, and
out of evil may come good. The development of open spaces, which may add to
the enjoyment and health of our citizens,
would be of great benefit, and there is
little doubt that the planning authorities
will consider this point with others when
preparing their schemes.
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
23 FEBRUARY 1943

The reference in the Gracious Speech
to additions to be made in the payments
to old-age pensioners and widows will be
approved. It is only right that these old
people should be compensated for the
increased cost of living, and be in a position to enjoy some comfort and cheer.
All right-thinking people must deplore
the activities of illegal organisations, the
members of which seek by force and
outrage to impose their views on others.
I sometimes wonder if these people appreciate their good fortune in living under
the protection of the British flag; that
their existence is ensured only by the
efforts of the many gallant men who have
given, or are risking, their lives in the
protection of this island.
The reference in the Gracious Speech to
the consultation of the Government of
Northern Ireland with the Imperial
authorities in regard to exercising control
over persons entering Northern Ireland,
who were not ordinarily resident here on
the 1st January, 1940, will be much
appreciated. One of the objects is to
facilitate the reinstatement in employment of demobilised men from Northern
Ireland who have served their country.
This is only right.
The future of social security, foreshadowed in what is known as the
Beveridge Report, has been before the
Imperial Parliament. This is a controversial question, but I am glad to understand that any improvements effected by
the Imperial Government will be carried
out in Northern Ireland. I beg to move.
Major BOYLE (in morning dress): I
am deeply sensible of the honour which
has been conferred on the county to which
I belong by my being asked to second the
Address of thanks for the Gracious Speech
from the Throne, which has been so ably
proposed by the Lord Mayor of Belfast,
whom we have so recently welcomed back
to this House. I must ask the forebearance of this House in my shortcomings,
following such a distinguished and experienced Member.
During the year we have just passed
through our Province has been honoured
by a visit from their Most Gracious
Majesties the King and Queen, who, in
the midst of their many duties, found
time to visit this small outpost and loyal
part of their Empire, and at a time when
the war outlook was somewhat different to
what it is to-day. The spontaneous
welcome they received did not altogether
express the great appreciation their loyal
subjects felt at their Majesties sparing
time to come among us and see us as they
did. The arrangements made for their
Majesties' visit were thorough in spite of
the many difficulties that had to be foreseen and overcome.
We must feel proud that our small
Province has supplied three of our most
distinguished generals-Field-Marshal Sir
John Dill, General Alexander and General
Montgomery, the latter two having so
successfully driven a resourceful enemy
some 1,700 miles from the doors of Egypt.
Possibly the Government might consider
sending them our congratulations and our
fervent hopes and wishes that they will
shortly, with the help of our Allies,
victoriously drive the enemy right out of
North Africa.
In the fourth year of war the appearance of our countryside has changed.
Instead of green fields, on which cattle
grazed, there is plough everywhere. Many
small and dilapidated mills have been
renovated and remodelled, water courses
cleared, and water is again turning wheels,
and so creating cheap power. New small
factories have been erected to deal with
flax and food production. Land improvement schemes by drainage, etcetera, have
been carried out and farm workers have
had their rates of pay increased.
this has been made possible by the very
considerable financial help given by the
Government by way of grants in aid,
subsidies, bounties, etcetera, amounting in
the last year to some £170,000. There
were also loans to farmers amounting to
£130,000 and one and a half millions was
paid to growers of potatoes. The farmers
and workers have now to be included in
what one has heard described as the
privileged classes of income-taxpayers.
All
In spite of the unfavourable weather
and with the help cheerfully given by the
troops and voluntary part-time workers
from the towns, the farmers succeeded in
gathering a plentiful harvest. No appea
has been made to the farmers and farm
workers to which they have not responded
with patriotism and enthusiasm.
It is satisfactory to know that legislation is to be brought in to strengthen the
Civil Authorities (Special Powers) Act.
We must all agree that every power
should be given to assist the authorities

be sent to the Irish Generals I will bring
to the notice of the Prime Minister.

department of education we have encountered difficulties on the financial side.
Our financial arrangement with regard to
the distribution of the cost of progress in
educational matters between the Con-
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
23 FEBRUARY 1943
solidated Fund and the local rating
authority was that when a rate of one
shilling was levied in any area, further
Government assistance ceased. Future
developments in education are almost
inevitably bound to be financed from
local sources, but, as the House is probably aware, our system is quite different
from the English system. We pay the
whole of the teachers salaries, and we
pay a proportion of the rates that are
levied for educational purposes.
In England the system is different.
There the local authorities control the
whole of the finance in their areas, and
they receive a grant from the central
fund which amounts to 51 per cent. Our
central fund grant amounts to 85 per
cent. at least. Our future progress in
education is bound to be in the direction
of the development of technical education,
the development of scholarships, and
probably the development of the feeding
of the children. It has been found in our
own area that if those things are
to be financed in a district in
which derating operates, mainly in
the country, the cost of those
services, unaided now by any further
Government grant, is going to fall upon
the underated premises and will cast upon
them a most inequitable burden. That
is a problem that has been present to the
mind of my Ministry for a long time.
Now we are really up against it, and it
is a matter that will have to be investigated and for which some solution will
have to be found.
It is too early yet to say on what lines
that solution will probably proceed, but
that something must be done is, I think,
obvious to every person.
In the future
development of my department we are,
of course, bound to go more or less,
though not absolutely, step by step with
England. We have had notice that there
is going to be a new Act touching education in England, but whether or not the
Board of Education and the Government
have made up their minds definitely what
they are going to do we do not know, and
I do not know whether they know
themselves.
There are two suggestions that have
been made. One is that the compulsory
school leaving age shall be raised to 16,
and that, in addition, there shall be parttime education up to 18. Those are, no
doubt, excellent ideas, but people who
talk about improvement and development
along those lines ought to remember that
if those are going to be the lines of
development they may take a long time
to put in force. You cannot have enlarged classes without more teachers; you
cannot have enlarged classes without more
class rooms; and it must be a long time
after the conclusion of hostilities before
such a programme, if adopted, can be
put in force. Buildings will be required,
and they will be difficult to get. They
will also be costly. Should progress not
be made definitely upon those lines there
are other directions in which we might
proceed.
We in Northern Ireland could perhaps
best advance in education by a further
development of our day technical schools.
I do not say that they are second to none,
but I do say they are clearly in advance
of anything they have in England or
Scotland. Our technical education has
always been in advance of what they have
had in England, and it seems to me that our
day technical schools can be developed,
and developed at a not prohibitive cost.
There is another direction in which our
education in Northern Ireland ought to
be developed, and that is by the further
provision of scholarships for the children.
of parents of poorer means both to
secondary schools, apart from day
technical schools and the University. In
those two directions educational reforms
seem certain to proceed without being
hampered by an impossible war of
expense.
There is, of course, another direction
in which educational reforms can be
carried out, and I am speaking about
education because it is the department
with which I am most familiar. That is
by a reduction in the size of the classes of
elementary schools. It is quite true that
our schools are in some places and at some.
periods of the year very much too large.
That seasonal increase is a curious thing.
It comes on about the month of April
and lasts until June, and there is a
redistribution after the harvest. What
happens is that in the cities and towns
mothers keep their own children at home
during the bad weather in the winter
and then they send them to school. We
have had the curious experience of schools
in Belfast with a normal enrolment of 45
springing up to 70 in April, with no
provision for extra teachers during those

seasonal increases which come at the end

required so many more teachers that our

nearly £300,000 a year. We would have

people, should not talk glibly about small

people should remember to count the. cost.
One other matter that I might refer to

farmers to reap their crops this year.
It is a serious matter and one that has
been pressing very heavily upon the

does count upon the goodwill and
enthusiasm of all our people in cities,
towns, and urban districts in helping to
solve this problem, and, after all, they
should help. If we compare the situation
of our country now with the situation two
years ago, and even when we met about
this time last year, we ought to be thankful for the events of the past few months,
which have given us ground for definite

for the future. There is nothing else to

Mr. BROWNE: I should like to join in
the congratulations to the mover and
seconder of the Address in reply. Their
remarks were in admirable taste and
covered, in a concise manner, all the salient
features of the Gracious Speech. I have
attended all the official openings of this
Parliament since its establishment, and I
find that to stand up here and attempt to
speak is entirely different from sitting in
the Press Gallery and listening to other
Members. However, if one is to be of any
service in the work of legislation one has
to deliver his maiden speech, and that is
what I am about to do.
The Gracious Speech cannot be described
as other than hopeful, encouraging and
inspiring. It does not contain much of a
controversial nature and yet it refers to
subjects calling for serious consideration.
It is gratifying to know that Northern
Ireland is bearing its fair share of the
terrible conflict now being waged throughout the world. By fair share I do not
mean that there should be a limit to our
endeavour. There should be no maximum
on our part.
It is not the fault of our Government
that conscription does not operate in
Northern Ireland. On two occasions the
Government tried to have the Compulsory
Service Act applied to this part of the
United Kingdom, but without success.
We are glad, however, to know that under
the voluntary system thousands of our
young men have joined His Majesty's
Forces.
In the different theatres of war Ulstermen have given good accounts of themselves. Wherever the fighting is hottest
they are to be found, and the distinctions
awarded to many of them testify to their
gallantry. This was only to be expected.
In the last Great War the men of Ulster
the
proved their worth. They wrote upon
pages of Britain's history a deathless story
of valour and heroic effort, and in the
words of King George V showed how
Ulstermen can fight and die.
King's Speech--
Address in Reply
23 FEBRUARY 1943
I have no doubt that when the present
titanic struggle is over the record of
Ulstermen for bravery and determination
will be no less brilliant. Alas, many of
them have paid the supreme sacrifice, but
though they have gone the spirit which
animated them remains.
It is interesting to note that four
Ulstermen hold key positions in the
Army-Sir Alan Brooke, Chief of the
Imperial General Staff; Sir John Dill,
Chief British Military Representative in
Washington; Sir Harold Alexander,
formerly Commander-in-Chief Middle
East; and General B. P. Montgomery,
Commanding the Eighth Army.
There are many other Ulstermen who
hold important positions-Sir Roger
Keyes, Admiral of the Fleet; General Sir
Claud Auchinleck, General Sir W. P.
MacArthur, Air Vice-Marshal Cole-
Hamilton, and Acting Air Vice-Marshal
Tyrell.
Since the outbreak of hostilities we
have had our fears, anxieties and reverses,
but these have only knit us more closely
together and made us more determined,
not to cease until Hitler and his gangsters
have been utterly routed and smashed.
To quote the words of our beloved
Sovereign: "We are determined to fight
on to complete victory with no thought
of parley."
And here it might not be out of place
to put on record a striking tribute from
Mr. Churchill. Writing recently to our
own Prime Minister he said:
"When war broke out the resources
of Ulster were pledged in support, and
this pledge has been observed with
characteristic loyalty and generosity in
the field of battle, whether in the air,
on land or on the high seas, as well as
on the home front.
"Ulstermen are to be found not only
in the famous Northern Ireland regiments, but also in every branch of His
Majesty's Forces and in whatever
quarter of the world they may be fighting. At least three of our most distinguished military leaders - Dill,
Brooke and Alexander, the victor of
El Alamein come from Ulster shores.
Seamen of Ulster, Ulster ships,
harbours and airfields are a determining
factor in the Battle of the Atlantic,
and in industry and agriculture alike
your country men have played their
part in producing the sinews of war.
"I am confident that Ulster will
continue this unrelaxing effort with
characteristic doggedness through the
stern days which lie before us."
Already we see the silver lining in the
dark clouds, and I am sure it is the
prayer of us all that the day of complete
victory will soon dawn, and with victory
peace.
That day will be hastened if only everyone will resolutely do his duty. The call
for more recruits is urgent and there
should be a prompt and ready response.
While we are gratified at the achievements of those in the Army, Navy and
Air Force, we must not forget the
magnificent work of those whose duties
are indispensable to war production and
who are devoting their spare time to the
Home Guard and the Civil Defence
organisations.
Together with the Special Constabulary,
a reliable and admirable force, these
organisations are ready to meet any
emergency which may arise and to
maintain the Home Front. After all,
to defend one's home and one's family is
an obligation which should not be passed
on to others.
are
I should also like to pay a meed of praise
to our women folk who have joined the
Services open
or
to them,
who
engaged in every sort of voluntary work
in connection with the war.
We appreciate what they are so willingly doing
to assist our fighting men.
Reference is made in the Gracious
Speech to the American troops from
across the seas. Between the people of the
United States and ourselves there are
many bonds of kinship, common interests
and love of freedom. We recall with
pride that 14 of the Presidents of the
United States are of Ulster descent and
that the Declaration of Independence is
in the handwriting of an Ulster Scot.
Those of us who attended the ceremony
at the City Hall last month when a stone
was unveiled to commemorate the first
anniversary of the arrival of U.S.A.
troops on British soil-the British soil in
this case being Ulster soil could not fail to
be impressed and thrilled by what we
saw.

THE SENATE
[Mr. Browne]
Had Northern Ireland not been an
integral part of the United Kingdom those
American troops could not have landed on
our shores nor could we have greeted any
of the British or Dominion forces. And
I dread to think what might have happened to us if all Ireland-North as well
as South-had been neutral and we had
been left to our own resources without the
protection of American and British troops
and the Royal Navy and the redoubtable
R.A.F. We also rejoice in the striking
tribute paid by Ulster on Saturday last to
the Red Army which has written such a
glorious chapter in defence of its native
land.
Not only have our people given of their
man power to the service of the Empire,
but they have contributed their money to
the prosecution of the war. It is almost
literally true that money fights. "Save for
victory" and "Lend to defend" are true
statements in themselves as well as being
sound slogans.
War Weapons Week,
Warship Week, Tank-to-attack Week and
the various Government issues including
Ulster and National Savings Certificates
have all been generously supported.
Altogether our people have up to date
invested in Government securities about
£58,000,000-surely an enormous amount
for this little part of the United Kingdom
and one-third of that is made up of small
sums. In giving this substantial and
necessary aid the contributors have the
double gratification of helping their
country in its hour of need, and at the
same time making a very sound investment of their savings.
I have no doubt that "Wings for
Victory Week” will also be well
supported:
Deserving of special mention is the
Spitfire Fund inaugurated by Major
William Baird. That fund was mostly
contributed to by the working classes in
Northern Ireland and by friends of
Ulster throughout the Eimpire and up to
the present no less a sum than £90,000
has been raised. It was certainly a
wonderful piece of work.
There are other subjects to which one
might refer such as the products of our
textile factories, mills, engineering workshops, and shipyards. All these industries are busily engaged turning out the
sinews of war. But I must pass them
over without further reference.
I should like, however, to draw attention to the important part being played
by the Ministry of Agriculture in the
drive for victory, and I think we are very
fortunate in having at the head of that
Ministry a gentleman of such great enterprise and wide practical experience as
Lord Glentoran.
Since the outbreak of war the Ministry
of Agriculture has become in essence a
Food Production Department directing
and controlling the production of crops,
livestock and their products in accordance
with national needs.
Some idea of the magnitude of the
Ministry's commercial or marketing
activities may be gathered from the fact
that the Ministry's annual trading turnover and financial transactions arising
from its war services amount to over
£33,500,000, involving well over 1,000,000
purchases and sales.
The farmers have responded splendidly
to the appeals to produce more food. In
the words of Lord Glentoran, "No country
in the world has given a better farmers'
contribution acre by acre than Northern
Ireland. No country has ploughed more.
than our farmers have ploughed, and our
products have been good. Our position
in the markets of Great Britain has improved and is likely to remain a strong
plank in the post-war platform.
""
It is hoped and expected that our
farmers will do even better this year than
last year.
I have alluded to the Civil Defence.
Services and it is gratifying to know that
they are in a state of efficiency. Air-raid
shelters which were found to be defective
have been strengthened or reconstructed
and many more have been built. Additional fire appliances including engines
have been provided, fire watching and
fire prevention measures have been improved and the fire services generally
have been unified and brought under
single direction as the National Fire
Service of Northern Ireland. Further
attention has been paid to evacuation
schemes and the provision of accommodation for workers engaged in essential
industry.
All that precautionary work is absolutely necessary as one never knows when
and where our enemies may next strike.
Though the war is not over and no one
can foretell when it will end, we cannot
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
23 FEBRUARY 1943
afford to ignore the future. That would
be a foolish attitude.
In July last our Prime Minister on
behalf of the Government outlined a postwar policy. He said, "We must be ready
to play our part in translating into practice the ideal of a full and free life for
all, for which the democracies are now
struggling."
What could be more democratic than
that. Many of the problems which we
shall have to face will be linked up indissolubly with those in Great Britain,
but we will also have our own problems,
and it is to these specifically that we must
direct our attention.
Naturally Mr. Andrews did not go into
detail, but he told us sufficient to show
that the Government intends seriously to
grapple with many of the problems which
will arise at the close of hostilities. He
spoke of housing, town-planning, transport, electricity, agriculture, sewerage,
drainage, water supplies and industries.
That is certainly an ambitious programme, but it is largely our domestic
concern, and it will be for the Government and Parliament together with the
local authorities, assisted by the people
generally, to carry it out.
Already representative committees are
at work investigating various aspects of
the question, and the reports which they
will present should be very helpful towards finding practical solutions.
Since Mr. Andrews made his statement
the Beveridge Report has been issued. It
is a comprehensive document, dealing
with social insurance and requires very
careful study.
Already it has aroused some controversy. I notice that the Belfast branch
of the British Medical Association has
passed resolutions dealing with the
medical proposals in the report and demanding freedom of choice as regards
treatment on the part of doctors and
patients.
However it is believed that a good part
of the Report-at least 70 per cent. of
the proposals-will be adopted at Westminster, and our Prime Minister has
declared that "whatever scheme is ultimately agreed to in Great Britain will be
applied to Northern Ireland." That
will be in accord with the Government's
declared policy of maintaining our social
services similar to those in Great Britain
and so preserving our equal citizenship
which we all so greatly prize.
What is looked for and has hitherto been
looked for in vain is nothing less than a
rew home policy. Only a courageous and
far-reaching policy, proclaimed in broad
outline with the full weight of Government authority can build up a national
conviction that victory will bring to the
people of this country a "freedom from
want" and a "freedom from fear"-and
above all, from fear of unemploymentnot known in the years before 1939.
And this reminds me of the arrangement come to between the British Government and our Government whereby power
is given to control immigration into
Northern Ireland. We do not forget at
the conclusion of hostilities in 1918 many
ex-Service men returned home only to find
their jobs filled by people who did not
belong to Ulster.
That should not have occurred, and I
am sure we all hope it will not occur this
time. The present arrangement should
be helpful in securing for the Northern
Ireland worker his rightful precedence
over outside competitors, provided, of
course, that his qualifications are equal.
The returned troops are entitled to their
pre-war occupations, or other work if at
all possible.
There is then the question of housing,
the importance and seriousness of which
cannot be overstated. I am afraid that a
generation or two ago there was little
or no sense of communal responsibility for
the personal health and physical wellbeing of the working classes. Fortunately
that has been changed. A new social conscience had been developed, and it is
accepted on all sides as a national duty
that the people shall be ensured social
conditions conducive to health and wellbeing.
Perhaps in no sphere has progressive
public opinion been more marked than in
that of housing. The days of indifference
and neglect are dead.
Even before the air raids in Belfast in
1941, which demolished some 32,000
houses and damaged about 53,000, the
housing conditions were not satisfactory,
and here I should like to pay tribute to
the Belfast Corporation, which many
years ago entered on a slum clearance

THE SENATE
[Mr. Browne]
campaign. Senator Sir Crawford McCullagh will remember the occasion.
I had the privilege of going round with

on a tour of inspection when he condemned
hundreds of houses as unfit for human

this city at that time were cleared and

The housing conditions in rural districts
at that time were also not up to the mark.
Many of the dwellings were mere hovels
which let in more rain than sunlight, and
in their places we have now bright and
attractive labourers' cottages. We want
many more such cottages.
We recognise that everything possible
should be done to encourage the people to
remain in the country, not to drift into
the cities and towns, and one of the best
ways of doing that is to provide them
with comfortable homes and desirable

some

and

cottages have been erected, the expenditure in housing subsidies amounting to
£3,364,677. It should also be remembered
that Parliament passed in 1939 another
Housing Act which would have been in
operation to-day, and under which large
numbers of houses would have been
erected, if it had not been for the war.
The air-raids in Belfast, which caused
such havoc, have accentuated the housing
difficulties. There is now the additional
problem of rehousing those who have lost.
their homes. Much has been done to
repair the damage. I understand that
repairs to approximately 42,000 such
houses have been carried out-not a bad
record.
We are glad to know, too, that persons
who have had to leave their damaged
dwellings and who are being charged
excessive rents for alternative accommodation, may make application to a rents
tribunal and have a fair and reasonable
rent determined for such accommodation.
I am just wondering whether that covers
the sub-letting of rooms in controlled
houses.
Enemy action in Belfast especially has
not only forced upon us a problem but
presented us with an opportunity. There
are now many open spaces in what were
formerly congested districts, and some of
us would like to see those open spaces
allowed to remain and to be used as playgrounds for the children. In the centre
of Belfast there are also open spaces
which might be utilised in a way to make
the city more attractive in its layout.
However, we can rely upon the technical
and other committees now dealing with
that subject to make proper suggestions.
But new houses to let at reasonable
rents, new houses for small owners-one
of the best and most stable features of
present-day national life-and new homes.
for those who have been condemned to
live in slums are not the whole of the
housing campaign. There must be an
attack, a systematic attack on the evils
of overcrowding.
There should be no leisurely drift in
dealing with overcrowding, neither should.
there be any slackening of effort in the
general housing programme and the slum
clearance campaign. The achievements
of the past should only be regarded as
incentives to bigger things in the future.
There should be no complacency in the
sphere of housing reform.
Healthy houses in well-planned areas
provided with ample open spaces are the
conditions essential to a healthy and contented people. All other health services
are supplementary to good housing just
as all other health services are crippled
by bad housing.
I might speak on other subjects, but
I am afraid I have taken up too much
of your time, and I conclude by thanking
you, my Lord, and the other Members of
the Senate for the patience with which
have listened to me.

Question again proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: I trust I may be allowed
to re-echo the feeling which was so generally expressed the other day, the feeling
of pleasure and gratification which was
experienced at the two speeches which we
We
had the privilege of hearing from the
right hon. Gentleman the Lord Mayor of
Belfast, who moved the Address, and
from the hon. and gallant Member who
sits opposite me, who seconded it.
have heard the Lord Mayor before in this
House, and we are very anxious to hear
him again. We rejoice to think that he
has been able to come back to us as full
of vigour as ever he was. The hon. and
gallant Member (Major Boyle) is a more
stable Member of this House, as he is one
of our elected representatives, and we are
always very glad to hear him when he is
able to address us.
I am not going right through the
Gracious Speech itself. It is, as was
remarked before, a document of very considerable length, and although the right
hon. Gentleman, the Leader of the House,
told us on Tuesday that there was a
certain lack of pabulum about it there
is still, I think, a good deal of opportunity
given by the Gracious Speech for consideration and discussion. As I say, I am
not going through the whole Speech, but
there are two or three points to which I
do want to direct attention for a few
moments, and at the conclusion of my
observations I want to try and draw a
particular moral from the Speech as a
whole.
The first matter I want to speak about
is agriculture, which looms rather largely
in the Gracious Speech. His Grace. says
he is gratified by the magnificent response
made to last year's appeal for an increase.
in food and flax production, and His
Grace lays stress upon the fact that very
little of last year's bountiful harvest was
lost. I am afraid I must say that I think
the view taken was rather optimistic.
is true, I believe, that practically all the
straw was gathered in, but I am very
much afraid that a great deal of the grain
was left behind, and also that when the
test of threshing comes the actual yield
will not be as great as was to be expected
by the promise of what was an unusually
bountiful harvest.
It
The farmer is going to be asked to do
more during this coming season.
I am
not at all sure that he is being asked
to do a little too much. The proposal is
that 45 per cent. of a man's arable land
is to be cultivated. Having regard to the
climatic conditions prevailing in Northern
Ireland, I am afraid the expectation that
that will be satisfactorily realised is rather
King's Speech-
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
unduly optimistic. There is no doubt you
will get your 45 per cent. ploughed. That
is one thing. You can get it sown and
possibly reaped, but are you going to get
it saved? I do not think, looking back
over the past two years, that the difficulty
of saving the crops has been so much a
difficulty as regards getting the necessary labour--though that has been severe
enough in some districts-but the difficulty has rather been unfavourable
weather. That is one thing the farmer
has always to contend with, and a thing
which he cannot counter.
Labour you
can provide for, but if you are to till 45
per cent. of your arable land-practically
half a man's holding-it requires a very
great trust in the elements, which we
cannot control.
The farmer has other difficulties to
contend with. Possibly one of the more
modern difficulties at the moment is the
increased expenditure on income tax,
which may, perhaps, be thought to be at
an unduly high rate as regards land
owned and occupied. The point is that
income tax at the rate of 10s in the
£ makes a rather undue demand on the
farmer, and does, in effect, curtail his
working capital. On the one hand you
are asking him for an increased tillage
area, and on the other you are depriving
him of the necessary sinews of war. That
is a matter which concerns those who sit
on the other side of the water, rather
than those in this Parliament, but it is a
matter that should be borne in mind.
To get
One of the great demands made on the
farmer this year is for a further growth
of flax. It is hoped, I understand, that
100,000 acres of flax will be grown, and
that 80 per cent. of it will be dam retted. I
expressed my own opinion as regards dam
retting last year when a Bill dealing with
flax dams was before this House.
80,000 acres of flax dam retted is a very
large task indeed. Whether it can be
done or not I do not know. The answer
will probably be different in different
parts of the country. One thing that is
common all over the country is a demand
for a higher price for flax. As to that
higher price, the Secretary of the
Ministry of Agriculture, speaking in
County Derry a week or so ago, said there
was no hope of an increased price, but I
rather gathered from a report in this
morning's newspapers of the proceedings
at Westminster, that another view may
be taken.
If you want flax grown you must make
the price thoroughly attractive. Many
people have no experience of flax growing
and are not likely to undertake it now
because of their other commitments.
Other people have a sound knowledge of
flax and they know not only how to grow
flax but they also appreciate the risks they
run with the crop, planting, gathering,
retting and so on.
I
pass from agriculture to another
matter which is perhaps not wholly disconnected, and that is the allusion in the
Gracious Speech to the effect of derating.
I must say that the allusion was rather
cryptic. The right hon. Gentleman the
Prime Minister, speaking in another
place, perhaps took the information a
little further. The right hon. Gentleman
the Leader of the House also referred to
derating in the speech which he addressed
to this House on Tuesday, and may I
digress for a moment to say that I have
rarely heard him in better form than he
was on Tuesday. I am not sure that I
quite agree with the conclusions he drew
from the difficulties derating does undoubtedly impose on the budgets of the
local authorities. He seemed to think
that land was possibly an available source
of further revenue. I am not so sure
about that.
I do know that the ordinary_rate
in the two rural districts in which I am
most interested, both in County Antrim
-Ballymoney and Ballycastle are going
to rise for the coming year by no less than
1s 1d in the £. They are going up a
penny more than the reduction which is
likely to be experienced in the city of
Belfast, and which will be very welcome
to the citizens. But 1s 1d rise in the
rates makes one think, and does not afford
any reason for casting any increased
burden on the local authorities. The
rural districts, which consist mostly of
farmers, who are ratepayers, have also to
contend, under present conditions, with
the War Damage contribution, which in
itself amounts to 2d or more than 2d in
the £. In addition, in some parts of
the country you have the Bann drainage
rate, which also adds to the existing
liability. There are, in fact, very heavy
burdens on the local rates already, and I
do not think this is a time when those
burdens ought to be increased.

Debate on the Address
25 FEBRUARY 1943
I rather gathered, possibly wrongly,
from the speech of the Prime Minister in
another place, that the intention was to
aid local authorities by a further contribution from the general exchequer, but
the Gracious Speech does not indicate
anything of that sort. In fact the
Gracious Speech is rather more
oracular than we could have wished.
That is a matter that will solve itself or
will be solved for us at a later day. I
see it is proposed that Parliament will be
asked to sanction further extensions of
the Civil Authorities (Special Powers)
Act. We all agree, I think, in this
House with the old maxim, salus
populi lex suprema. I do not think that
anybody here is likely to raise any objection to any step which may be regarded
as necessary. At the same time there
are matters relating to that Act which it
is difficult for us as a
constitutional
assembly to approve.
I rather hope if a new Bill is to be
presented, as I gather it is, that the opportunity will be taken to do two things.
One is to provide for a fairly frequent
recourse to Parliament for a renewal of
the power on the ground that if the
Government of the day requires special
powers of Parliament they ought to come
back to Parliament at intervals not too
infrequent for a renewal of the powers.
and to give an account of their stewardship if required. That is one thing. The
other is the great difficulty of the people
called internees; that is, people who are
kept in detention without being brought
to trial. It is a horrible state of things.
I cannot imagine that there is anybody in
the House who hates it more than I do
with the possible exception of the right
hon. Gentleman the Leader of the House.
In fact, no constitutionally-minded person can regard procedure of that kind
with other than dislike.
But as we know arrangements have
been made to put a check on what might
be described as the arbitrary exercise of a
great power. There is a tribunal to which
any internee has a right to apply. But
that tribunal is not, so far as I can
ascertain, of a statutory character. I
think it ought to be made statutory. It
would, I think, ease the passage of any
measure such as that adumbrated. It
would ease the passage, perhaps not so
much in this Parliament as
across the
water, if the right of recourse to that
tribunal
and I have no reason to doubt
it is a perfectly fair and impartial
tribunal which is quite satisfactory to an
innocent person-it would ease matters if
the right of recourse to the tribunal was
given by statute.
Again arrangements are forecast for
post-war planning. I can only say as
regards that, I do hope in any of these
arrangements an endeavour will be made.
to take the property owner along with
you in this endeavour to design planning.
There is too much tendency, perhaps,
to-day to appoint somebody or other
to deal with somebody else's property
according to his own sweet will. The
people who so deal or attempt to deal are
not always most experienced and have not
always full knowledge. But if you are
going to do any good in post-war planning
it is most essential that the people actually
concerned should be brought into your
counsels, so, as I say, if possible take
them along with you. There are opportunities in Belfast certainly for
readjustment of certain disfigurements,
may I call them, of the city. Some of
these have been removed, and the opportunity for rehabilitating the city and
effecting improvements is great. But great
care will have to be exercised, and also
great regard must be paid to the expense
involved.
a

After all, we have often heard the sad

Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL:
am glad to see that in the Gracious Speech
there was nothing to encourage a feeling .
of complacency. Indeed there is no reason
for complacency and in my judgment not
very much reason for great optimism. The
sentence which seems to me to be the key
sentence of the Speech is that which
contains the words "face the tasks which
lie ahead with renewed determination and
courage." We get from time to time a
feeling of optimism when our magnificent
King's Speech-
Debate on the Address
25 FEBRUARY 1943
troops win a victory. But I would point
out that that optimism is due to the feeling we all have of relief, and thankful
relief, that we have survived at all in this
struggle, and what is more, survived with
a limited power of striking back at the
enemy.
So far our margin of strength has
allowed us to strike back only in a limited
way. I should like to point out to the
House, and to anyone who thinks that
because of recent victories we are necessarily on the upgrade, that in no place.
where we are attacking the enemy is
German territory directly affected.
In
Lybia and Tunisia the German soldier is
fighting primarily the cause of an ally,
and he is bleeding and dying on the sand
to save from utter destruction an ally
whom he thoroughly despises. In Russia
he is fighting, and fighting magnificently,
under great difficulties, and in a cause.
which he must know in his heart is not
only unjust but unwise. In spite of all
these moral and physical disadvantages he
is fighting extremely well, and he can be
expected to fight even better when his own
country is threatened.
We should not be more than thankful
that we are doing as well as we are, and
I feel that some of the optimistic state. .
ments which are sweeping round the
country now are not justified by actual
events. We are deeply proud of the Army
of our Russian ally which has done such
magnificent work in defeating the enemy
at a time when we ourselves were too
weak to direct our attacks against him,
except on a limited scale.
The reason we have been so slow in
directing our major offensive against the
Germans is common knowledge. It is
because we had to build up man-power,
supplies and shipping. I would point out
that these three factors, which, so far,
have limited our attack, are matters which
cannot be remedied by the fighting forces.
They are in the hands of the civil population, and it is to the civil population to
which we must look to replace casualties.
That being so I am glad that one of the
strongest points made in the Gracious
Speech is in regard to recruiting.
have magnificent regiments from the
North of Ireland, and it would be a very
grave reflection on this country if those
regiments had to be filled by men who
have no connection with Northern Ire-
We
land. Belfast supplies many ships, and
the country districts can supply muchneeded food.
I should like to turn now to another
aspect of the matter. The Leader of the
House in his speech the day before yesterday referred to the administrative timelag which often intervenes between the
adoption of the Government's intention
and its fulfilment. It seems to me that
this time-lag, which is well known to
every administrator, and which has to be
taken into account in the making of all
plans, places on the Government the very
grave responsibility of making up its
mind with reasonable speed and getting
the administrative machinery working as
quickly as possible.'
The Government have stated that they
are going to improve the water supply. I
am delighted to hear that. The House
will, perhaps, remember that in 1936 and
in 1937 I pressed very hard upon the
Government to carry out this muchneeded reform. I pointed out then that
not only was the limited supply of water
and the unevenness of the supply a limiting factor in regard to agriculture, but
also in reference to industrial development. The answer I got then was that
the existing urban areas had adequate
water for their needs; that there were
plenty of wells in the country, and that
the Government, having reflected on the
matter, had come to the conclusion that
a comprehensive water scheme for the
Province was too expensive. I believe
the Government will now realise that if
they had expended money in 1937 it would
have been a very small amount compared
to the expenditure to which they will
now have to commit themselves.
In spite
of the delay I rejoice that they are now.
taking a realistic attitude in regard to
this matter which I regard as of primary
importance for the industrial and agricultural development of this country, and
also for an adequate and proper system of
town planning after the war.
I should like to point out that any
scheme for increasing the water supply
of this Province should be done in a consolidated way, so that full account may
be taken of the needs not only of such
urban areas as exist at present, but also
of the country districts and all possible
extensions of the urban areas in the
future, so that after the war it may be
possible to ask manufacturers from

THE SENATE

England to come over here and start industries with the sure knowledge that

very
Our contribution to the war effort in

creditable. There have, of course, been

are bound to occur in any industrial concern, especially when men are working
longer hours than they have been in the

Some reference has been made to derating, and on that subject I thoroughly
agree with the Lord Mayor of Belfast.
never could understand why these prosperous concerns should be relieved of a
considerable part of their liability, while
a greater burden fell on the smaller
struggling people. It looks rather ridiculous. I know of one firm in Belfast
that is doing an enormous business; it is
relieved to some extent of its liabilities,
while the burden falls on poor, struggling
trades. I think that is neither fair nor
reasonable, and I hope the Government
intend to do something about it. It is
certainly very bad.
Reference has also been made to the
question of workmen's compensation.
This is a matter that is long overdue. The
existing legislation requires amendment
owing to the high cost of living and the
improvement that has taken place in the
standard of living. The compensation
that is now being paid cannot adequately
compensate those who have met with
accidents, and I do think it is necessary
that something should be done to meet the
needs that exist.
I do not altogether agree with the
remarks that have been made about the
Special Powers Act. It is a serious
matter that there are some people who are
endeavouring by force of arms to compel
the majority to accept their views. I
think that is a very unfair position. I do
not know any better way of dealing with
those people than interning them and thus
preventing them from doing what they
intend to do. It is an old saying that
"Prevention is better than cure. So far
as I can gather it is the younger men who
are set to do the work that is plotted
behind the scenes. I do think the Special
Powers Act is very necessary. Legislation
of a similar kind is in operation not only
in the Free State but in England. We
were condemned at one time for passing
this legislation. I was speaking to a very
devout Roman Catholic, a very decent
fellow, this morning.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I did not think
you would speak to one. (Laughter.)
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
am in a charitable frame of mind.
(Laughter.) As I say, I was speaking to

with the I.R.A. He chased the man

of their own people was found chained to

Mr. TAYLOR: Two of them.

THE SENATE
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: Those
people are as much in danger as we are,
and I do not think the Government could
do better than collect the people who are
suspected of being dangerous and putting
them out of the way. Those who are
interned are not ill-treated in any way,
as internees in Poland and Germany are.
In fact people who are. suspected of being
a danger to the State in those countries
are shot. My friend, Mr. Jack Beattie,
seems to have a very poor opinion of the
treatment of the internees by the Government, but I think he has exaggerated the
position to such an extent that no one
will take much notice of his remarks. If
the Government require any further
powers to counteract the activities of these
people, I think both Houses of Parliament
would be very pleased to give them; it
would be only wise to do so.
a
The King's Speech also contains
reference to planning. I agree that something will have to be done to provide
better houses for the people. I am sorry
the Government have made no reference
to any action, or intended action, on their
part to provide houses for the poor
creatures who have been left homeless as
a result of the air raids. It was bad
enough that those people had to endure
the experiences of those two nights. Few
in this House have had the actual experience, but it was like a hell on earth.
I think something should be done, even
during the war, for the considerable
number of those people who are now living
in barns and outhouses, in many cases
families having only one room.
I know
it is difficult to get timber and materials,
but surely they could be supplied for this
purpose as easily as for the erection of
canteens and such places. I think the
Government should give this problem their
very earnest consideration. It would be
a good thing if they could put up reasonably-comfortable huts near the city to
enable men and women working in the
city to reside with their families. I
know men who travel considerable distances by bus and train in order to be
with their families in the evening and at
night. I am sorry the Government have
made no provision for the housing of
those people, although they have promised
that after the war something will be done
in that respect.
Another thing for which the Government deserve our commendation is the
control of persons entering Northern
Ireland. That was a very serious matter
in the last war, when people from over
the Border flocked into an industrial
centre like Belfast. After the war, when
the fighting men were demobilised there
were no jobs for them, and the result was
a considerable amount of unemployment
for a long time. Fortunately through the
accumulation of funds in the insurance
scheme, unemployment benefit was available, but it was not satisfactory to find
that the men who returned home, after
doing their bit to protect their hearths
and homes, were unable to find work. I
think the Government deserve to be commended for the action they have taken in
this connection, for, in addition, it
enables the police to keep an eye on the
people who come over the Border and
satisfy themselves as to whether or not
they are following lawful occupations.
The Beveridge Report has also been
mentioned. I think there is hardly a
worker in the whole of the British Isles
who is not expecting a great change
when this scheme comes into operation,
although it may be a long time before we
realise the full benefits of the proposals.
The scheme proposed will relieve men
and women of the nightmare of long
periods of sickness or unemployment.
Children's allowances have been very
wisely included in the Beveridge Report.
I think it is about six years ago since in
this House I recommended a course of
that kind. Of course a prophet has no
honour in his own country, and I did not
expect any, but I advocated a scheme of
children's allowances, to be paid through
the health insurance scheme six or seven
years ago.
I was rather moderate in my views so
far as finance was concerned, because I
recommended somewhere about 3s per
child. Sir William Beveridge, in a very
generous way, recommends 8s. I am
afraid that figure will hardly be realised,
as the Imperial Government seem to have
fixed a figure of 5s as the maximum.
However, 5s will be of great assistance to
a man and his wife who are rearing a
big family. I never regarded it as fair
that a single man should be paid the
same wage as a married man with a wife
and young family to maintain. I can
visualise cases where there are six or
eight of a family, fine healthy children,
who are likely to be of great benefit to
the State in the course of a few years.

I do not know how the parents could be
expected to provide for those children
without some assistance, and I think the
suggestion about children's allowances is
the wisest proposal in the Report. It
certainly will be a great encouragement
to the men to know that when they come
back from the war there will be better
times in store for them.
The men who fought in the last war
were promised a place fit for heroes to
live in, but they came back to a nightmare of want and unemployment. They
were faced with starvation. If parts of
this scheme are put through as quickly as
possible it will give a great deal of encouragement to the men now serving to
carry on, as they will know there will be

Major DOBBS: I should like first of

are nearing completion. That is good
news.
I wish the Gracious Speech had contained an announcement that our telephone service had also completed its extra
work in the laying down of junction
cables, and so on. The essential quality
of the telephone service is speed, otherwise absence of delay. At certain times
of the day for the last nine months or
so it has been very bad, and the delay
caused, even in local calls, has been excessive and aggravating. It is unfair to
the telephone user, and it is equally unfair to the telephone operator, because
however expert he or she may be it does
not allow him or her to give the good
service which we look for and which
could be given.
It is very gratifying to learn that
additional hospital and medical services
are being provided. I am sure this
House would like to know whether this
very substantial increase in hospital
accommodation is purely for the period
of the war or will be for the civil population after the war.

to Northern Ireland the

volunteers.

definite implication to that effect-then
we must be certain that the earnestly expressed hope at the beginning of the
Speech that there will be an increase in
the volunteers from here will take place.
I have great pleasure in supporting the
Motion.

THE SENATE

under their control, but also the

That I would describe as a declaration of

An HON. MEMBER: Hear, hear.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: That is nice-a
"hear, hear" to a declaration of war on

Under these powers you have at present
interned about 500 men and women, 99
per cent. of them Catholics. You refuse
to bring them to trial because you have
no evidence to produce against them; you
merely state you have reason to believe
they should be under restraint. I say
that the internment of these people without trial has been an incitement by the
Government to create a most uneasy feeling amongst the minority in the Six
Counties. Where is the justice or freedom
in keeping 500 interned without a fair
trial? Do the Government realise the
seriousness of the position? Do they ever
consider the families and friends of these
internees who would number, at a low
estimate, at least 50,000 people, a large
proportion of our population who resent
such treatment of their fathers, mothers,
sons, daughters, sisters, brothers, relatives
and friends? Not at all. The Government, to my mind, only hope to keep alive
the old bigotry cry.
women
I certainly agree with our learned
Friend, Mr. Leslie, in what he has stated
with regard to the tribunal. I
think he will recognise that the
tribunal before which these men and
are supposed to go is a onesided tribunal. I say that if we had a
fair-minded Government, they would at
least give representation to one-third of
the population under their care. What
is the position with regard to some of
these internees? I have tried often and
often to get some of them released. I
have been told they were prepared to go
before the tribunal and to give any undertaking or any bail required to be lawabiding citizens, and still they are kept
interned. That state of affairs is not fair.
I have been criticised for taking my
seat and attending this Parliament. I
have always been against abstention from
any public board. I have been elected to
represent the minority. I have been
asked what good have I done by attending
here, and I must confess I have done little
or none simply because the Government
do not want us here. They have never
made us any concessions. They merely
tolerate us. I reply by stating that I have
a right to sit in this assembly. I do so
not of my own free will. By doing so I
give away no principles. I take my stand
against the partition of my country, but
by my presence here I am able to get
publicity to expose only in a small way the
many injustices done to the minority of
more than one-third of the population by
this Government.
I deplore outrages against law and
order. I am anxious to maintain law and
order and to live peacefully. Still, as
long as I can I will do anything I can in a
constitutional manner to do away with
the Border, and I trust I may live to see
the day when partition will be a thing
of the past, when we will be living at
peace with our neighbours and with each
other, irrespective of creed or class, the
day when we will have a Government
of the people for a a free and united
Ireland.
With regard to the improvement of the
educational affairs of our country, I trust
the Government will keep before them the
experience they have gained, and that
they will be fair in the administration of
the Education Acts and will give equal
justice to all the people concerned.
Education, let us hope, will be improved.
We also hope that all religious bodies will
get equal treatment, which has been
denied up to now, and that the children
will get adequate religious instruction,
independent of what religious faith they
belong to.

Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I also wish
to tender my congratulations to the right
hon. the Lord Mayor of Belfast who
moved, and the hon. and gallant Member
who seconded, the Motion. They both
made remarkably good speeches and
covered a good many of the points brought
before us in the Gracious Speech. I am
whole-heartedly in sympathy with what
was stated by the hon. and gallant
Senator, Major Perceval-Maxwell, with
regard to recruiting. Although the figures
given to us look fairly well, they are
not, in my opinion, anything like what
we should have from Northern Ireland.
He has rightly stated that the Forces of
the United Nations have not yet met on
German soil. We have a great struggle
before us, and probably a great struggle
in North Africa, and the wastage of war
must be made up by volunteers. There
is conscription in Great Britain, but
through no fault of our own it was not
extended to this country. The result is
that a good many young men who might
be serving their country usefully are
holding back. I hope they will reconsider their position and feel that they
ought to do their duty to the country to
which they belong.
With regard to the Home Guard, I
have had several letters from responsible
persons in three counties with reference
to the lack of recruits for this essential
service. If it was brought up to full
strength for the defence of the country
this service would relieve a great many
serving men for service overseas. In
addition to fighting for their homes and
their wives and families they would be
relieving men who could be serving elsewhere and fighting and bringing the day
of victory nearer to us. I do hope that
as a result of the speeches that have been
made, and the representations that have.
come through the Government of Northern Ireland, we will have more
volunteers for the Fighting Services, and
more volunteers for the Home Guard.
I am sorry to think that Senator
McLaughlin should feel for a moment
that he was not wanted in this assembly.
We always welcome the Opposition and
look forward to hearing them put forward their views. In my experience in
this House I have never known the hon.
Senator or any of his colleagues who represent the minority to have been shut
out of criticism in any matter. I am
certainly wholeheartedly with the Government in their proposal to strengthen the
Civil Authorities (Special Powers) Act.
We have had a number of outrages committed in Northern Ireland. Policemen
have been shot in cold blood, bombs have
been thrown, a number of people have
been injured, and some of them have lost
their lives. We have had to face claims
for malicious injuries, which in some
counties have become quite a burden.
Now, if this can be relieved by tightening up the powers, I think it should be
done and done as quickly as possible.
There is no use talking about interning
400 men, some of them young. They
are men who are under suspicion. We
have to admit we know why these men
cannot be brought to justice. We know
the difficulties. How are you going to
get the information? They have terrorised
the people so that they will not give information to the police authorities in
order that a prosecution can be brought.
And if there are 400 here in Northern
Ireland across the Border they have 700.
In addition to that there have been men
brought to trial and found guilty and
executed, and not one word of complaint
from the minority of Northern Ireland,
nor any one word of complaint from the
majority in Eire at the executions. Some
of them were not tried for murder, but
tried for something approaching it, not
actual murder, and executed; one as
recently as two months ago. I think all
round that we have dealt remarkably.
leniently in a great many of these cases.
There is no injustice to the minority in
Northern Ireland, in my opinion, whatever, either in education or in their
lives or in their homes in any way. If
men are law-abiding citizens they will
have the protection of the law. But if
they do not afford the necessary information to the police authorities when outrage
or murder takes place they must bear the
consequences themselves.
With regard to the matter of the
powers that were granted under a regula-
