THE SENATE
[Sir Joseph Davison]
tion made by the Home Minister in
England I am very glad to see that
brought into being and I hope these
people who have come from outside our
borders will register and register at once.
We know the difficulties that arose after
the great war when our workmen came
back. A great number of them could not
find employment, for their jobs had been
taken by those coming from the South and
West of Ireland. We know the terrible
happenings that were brought about as a
consequence. We never want to see that
again. I join with Senator McLaughlin
in wishing that we could all in Northern
Ireland live in peace and harmony with
one another. (Hon. Members: Hear, hear.)
There is nothing would please me more.
It has always been my idea as an Ulsterman and an Irishman that we should live
agreeably with one another. But if the
forces of the Crown are not helped to
maintain law and order we will always
have the same conditions as prevail today.
With regard
to what has been
said about the Beveridge Report we
know what attitude has been taken
up by the Imperial House, but we
do not know what the state of affairs will
be after this war. A great many people
seem to think that because we are spending
£14,000,000 a day for the conduct of the
war, money can be found for every scheme
that can be evolved from the minds of
men. We must win the war first and
then we will know how we stand both
financially and economically in regard to
schemes which will be helpful to all
classes and creeds in this country.
Mr. TAYLOR: I should like to asso-.
ciate myself with the previous speakers in
congratulating the mover and seconder of
the Motion on the very able manner in
which they discharged their duty. I was
very pleased to see in the Gracious Speech
the tribute paid to our police force, both
the R.U.C. and the Special Constabulary.
I have been closely connected with the
Special Constabulary from its formation,
and the more I see of its members, the
more I admire them. Officers and men
perform their duties as ordinary citizens,
and after their day's work they sacrifice
their leisure to the duties pertaining to the
maintenance of law and order.
As a
result the majority of the people are able
to rest quietly in their beds without fear
of
disturbance from any disorderly
elements. It speaks well for Northern
Ireland that it is so fortunate as to
have such a large number of citizens
willing to come forward and offer their
services. I feel that the general public
fail to realise the long and arduous hours
which the men put in at drills, in addition
to the long nightly patrols in all weathers.
The number of favourable records they
have received for police duties, their devotion and courage, the high commendations
which they invariably receive from His
Majesty's Judges of Assize, testify to
their work and worth. I can assure this
House that the "B" Special Constabulary
will continue to do their duty without
fear or favour. In dealing with crime.
and criminals they do not consider a man's
creed, but his conduct, and nothing will
ever tempt them to injure a man because
he does not belong to their religious body.
I hope that the dangerous and
troublous times we have gone through will
not return, but whatever the future of
the Special Constabulary may be, the
presence of such a body of men will help
Northern Ireland to overcome any difficulties she may be called upon to face in
the future. They have done their duty
nobly in the past, and I have no hesitation
in believing that they will continue to set
an example of straightforward, honourable and impartial dealings with their
fellowmen, so that only the law-breakers
and evil-doers will have reason to fear
their existence.
Before sitting down I should like to
know if the Leader of the House has seen
the statement made by a Member of this
Parliament, Mr. Beattie, in which he
makes a very strong indictment against
this Government. This is a very serious
matter for Northern Ireland. The statement is published in many leading newspapers across the water, and no time
should be lost in contradicting these vile
and libellous allegations. Surely it is up
to our Government to issue an authoratative reply, and to let the people everywhere know that there is as much freedom,
fair play and justice in Ulster as in any
part of the United Kingdom.

Questions to Minister.
SALE OF CALVES.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Maguire:-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether it is a fact that dropped calves
are being sold at as low a figure as 2s per
head in Londonderry and other centres;
whether farmers are killing off calves
owing to shortage of feeding, and what
steps the Ministry of Agriculture is
taking to ensure that heifer calves of
a good milk strain are being retained
for further milk production.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I have a good deal
of information with regard to the sale of
dropped calves in Northern Ireland. The
farmers in a good many districts are selling off dropped calves for the reason that
they have not sufficient foodstuffs and the
cost of what is available, does not warrant
their raising calves. There is also the
cost of transport to take the calves to the
saleyard, where they get as low as 2s per
head for them. Then there is the loss of
the farmers' time. It is wrong altogether
to ask farmers to waste their time in sending or taking calves to market to sell them
at from 2s to 5s a head. It would not
pay for the transport apart from the loss
of time.
I should like to quote one definite
instance which I am aware of, and which
occurred in Londonderry at a sale mart on
23 MARCH 1943
Questions to Minister
54.
Questions to Minister
farmers from the creameries and been used
for rearing these calves. There being no
separated milk nowadays the farmer has
nothing on which to feed these calves. I
should also like to ask the Leader of the
House if it is not a fact that this is a major
disaster both from the point of view of our
cows in the next few years and also of our
beef trade.
the 10th of this month. Calves were sold
at that mart at 2s, 3s and 5s, the average
being 5s per head. That is a ridiculous
price, and if the calves are allowed to be
slaughtered I do not know where we are
going to get the production of meat in
the future. Some check should be put on
this. The Ministry should take up this
matter in some shape or form in order to
compensate the farmer for rearing calves.
We have stabilised prices for feeding
stock, but my opinion is we should begin
at birth, and there should be some subsidy
at birth in order to encourage the farmers
in production. If we fail in production I
am afraid that is going to tighten up
consumption to a great extent, unless the
Ministry have some view, that I am not
aware of, whereby we are going to be
provided with tinned meat in future instead of fresh produced meat, or meat in
some concentrated form. That is due to
neglect in dealing with this matter before
it got to such a stage.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House): All
stock sold for immediate use as human
food must, of course, be sold only to the
Ministry of Agriculture. Calves bought
for food are purchased at the prices fixed
by the Ministry of Food. The average
price paid for calves purchased by the
Ministry of Agriculture is far in excess of
the figure quoted by the hon. Senator,
while the lowest fixed price for reject
calves also exceeds the figure mentioned.
Purchasing statistics do not indicate that
farmers are killing off excessive numbers
of calves. The Ministry has no power to
insist on farmers keeping calves, but again
purchase figures indicate that heifer calves
are being retained for future milk production.
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: Can
the Government give the prices?
Mr. ROBB: I have not got the prices.
If any Member wishes for further information on this particular subject, I am

CALVES FOR VEAL.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Maguire:-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether it is a fact that prices for
calves for veal are fixed in Great
Britain, and, if so, what steps the
Ministry of Agriculture for Northern
Ireland has taken to adopt similar
prices.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I noticed that since
our last meeting some prosecutions took
place in Belfast, and a great number of
the leading hotels were fined heavily for
using veal purchased from a dealer in the
east end of the city. Veal is a very
inviting food, and surely we should have
some control over its production and selling price. I understand that in Great
Britain they do control the price of
calves and veal. Both the dealer and a
big number of our leading hotels in the
city, as well as a prominent club, were
fined heavily for using it. Why should
something not be done in this respect to
make legitimate the sale of veal? It is
a great substitute and a great saving of
meat.
Mr. ROBB: It is a fact that prices for
calves for veal are fixed in Great Britain,
and, as in the case of all fat stock prices,
these prices apply in Northern Ireland.
Mr. MAGUIRE: Would the Leader of
the House give us any information in
regard to the prosecutions last week?
What were the reasons for the prosecution of the dealer and the hotels?
Mr. ROBB: I would need notice of
that question.

Civil Authorities Bill-
Second Reading
23 MARCH 1943
""
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time. This
Measure makes two changes in the law as
it was enacted by the Civil Authorities
(Special Powers) Act passed by this
Parliament in 1922. By Section 3, subsection (1) of that Act it was enacted
that persons alleged to be guilty of
offences against the Regulations may be
tried by a court of summary jurisdiction
constituted in accordance with this
section, and not otherwise. Under subsection (4) the court of summary jurisdiction was to be constituted of two or
more resident magistrates. This Bill proposes to amend those provisions by altering Section 3 and enacting that a person
alleged to be guilty of an offence against
the Regulations may be tried by a court
of summary jurisdiction constituted in
accordance with the Act or on indictment.
It has been found as a matter of experience that many of the offences which
were alleged to have been committed, and
in fact had been committed, under this
Act were of a very serious nature, and it
was found desirable that where the offence
was very serious it should not be tried by
a
court of summary jurisdiction, but
should be tried on indictment by a judge
and jury. The person to decide whether
any case shall be so tried is the Attorney-
General. If in his opinion, and in the
opinion of the Government, it is desirable
that the more serious offences against the
Regulations under the Act should be
brought before a judge and jury instead of
before a court of summary jurisdiction
constituted in accordance with the Act,
they shall be so tried on indictment.
At the other end of the scale it was
found that many of the offences were very
slight, and that the difficulty and delay
involved in constituting a court of two resident magistrates was an unnecessary waste
of both energy and time. Accordingly, this
Bill proposes that when a court of summary jurisdiction is trying a person
charged with an offence against the
Regulations in respect of which the maximum punishment is imprisonment for a
term not exceeding two years, or a fine
not exceeding one hundred pounds, in
those cases one resident magistrate
sufficient to constitute the court.
is
The Bill goes on to make a further
amendment to Section 4 of the Act of
1922. The Section provided that a person
convicted of an offence against the
Regulations should be liable to a sentence
of imprisonment, with or without hard
labour, of two years, or to a fine of £500.
or both.
It was thought that, in the more
serious cases, it was desirable to have tried
before a judge and jury, the punishment
that might be inflicted would be greater
than that provided for in that particular
Section of the 1922 Act, and accordingly
Section 2 of this Bill provides :-
The following section shall be substituted for section four of the said
Civil Authorities (Special Powers) Act
(which relates to the punishment of
offences): -
(1) A person convicted of an offence
against the regulations shall-
(a) on summary conviction, be liable
to imprisonment for a term not
exceeding two years, or to a fine not
exceeding one hundred pounds, or
to both such imprisonment and
such fine; or
(b) on conviction on indictment, be
liable to be kept in penal servitude
for a term not exceeding fourteen
nor less than three years or to a
fine not exceeding five hundred
pounds, or to both such penal
servitude and such fine:
It may be asked why the lower limit of
penal servitude shall be not less than three
years.
The explanation-which is perhaps a lawyer's explanation-is that in
the year 1891 an Act was passed by which
it was decreed that in respect of any
offence for which penal servitude was an
appropriate punishment by virtue of an
Act passed before that Act, the minimum
of penal servitude should be three years.
That Act, in fact, only applied to offences
against Acts passed before that date, but
it became the invariable rule of the courts
that where a sentence of penal servitude
was going to be imposed the minimum of
three years should apply. That practice
has, in fact, obtained ever since that time,
and it is proposed in this Bill that a
minimum sentence of three years shall be
incorporated.
Those are the two objects of this Bill,
and I move that it be read a Second time.
Question proposed.
Mr. MCALLISTER: This Bill in
another place, and up to the present in
this House, has had a rather significant
and unique experience. So far as I can
ascertain, in another place the Bill was
Civil Authorities Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
introduced by a Junior Minister, the
Parliamentary Secretary of the Ministry
of Home Affairs, and the only other
Member of the Government Party who
spoke on the Bill, or was allowed by the
Party Whip to speak on the Bill, was the
Attorney-General. Up to the present, so
far as this Bill has gone, the Government
have been afraid to entrust discussion of
the measure to the ordinary rank and file
of its Members. For this purpose they
selected three very distinguished lawyers
who, on their merits, have attained very
high place in the legal profession of Northern Ireland. The Parliamentary Secretary, in introducing the Bill, which is a
violent attack upon constitutional law,
contented himself by introducing it in a
few sentences, and with the skill of the
skilled advocate he avoided or ignored all
the difficulties. I think I may say that,
from the point of view of advocacy, the
Leader of this House has proved himself.
the most skilful of those three lawyers.
This debate will naturally follow the
lines followed in another place. I do not
intend to comment further on the speech
of the Parliamentary Secretary of the
Ministry of Home Affairs, but I hope to
deal with some of the statements made by
the Parliamentary Secretary. To find the
fundamental and bedrock reason for the
introduction of this measure, or the
alleged reason for the passage of this
special legislation, one has to examine the
causes which have led up to it. The
whole Bill is a violent contradiction of
well-recognised constitutional law, and
undermines the very foundations of
Magna Charta, which has been sacrosanct
from generation to generation of people
who want to see the liberty of the subject
preserved.
For forty years a constitutional movement was carried on in Ireland by the
Irish Nationalist Party. I always
believed in that movement, and to-day I
maintain the same convictions. After
forty years of
of skilful Parliamentary
effort the Home Rule Bill was put upon
the Statute Book. In passing, I may say
that the reasons given by the Parliamentary Secretary for the introduction of
this measure were the same reasons that
were advanced for the passing of the
Treason Felony Act of 1842. By that
Act any design to overawe or intimidate
Parliament was made an offence. Throughout all those years we had a constitutional
Parliamentary agitation in regard to
Home Rule. The Bill was passed by the
King, Lords, and Commons of England,
according to the Constitution of England.
Then we had the Ulster Covenant, by
which men pledged themselves to ignore
that law, and to resist it by every means
in their power.
Surely there could be no more serious
design to overawe or intimidate Parliament, to use the words of the Treason
Felony Act of 1842. If those responsible
for that Covenant had themselves been
charged and given fourteen years penal
servitude they might have come in here
with their crime purged and introduced
a measure like this with clean hands.
But that is not all. Some of the Members
of this Government were the chief
organisers of the Ulster Volunteers and
the running of arms. According to
General Crozier-the statement has not
been denied he had authority from that
volunteer body to shoot down men wearing the King's uniform. Can that.
treason be surpassed? Is this Act going
to be made retrospective so that Members
responsible for that treason may be made
amenable and be subject to 14 years'
penal servitude? Is not that logic?
Can its justice be refuted?
a
This anarchy became so extreme that
very distinguished and responsible
English statesman accumulated in book
form the speeches and writings of leading
politicians in Ulster, and he issued this
book as The Grammar of Anarchy. Those
are
the people who are responsible
for this Measure. I do not believe there
is any necessity for this very extreme
Measure now. I think I have shown that
in the past in times of far more serious
treason, the British Government did not
deem it desirable to bring in extraordinary legislation such as this.
The reason this legislation is being
brought in to-day is because the Government and the Government party in
Ireland are alone responsible for such
trouble as there is to-day both North and
South. They sowed the seeds; they set
the example; they taught the youth of the
country that rebellion was a paying game,
but the unfortunate thing is that the
youth of our country who were misled are
to get 14 years' imprisonment as a reward
for their efforts, while Members of the
official party are to sit in places of power,
Civil Authorities Bill-
Second Reading
23 MARCH 1943

privilege and emolument as a result of
the treason which I have quoted.
any
Would any neutral State, would
independent country say that that was
just or fair? Is that consonant with the
Atlantic Charter about which we have
heard so much. Will our kith and kin in
America approve of the partial, unjust
and nefarious administration of the law
to which we have been subjected in this
area?
From a legal point of view this Bill is
contrary to every element of criminal and
constitutional law passed up to the present time. The Leader of the House has
very skilfully tried to get round the three
years' minimum sentence, but can he show
me in any Act of Parliament where a
minimum sentence of three years was to
be imposed on conviction for any offence
prior to the establishment of the Northern Ireland Parliament? What is the
reason
for putting in this minimum
sentence of three years? Have the
Government no confidence in the courts;
have they no confidence in the judges who
hear the evidence and see the demeanour
of the prisoner in the court and in the
witness-box? Have the Government no
confidence in a judiciary set up by themselves? Are the judges not in a better
position than the Minister of Home
Affairs to say what is the proper sentence?
Anyone who has any experience of the
law knows that in almost every case there
is a different degree of guilt, and the only
can differentiate between
person who
those cases is the judge who hears the
evidence.
The Leader of the House very skilfully
tried to sidetrack the issue in regard to
this minimum fine and did not directly
face up to it. As a very humble lawyer I
say that neither the Leader of the House
nor any other lawyer can justify its introduction into the criminal code.
We are
told that Hitler has superseded the courts
and the judiciary in Germany, and he has
been severely criticised both by the Press
and the public. I think he has been
rightly criticised if that is so, but here we
have in this Bill a proposal that the
Minister of Home Affairs with some civil
servant in his office shall determine
whether the fine or sentence shall be
reduced or varied.
I should like the Leader of the House,
when he is replying, to deal with that
aspect of the situation. If they have
superseded the law in Germany and it is
wrong, we on these Benches should not be
prepared to accept the Minister of Home
Affairs as a pocket edition of Hitler or a
Nazi in tabloid form. That is what we
are asked to do by a clause of this Bill.
As a justification for trying prisoners
either by summary jurisdiction or on
indictment by judge and jury the Leader
of the House has stated that there are
very slight cases which should be tried by
one magistrate in a court of summary
jurisdiction. I say it is the right of this
Parliament to determine what those cases
shall be. I say it is the right of this
Parliament, and not the right of the
Attorney-General or the Minister of Home
Affairs, to determine what are slight and
what are serious offences. Under that
method, one man of one class may be
treated as if his offence were a minor
matter, while the offence of another man
of another class may be treated as serious.
This is left to the whim of the Attorney-
General for the time being. This matter
should have been set out in a Schedule to
the Bill, and the offences to be charged
before a court of summary jurisdiction
and those to go before a judge and jury
should also have been mentioned.
It may be stated that it is an advantage
to be tried by a judge and a jury. On
principle I agree with that, but, unfortunately, the whole trouble about the
administration of the law is that jurors are
challenged and hand-picked. I have
defended a Catholic prisoner who committed an ordinary offence, and the prosecutor challenged every Catholic name on
the jury list. A Protestant solicitor sitting
beside me sympathised and expressed his
horror that such a thing should take
place. In case what I have stated should
be misinterpreted, I must say that that
did not occur in connection with any of
the present prosecutors. I should not like
unconsciously to pass any reflection upon
any man, or to have my remarks misinterpreted. It is my experience, and the
experience of practitioners, that jurors are
hand-picked. Catholic jurors are challenged without any reason being given,
good, bad or indifferent. If we were sure
of a fair and just trial before a jury which
was not hand-picked I certainly would
place my liberty and freedom with confidence in their hands.
It is generally accepted that this Bill is
not being passed for any particular motive

Here in the Six Counties the sanctity
of the home, and the sacredness of
family rights that are thus upheld by
the common law, are still further
violated by the Special Powers Acts.
The enforcement of these Acts for so
long, and the threat that they will continue in operation may lead us to forget the fundamental freedom to which
the subject is entitled. Accordingly
no excuse is required for quoting at
length these illuminating words that a
famous jurist recently penned: "The
whole frame and balance of the Constitution, and the separation of the
spiritual and the temporal powers
were directed to the institution of every
man in the status of freedom. The
law had an immense respect for every
man. In him was all the dignity that
goes with freedom, and all the responsibility that is proper to human personality. The King's judges presumed,
until the contrary was proved, that
every man was a good man; innocent
of crime and wrong doing; honest in his
dealings, efficient in his work; of good
repute among his neighbours; firm and
constant in danger and emergency; a
worthy fellow living in peace and amity
with his fellow men. When he came
of age every man was entitled by
virtue of his rational nature to own and
administer property; to take thingsthat is to say, not merely for
momentary or temporary use, but to
have and to hold them in stable and
permanent possession. In virtue of his
rational nature, every man might also
enter into a contract by exchanges of
promises, such as marriage, and when
he made such a contract the law
assumed that the spouses were worthy
to beget and bear children, and that
ordinary men and women possessed the
high qualities and the fine craft that
are required to maintain and educate
a family."
How these words can be reconciled with
the present day policy of disintegrating
the family; how they can stand with the
constant raiding of houses, arrests, and
detention of men without charge and
without trial that take place under the
Special Powers Act which now form part
of the regular legal machinery-these are
Can
questions that demand an answer.
it be that the institution of every man
in the status of freedom" and the rights
and responsibilities of the family are now
regarded as anachronisms? If they are
what will be the effect on civil society?
Is this to be the achievement of modern
statesmanship?

Here to my mind is the Atlantic

majorities have been gerrymandered so

deprived of their just rights in education,
obliged to pay rates to build and equip

to build and equip Catholic schools, which

funds. Not ten per cent. of the members
of the Royal Ulster Constabulary are
Catholics. The Special Constabulary is a
sectarian force maintained by the State.
Not one Catholic judge is on the High
Court bench. There is only one Catholic
resident magistrate out of thirteen.
representation is given to Catholics on
most Government official committees, and
not more than five per cent. on official
committees. Catholics are deprived of
their legitimate share of all official positions. Catholic homes are raided by day
and by night, and this is backed up by
this Special Powers legislation.
No
If the Government are honest they will
try to meet the situation without bringing

Mr. LESLIE: The Bill before us this
afternoon is, if I may say so, distinctly
limited in Title. It does not refer to
Civil Authorities Bill-
Second Reading
23 MARCH 1943
either of the two subjects I mentioned in
support of the humble Address, and therefore, I do not propose to say a word about
either of those two subjects. I want to
keep as closely as possible to the Bill
before us. Neither I nor, I imagine, any
constitutionally-minded person can really
like the Bill. I do not think it is possible. We cannot like the suggestion
that for breaking a Regulation, if no
other offence can be charged against the
alleged breaker, penal servitude may be
the punishment for that offence. We do
not like the power which this Bill gives
to the Minister of Home Affairs to alter
the existing law.
This Bill proposes alteration in two
directions. We do not like that. It is
not according to modern constitutional
standards, but I think I have exhausted
the items of dislike and I come to the
more serious question.
If we dislike proposals such as I have
just referred to we do more than dislike;
we detest and we abhor the causes which
have given rise to the proposals now before us.
In fact we recognise that we are
not dealing with normal conditions, and,
therefore, we cannot judge the steps to
be taken entirely by the normal standard. I think that is so. None of us
who remember the last Winter Assizes or
the City Commission, which immediately
preceded it, can possibly describe the conditions at present in Northern Ireland as
normal, law-abiding and satisfactory. As
I say the conditions are not normal and,
therefore, abnormal steps may have to be
taken.
I would ask all critics of this Bill not
only in Northern Ireland, but across the
water to remember that and consider it
with some care, perhaps with more care
than those across the water usually bestow
on matters relating to Northern Ireland. I
should also like them to remember two
other things-that the whole of the special
authorities code is, in theory at all events,
a temporary measure, and you cannot
alter your general law to meet a temporary difficulty. When I say cannot I
mean you cannot in practice, but, of
course, Parliament can if it chooses. That
is one thing to remember. Another thing
is that this Parliament has no power to
deal either with treason or with treasonfelony. Something has been said this
afternoon about treason-felony. The intention, I think, was to refer to the
Treason-Felony Act of 1848 which was
passed, as the House will remember,
primarily in order to mitigate certain inconveniences associated with the trials for
what was then called high treason.
As a
matter of fact, most of the Members of
the House are probably aware that,
speaking generally, there is no, offence
created under the Treason-Felony Act of
1848 which could not be prosecuted under
the older Statute of 1351 as high treason.
At all events we can deal with neither of
those Statutes in this Parliament.
Passing from that to the Bill itself I am
going to say it is not a harsh Bill at
all. It is not. I am further going to
that there is nothing in the Bill
contrary to Magna Charter. There is
nothing in this Bill that provides that a
man shall not be gone upon or sent upon
except by the judgment of his Peers or
by the law of the land. There is really
nothing of that sort in this Bill, which
merely deals with the trial of certain
offences.
I think the Bill has really two admirable features. Certainly as regards the
first part I should prefer that an offence
normally liable to two years' hard labour
should be tried on indictment before a
judge and jury, because I think the
chance of a prisoner is at least as good
before a judge and jury as it is before
two resident magistrates sitting alone, and
also the chances are that the proper
amount of punishment to be awarded, if
it is decided to award punishment at all,
and that is not necessary under this Billof being carefully weighed up are better
before an experienced judge than before
two resident magistrates.
I also welcome the provision which
reduces to six months the maximum
penalty for what are called ordinary,
that is minor, breaches of the
Regulations, and also the new power
which is given to one resident magistrate
to hear that case alone. At present you
have to have two resident magistrates
brought in together, usually at considerable inconvenience, in order to hear a
relatively rotten little case for which they
do not impose more than six months.
That provision will operate to reduce the
strain on a resident magistrate, to some
extent to reduce expenditure, and it will
also operate to the benefit of the defendant, who may very likely already
be in custody awaiting trial, because
he is much more likely to be tried more
quickly if you have to get only one
Civil Authorities Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
resident magistrate than if you have to
get two. That is a good point.
As regards the third point, the alteration proposed to be made in Section 4 of
the Act of 1922, the sentence not exceeding twelve years' penal servitude and
not less than three years, on which something has been said, if that twelve
years' penal servitude is the maximum
sentence the three years is not the
minimum sentence, because the House
knows, or ought to know, that where a
sentence of penal servitude is imposed,
where power is given to impose it-and
it cannot be imposed unless a Statute
gives power to do it-the court has full
discretion to allot, instead of penal
servitude, such period of imprisonment
with or without hard labour up to two
years as the court may see fit. That is
the general law, so that the reference
there to the minimum of three years does
not apply. I am going to speak again
about that three years in a moment.
I do not know whether there is any
precedent under the Defence of the Realm
Acts for a twelve years' sentence, but
it really does not matter. Penologists
usually admit that a sentence of three
years penal servitude is a less severe sentence than two years hard labour. I think
that is generally agreed. The House will
also, remember that in the case of a
sentence of penal servitude very considerable reductions of the sentence have been
obtained by good behaviour while in prison.
The distinction between hard labour and
penal servitude is really a mere matter
subject to the prison rules prevailing at
that time. Any alteration as regards the
existing regulations will have to be made,
as I have understood it, by a new regulation, and that regulation will have to be
laid before Parliament. There is no doubt
about that. That is in the principal Act.
I imagine it will require a new regulation
SO as to divide the regulations-six
months and the other classes. Really
I cannot see anything to prevent anybody
supporting this Bill if they are actuated
by the same desire as I am to do everything they can to stop the attacks being
made, more or less in secrecy, against the
safety of the State.
Passing from all that, when Bacon
wrote his celebrated essay inviting people
to read neither to contradict nor believe,
but to weigh and consider, he might possibly have been thinking of Bills in the
Parliament of that day across the water,
but certainly not of Bills brought from one
Chamber to another of this Parliament.
But the maxim is good, and I think there
are points in this Bill that do require
examination. I shall have three matters
on the amendment paper. None of them
affect the principle of the Bill or probably
the practice either, but I think they are
matters which we ought to debate in order
to make sure what the will of this House
is as regards those matters.
The first of them is the question of
whether sending persons for trial by
indictment should be retrospective or not.
As the Bill is drawn, power to prefer a
charge for trial on indictment is distinctly
retrospective, and I am not sure whether
it is the intention of Parliament that that
should be so, and I think we must find
out. In the principal Act, it will be
remembered, new punishments were
imposed, and in each case it was stated
they should not apply to offences committed before the passing of the Act. That
will be a point for consideration and
debate.
The second point is more amusing. It
is more like a cross-word puzzle. It is
whether those words, "or less than
three" should be included. Precedents
are so conflicting, and this Parliament has
committed itself to one already. I think
I am bound to take the decision of the
House as regards those four words.
Whether we leave them in or take them
out will make no difference to the sense
of the enactment. It may be that the
best method of dealing with the question
lies rather in an amendment of the Penal
Servitude Act of 1891 than in any other
direction.
The third point is the question of
Quarter Sessions. We do not say that
these offences shall not be prosecuted in a
Court of Quarter Sessions. We have not
got a Quarter Sessions Act in this
country. In the old days, when I was
permitted to sit on the bench, had a case
of this sort been brought before me I
should not have sent it to Quarter
Sessions, because I should have considered
myself bound to consider those old circulars from Dublin Castle which, although
they did not directly restrain, did give
one very strong hints. One of those
hints certainly resulted in such cases being
sent to the Assizes. But we do not say
Civil Authorities Bill
Solicitors Bill
23 MARCH 1943
so in this Bill, and I am afraid it is a
matter for consideration whether we ought
or ought not to say what we want about
that. However, I will give the House an
opportunity of considering that matter at
a later stage.
For the rest, I shall support the Bill.
There is an old Latin pentameter-
"Where fear is put among the guilty there
is found safety for the citizens." In that
spirit I support this Bill.

Solicitors Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LESLIE: This small Measure is
designed to afford to people who now are
or may hereafter become desirous of entering the solicitors' profession the opportunity to count towards their period of
apprenticeship any time that they, during
the present emergency, have spent in the
National Service. The term National
Service is defined in the Bill in almost
exactly the same words as used in the
English which is to that extent also
a Scottish Bill. The intention is
Mr. McHugh
Mr. McLaughlin
| Rt. Hon. J. G. Leslie
Rt. Hon. J. H. Robb
Mr. Taylor
Mr. Whaley
that no man from Northern Ireland
who goes forward to serve his country
shall suffer for it as regards his professional career.
He is given consideration
for the time he spent in National Service
as against the requisite period of apprenticeship he has to serve. There is a provision, of course, that he must serve for
two years as an apprentice. Beyond that
the Incorporated Law Society has the discretion to exempt from examinations and
permit a reduction of the term of legal
service, and, of course, the Lord Chief
Justice has the same powers as he has
in other cases.
I venture to commend this Bill, so very
short and so very simple, to the House
and express the hope that the Government
will afford the facilities in another place
for the promotion of the Measure. I may say

Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Acts of

which they desire the concurrence of this

Acts of Parliament (Citation and
Effects) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
This
Mr. ROBB: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time."
is quite a short Bill, and does only two
things. As the matter stands at present,
at the top of each print of an Act of this
Parliament the Act is described as in the
example given in
in the memorandum
accompanying the Bill; that is, 6 and 7
Geo. 6. Chapter 22. Six and 7 George 6
refers to the year of the King's reign in
the Session of which the Act was passed.
It is now proposed that, instead of having
6 and 7 George 6, Chapter 22, we should
have the Anno Domini year, which means
that, in the year to which the example in
the memorandum refers, we would have,
"1942, Chapter 22." This proposed
change is really more for the benefit of
lawyers than for anyone else because it
is only lawyers who have to deal with
these things. It would be far more
simple for a lawyer to be able to look up
an Act by the calendar year rather than
by the year of the King's reign.
I may say that this is a reform that
has been advocated for a considerable
time. I remember myself pressing it
upon our first Prime Minister, but, not
Acts of Parliament Bill—
Second Reading
1 APRIL, 1943
being a lawyer, and being very tightly
bound by precedent, he did not see his
way to change the method adopted at
Westminster. Things have moved since
then, and this new method of
quoting Acts of Parliament has been
develpoed in the Colonies and elsewhere, although it has not been
adopted at Westminster. It is so much
more simple than the former method, and
everybody who has to deal with these
things is in favour of the change.
The object of Clause 2 is this. When
an Act of Parliament is passed, the date
on which the Royal Assent is given is
inserted on the Act, immediately after its
title, by the Clerk of the Parliaments.
There is no statutory authority for that,
but it has always been the practice, and
it is now proposed, in sub-clause (2) of
Clause 2, to give statutory authority to
that practice and to require that it should
be done. It enacts that-
(2) The clerk of the Parliaments
shall insert on every Act of Parliament,
immediately after the title of the Act,
the day, month and year when the Act
has passed and has received the Royal
Assent; and the date so inserted shall
be taken to be part of the Act and to
be the date of its commencement where
no other commencement is therein
provided.
The third sub-clause of Clause 2 also
gives statutory authority to what has, in
fact, been the practice.
It says that:
(3) Where a Bill is introduced into
any session of Parliament for the continuance of any Act limited to expire
in that session, and that Act expires
before the Bill for continuing it has
received the Royal Assent, the continuing Act shall be taken to have
effect from the date of the expiration
of the Act intended to be continued,
as fully and effectually as if the continuing Act had actually passed before
the expiration of the Act intended to
be continued, unless it is otherwise
expressly provided in the continuing
Act;
Therefore when an Act expires before
the Bill for continuing it receives the
Royal Assent, the continuing Act should
be taken to have effect from the date of
the expiration of the Act unless the Bill
otherwise provides. There is a proviso
that this should be without prejudice to
anything that may happen between the
expiration of the old Act and the Royal
Assent to the continuing Bill. It is really
a method for simplifying our procedure,
and for facilitating the work of Parliament. Although the continuing Act
may be given the Royal Assent only after
the Act it is continuing has expired, the
Act will be deemed to have continued
from the date of its expiration. These
the provisions of this short and
simple Bill.
Question proposed.

Mr. LESLIE: It is about time we

gave me some trouble when I first saw the

any difficulty so long as the introduction

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause

clear that the Clause is retrospective, for
Chandra Dharma's ghost still flits across
the scene. I do not suggest that the Clause
as it stands can do us any harm, because
the Attorney-General has, some discretion.
Although you may give the Attorney-
General as much discredit as you like
for the worst course of action you
could not do very much harm because he
could only send the case before a judge
and jury. But to make a penal section
retrospective is distinctly unusual in a
new Act. There are precedents in the
principal Act of 1922, where it was
distinctly and expressly provided that the
new punishments to be inflicted under
that Act should not apply to any offences
committed before the passing of that Act.
There has also to be considered, I
think, what I may call the time faction.
As the Committee knows, offences
punishable under summary jurisdiction
have to be brought before the court
within a certain time, but in the case of
indictable offences there is no limitation.
If we leave the Clause as it is, retrospective in character, we shall be charged,
perhaps, not without some semblance of
justice, with being rather vindictive, and
I think we do not want that. Therefore
I think these words should go in, and I
move accordingly.

in
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move,
Clause 2, paragraph (b), to leave out the
words, nor less than three." When I
spoke the other day on the Second Reading of this Bill I ventured to recall to the
House a saying of Bacon about reading,
considering, and so on. To-day I am
thinking not so much of Bacon as of
Tennyson who spoke of the "myriad
maze of precedent," and it is to the
myriad maze that I am afraid I am going
to direct the attention of the Committee.
to-day. Before I do that may I say once
more that the alteration I propose, if it is
made, does not mean the imposition of a
minimum sentence. It will be made
more clear that it is a minimum sentence
of penal servitude, but there is nothing
in the Bill to prevent a judge exercising
his discretion and imposing a sentence of
up to two years' imprisonment with hard
labour. A judge is not bound to give
penal servitude. That has to be made
plain, particularly because of a misunderstanding that arose in another place.
The difficulty is of an artificial, even
academic, character, and it is a point in
which I shall find considerable difficulty in
interesting the Committee. That difficulty
arises from two causes. First of all, the
Penal Servitude Act of 1891, to which, I
think, the right hon. Gentleman referred
the other day, and which, no doubt hon.
Members have consulted in the meantime,
was not specifically prospective as regards
this matter of penal servitude. I am
going to try to show why it was not prospective in that matter. One has to look
at the causes of these things. You cannot
jump to conclusions, even in the case of
Westminster, which came under the lash
of the right hon. Gentleman few
moments ago. You cannot assume that
at Westminster the draughtsman made a
mistake. That is a thing draughtsmen
never do. Nor can you think that something was overlooked. That is the first
cause of the difficulty.
а.
The second cause is the remarkable
diversity of precedents which has been
followed in the Parliament at Westminster. I think you
have got to back
go
some way beyond the Penal Servitude
Act of 1891 and remember why penal
servitude was invented. It was invented
a substitute for transportation right
away back in the fifties of last century,
and nobody ever heard of a sentence of
three years or less than three years' transportation. The thing could not have
been done. Even in those days the possibility of two years' imprisonment is
there, and the difference in time between
two years' imprisonment and three years'
penal servitude in those bad, old times
would be taken up in getting the unfortunate convict from England to the place
to which he was to be transported. There
was never any transportation for a period
of three years.
As a matter of fact three years was the
minimum of penal servitude away back in
1857. It oscillated between three years
and seven, but the Act of 1891 introduced
three years.
If penal servitude is inflicted at all by a judge it cannot now be
for a less period than three years.
If you
want to inflict penal servitude for a period
of less than three years you would have
to pass an Act of Parliament to enable
the judge to pass a less sentence than
three years.
It is that way, and not the
other way, but I think that is the answer.
As I say, you cannot explain the lack of
prospective in the Act of 1891. The Act
of 1891 agrees with that theory, and it
does nothing to destroy the view that
penal servitude was a substitute for
transportation. I think that is so. As
a matter of fact, there is a precedent in
the Act of 1864 for the form of words
used in 1891, and, as I have indicated, I
think the 1891 Penal Servitude Act was
Civil Authorities Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
passed rather to relieve the judge of
passing a sentence higher than three
years than to ensure he did not pass a
sentence of less than three years. That,
of course, I cannot say, but the Statute
Law Revision Act of 1893 confirmed that
by repealing words repugnant to the
Act of 1891.
What do we get from that? We have
got to look at the precedents that were
set by the Parliament of the United
Kingdom, the precedents set after 1891;
when the minimum sentence of penal
servitude was made three years. I am afraid
I shall have to take the Committee through
some of them. The first of them is the
Larceny Act of 1926, and in that Act the
words that I suggest should not be inserted
here did appear. That was in 1926.
Also I may say that the words "two
years' imprisonment" also occur. Again
in 1908, when the Incest Act, to which
the right hon. Gentleman referred the
other day, was passed the words occur
again, but in that case I am afraid I
have got to say that I think that was a
thoroughly bad Act as regards this matter
because not only do these words about
imprisonment occur again, but also there
is a provision about a felony, to which the
penal servitude sentence would have
applied anyway. One offence under that
Act would have been a felony, and, therefore, I do not think we can get any help
out of that Act.
In 1908, in the same year, the
Companies Act made one Section of three
Sections of the Act of 1867 from which
the words had been taken out in 1893,
and it put them in again. In 1911 the
Official Secrets Act again put in the words.
I have done with the four precedents. I
can find where the Parliament of the
United Kingdom, shortly after 1891, put
in these words. Now let us look at the
other side of the picture. In 1911 the
Perjury Act was passed, which did not
apply in Ireland. These words do not
occur in that Act. In 1913 the Forgery
Act was passed. It is in common use
in Ireland, and these words do not occur
in that Act. It was that Forgery Act
which repealed two-thirds of that Section
of the Companies Act of 1908 of which I
have spoken. Again, in 1914 we got a
Trading with the Enemy Act, and the
words were again put in. I will ask the
Committee to remember that. In 1916
we got a Larceny Act which is still in very
common use in Ireland. That 1916 Act
repealed the Larceny Act of 1926 which
put in the words, and it did not reinstate
the words which I am objecting to this
afternoon.
Again you have the Firearms Act of
1920, which is very amusing in some ways,
because the only Section which allows
penal servitude to be imposed takes you
back to the Explosives Act of 1883, the
provisions of which are rather curious.
In one Section the Act referred to penal
servitude for life or any less period
allowed by law, and two other Sections
referred to penal servitude, and these
words did not occur. In 1920 again
came the Official Secrets Act, to which
the right hon. Gentleman referred the
other day, and that Act attached itself
to the 1911 Act, which switched back
again to the Act of 1889, and the words
that appear there, I say, were simply
taken from that old Act of 1889.
Then finally, in 1939, you get the new
Trading with the Enemy Act. That Act
repealed the Act of 1914, which put the
words in, and it did not reinstate them.
That is the latest precedent I can find.
Therefore, there are only two surviving
Acts applicable to Northern Ireland in
which these words occur, and neither of
them are very reliable guides at the
moment. On the other hand, for excluding
the words you have the precedents of the
Forgery Act, the Larceny Act, and the
Trading with the Enemy Act, 1939.
These Acts are precedents against the insertion of these words. I had thought,
and even hoped, that I could find some
sort of formula showing that perhaps the
Parliament of the United Kingdom had
applied the spirit, if not the letter, of
the well-known Section of the Interpretation Act which deals with Repeals, and
refrained from using the words when they
had already been used in an earlier
Statute in the same code, or applied only
to felonies, but I could not establish that.
I was left with nothing except the latest
of the United Kingdom Acts as our best
guide.
The question now arises whether we
have not got any precedents of our own.
We cannot get much help from the
United Kingdom Parliament. What
about ourselves? We have one precedent,
and only one, the precedent we set in the
Companies Act of 1932. Our Companies
Act of 1932, the Committee will remember, was a Consolidating Act, and the
Civil Authorities Bill-
Committee
1 APRIL, 1943
Committee which sat on that, in dealing
with the earlier Statute, the Companies
Act of 1908 which I have just referred
to, with the Forgery Act of 1913 in their
minds, left out these words as regards
the one little bit of the Forgery Act that
was left. That was the precedent we
followed. We followed the precedent
of the Companies Act of 1867, as
amended in 1893, and that is the
precedent I invite this Committee to
follow. We are almost driven to it,
because it is the only precedent we set,
and it was set on the advice of one of our
own Committees, and approved of by both
Houses of Parliament. If we depart
from that precedent we shall be leaving
that Larceny Act of 1916, and the
Forgery Act of 1913 rather in the air,
because we shall have left it without any
indication of what the minimum term of
penal servitude is.
But I am satisfied that if you are going
to do it at all it is now really a matter of
law and has got past the sphere of convention that the term of penal servitude
cannot be for less than three years.
Accordingly, for the sake of precedent,
and for the sake of keeping the Act of
1916 and the Forgery Act in line, we
ought not to insert these words, but
whether we insert them or take them out
will not make the smallest difference to
the practice under the Bill. However, I
think the words ought to go, and I beg
to move accordingly.
The MAYOR OF DERRY (Mr.
Simmons): The speech of the right hon.
and learned Senator would probably be
most enlightening to a bench of solicitors,
but to the ordinary business man I am
afraid it has only left confusion worse

Mr. ROBB: The right hon. and learned
Gentleman, who moved this Amendment,
says it does not make any difference
whether the words are in or not. There
is no doubt whatever that the precedents
are not uniform. The way the matter
stands is this. In 1891 an Act of Parliament was passed, and that Act said that
in respect of any Act that had been passed
before that date in which penal servitude
was a punishment, henceforward the
minimum sentence of penal servitude
should be three years. That did not
apply to future Acts of Parliament, but
although it did not apply to future Acts
of Parliament the judges adopted the
practice, which is a universal practice,
that if they are going to impose a
sentence of penal servitude, that sentence
shall not be less than three years. Sometimes, when Parliament came to pass an
Act which included penal servitude as a
punishment it said that the sentence
should be not less than three years.
We propose in this Bill to give legislative effect to that practice; that is, to
put in the Act of Parliament what we,
in fact, know the judges will do. I cannot see any harm in that; in fact I see an
advantage in it. If the judges are not
going to impose a less sentence than three
years why should we not say so in the
Act itself. I would also like to make it
quite clear that this does not affect the
judge's right to impose imprisonment for
a less period than three years. By subsection (2) of Section 1 of the Act of 1891
which introduced this minimum sentence
of three years it provided that two years'
imprisonment may be awarded with
regard to future Acts. The provisions of
the Act of 1891 touching penal servitude
applied only to Acts passed before that
date, but sub-section (2) of Section 1 said
that in future Acts, which include Acts
of this Parliament, instead of penal servitude the judges may impose imprisonment for two years.
All the Bill has done is to put in an
Act of Parliament what we know is the,
practice of the courts, and will be the
practice in the future. It seems to be
far more simple to put it into an Act of
Parliament than to rest upon an existing
practice of judges. If it is in the Act.
there is no question of practice or custom.
The only reason the right hon. and
learned Gentleman has adduced for his
amendment is that sometimes we did and
sometimes we did not do it in the past.
Mr. LESLIE: No, not us- -Westminster.
Mr. ROBB: If we made mistakes in
the past and I think this was a mistake
-it is far better to start right now, and
from now onwards say not less than
three years.
If that is what is meant,
why we should not say so I do not know.

I have spoken several times against this
very obnoxious system of laying down in
Acts of Parliament a minimum fine and
other penalties. I think it is entirely
wrong. When we pass an Act of Parliament directing a certain term of imprisonment we are pre-judging cases that will
come before the courts. We all know
that in regard to various forms of offences
there must be different degrees of guilt.
One man may be charged with a certain
offence, and another man may be charged
with a similar offence which is of a more
grave character than that of the first man.
The first man may have in his favour
circumstances condoning his offence; yet
we are sentencing both men to the same
term of punishment, in the case of penal
servitude.
Mr. ROBB: This does not take away

Leader of the House says these words
merely clarify the existing position, because even without these words a judge
would not give a sentence of penal servitude of less than three years for serious
offences. It seems to be that there is
a question involved regarding the difference between imprisonment with hard
labour and penal servitude. At the present moment the reformative character of
penal servitude makes it almost impossible to carry out unless it is given for a
period of three years or more. For
offences where a lesser period of imprisonment is imposed another form of reformative treatment is employed, I am told,
although, of course I have had no experience of it.
It seems to me that the time might
come when, with the introduction of
various prison reforms, amendments might
take place which would make it quite
feasible and reasonable to give penal
servitude for a less period than three
years. Those provisions have not been

think we ought to provide that quarter

Ι
Mr. ROBB: The Government do not
wish to accept this amendment, and for
this reason. Sometimes a trial can be
had much more expeditiously by sending
it to quarter sessions. Supposing you had
an offence committed in August and had
to send it to the assizes. That would
probably by the Winter Assizes in
December, but you would have quarter
sessions in September. There might be
an odd case where it would be a help to
get the case tried quickly. Accordingly
we prefer that these words should not be
inserted, because they would prevent a
trial being had at quarter sessions, which
might well be within four or five weeks,
whereas the case might have to wait three
or four months for the next Assizes.
That is the only reason why the Government cannot accept the amendment.
reason
Mr. MCALLISTER: I entirely approve
of what the Leader of the House has said.
At the same time, I would like to put
before the Committee another
against the amendment, and it is this.
You might have a man charged with an
offence who could not afford to have himself defended at the assizes, but who
might be able to afford proper legal aid
at quarter sessions. If this amendment
was passed it would deprive such a man
of the chance of being properly defended,
and I think that is a very material
objection to the amendment. As the
Leader of the House has pointed out,
under the Bill as it stands, if quarter
sessions intervene an innocent person on
remand will not be kept an unnecessary
time in prison.
Mr. LESLIE: In view of what the
right hon. Gentleman has said, I do not
desire to press the amendment.

part of the Bill."

(2) The clerk of the Parliaments shall

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
2, sub-clause (2), to leave out the words,
"passed and has. I mentioned this little
matter on the Second Reading. I think
it will be agreed that it is better to follow
the later precedent, that set by the Parliament of Ireland in 1795, rather than
the precedent set by the Parliament of
Great Britain two years earlier, in 1793.
The words used in the Act of 1793, which
are reproduced here, are perhaps not
quite accurate, and the words in the other
Act are more definite.
Mr. ROBB (Leader of the House):
The Government accepts the amendment.
Mr. MCALLISTER: There is one point
on which I would like the observations of
the Leader of the House. As I understand it, the object of this Clause is to
prevent loss of time and labour by having
to read through the long Act of Parliament to ascertain the date on which the
Act comes into operation. By this subclause it is provided that the date when
the Act received the Royal Assent shall be
inserted immediately after the title, and
that that shall be the date when the Act
comes into operation, "where no other commencement is therein provided." What
I want to put to the Leader of the House
is this. Supposing an Act of Parliament
complies with that requirement and shows.
the date when the Royal Assent was given,
so far as I can see no one will take the
risk of assuming that that is the date on
which the Act will come into operation.
One will still be compelled to read through
the Bill to find out if any other commencement is provided for. If I am right in
that interpretation of this sub-clause, how
is it going to save time and labour? The
point has only occurred to me now.
Mr. ROBB: It does not save time and
labour. It merely gives legislative effect
to what has hitherto been the practice.
When a Bill receives the Royal Assent
the clerk of Parliaments does insert the
date on which the Bill receives the Royal
Assent, but there is no statutory authority
to say he shall do so. If a Bill does provide for some other date of commencement
one will have to read it through to find
that out.

YOUTH WELFARE RECOMMEN-
DATIONS.

move, "That this House do now adjourn
until such date as Mr. Speaker do
appoint."
Mr. STEVENSON: I should like to
ask the Leader of the House whether the
Government has considered the recommendations made by the Committee on
youth problems, which he himself
appointed, and whether any arrangements
have been made to carry out any of the
proposals.
Mr. ROBB: The Government has had
the recommendations under consideration and recognises the value of the Committee's Report. A start has been made
in carrying out some of the recommendations, but the main proposal the
Committee made was for the setting up of
a Government Youth Welfare Committee
with not only advisory but certain
executive powers, and on looking into this
proposal the Government was advised
that legislation is necessary if it is to be
carried out.
Legislation is being prepared and will
be laid before Parliament as soon as possible.

Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. Speaker]
Rev. the Right Honourable Robert
Corkey took and subscribed the Oath.
QUESTIONS TO MINISTER.
Control of Feeble-Minded Children.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Joseph
Cunningham:
To ask the Leader of the House
Whether he can state what arrangements, if any, are proposed for the
segregation and control of feebleminded children who are not of the
educable type; whether there is any
prospect of the setting up of a Board
of Control similar to that functioning
in England and Wales, whereby the
future supervision of such persons could
be exercised; whether the Government
has made any arrangements whereby
the number of such persons resident in
Northern Ireland could be determined;
and whether any arrangements have
been made for the following-up and
after-care of feeble-minded children
who have passed through institutions.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: It
appears that I am the first Member to seek
the assistance of the new Leader of the
House, and I should like to say how
pleased I am to see him amongst us.
am sure he will find that this House is a
very pleasant place to labour in, and I
hope he will find that its members are
very kindly and well disposed. This
House has hitherto been representative of
professional men, agriculture, and industry, and now we have a distinguished
member of the Church. I hope we shall
all feel much better for his presence
amongst us.
I
The question I have on the Paper
really embodies four questions. The first
is to ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state what arrangements,
if any, are proposed for the segregation
and control of feeble-minded children who
are not of the educable type. There has
been much done recently for the health
of children, and there has also been much
done to improve the conduct of the
children and youths in our Province, but
the particular class of children to which I
refer in the question seems to have been
completely neglected. It is very hard, I
understand, to ascertain the actual number of these children. There are three
different classes-what are known as the
feeble-minded, the imbecile, and the
idiot. The only figures that can be
obtained are taken from an inquiry that
was held prior to the war affecting such
children in England and Wales. They
discovered that in the urban districts, of
37,743 children between 7 and 14 years of
age on the school register there were
17.14 per cent. feeble-minded; 3.02 per
cent. imbeciles; and .074 per cent.
idiots. In the rural districts they
discovered that out of 28,637 children on
the register, 33.56 were feeble-minded;
4.82 were imbeciles; and 1.32 were idiots.
A
If the position in Northern Ireland is
anything like that it is certainly necessary
that something should be done, because I
understand that if these children are
allowed to grow up and mix together they
become a danger to the community.
Even before they arrive at that age, I am
told they are a very serious trouble to the
poor mother who has charge of them.
doctor to whom I was recently speaking
told me that he came across one case in
Belfast where the mother, a widow, had
to chain the boy in the yard when she
went out, because no one else could take
charge of him. It is a pity, if there are
many children like this, that no provision.
is made for them. When we are doing so
much to improve the health and conduct
of normal boys and girls, it is a great
pity to neglect this small group, because
they may become a source of danger to
the rest of the community. I do hope the
Leader of the House will be able to give
us some information as to what is proposed to be done for this non-educable
type of child.
The next part of the question is,
whether there is any prospect of the
setting up of a Board of Control similar
to that functioning in England and
Wales, whereby the future supervision of
such persons could be exercised. I understand that in England there is a Board.
of Control, and that all these children
come under its direction and care. If
they are not fit to be allowed to enter
the community the Board takes care of
them for the remainder of their lives.
do not think it would be a very costly
thing to set up such a body as that in
Northern Ireland. I always think the
people of Northern Ireland are of very
I
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
8 JUNE 1943
good class, and I feel that too much
cannot be done for them. Therefore, I
think something in the nature of a Board
of Control should be established here to
supervise and care for this class of
children.
The third part of the question is to ask
whether the Government has made any
arrangements whereby the number of such
persons resident in Northern Ireland
could be determined. I do not think
anything has been done in that respect.
Perhaps the Government will inform us
whether they have taken any steps to
ascertain the number of these poor creatures living in urban and rural districts
in Northern Ireland.
The last part of my question asks
whether any arrangements have been
made for the following-up and after-care
of feeble-minded children who have
passed through institutions.
It appears
that the class of children I have referred
to, the non-educable type, pass into the
world without any after-care, and they
may become just as troublesome as the
other types if no arrangements are made
to see that they conduct themselves in a
respectable manner. I hope the Leader
of the House will be able to give us some
information which will show that the
Government have this matter under consideration and intend to do something
about it.
Mr. BARCLAY: I wish to support the
remarks of the hon. Senator who put
down this question. Some years ago
I
raised the same question, and I was afterwards given to understand that something
had been done; that some large building
had been taken, or was going to be taken,
and converted into a home for mentally
deficient children. Whether or not that
has been done I cannot say. I know of a
number of children who were mentally
deficient, although not too bad to be made
useful citizens if there had been some
kind of institution or home where they
could have been trained and educated to
some trade. I have visited Purdysburn,
where I saw four or five boys. I spoke
to the attendant about those boys,
and he told me they were not too
bad. He said they were mentally deficient to a small extent, but not to
such an extent that they could not be
made into useful citizens. However, the
Purdysburn authorities sent them back to
the Belfast Union, but they kept one
particular boy. The head attendant told
me this boy was very intelligent to a
certain degree, and if he had been under
proper supervision he could have learned
a trade and become a useful employee.
I suppose the poor fellow is still there.
I hope the Government will see their way
to do something in this matter. We look
after the eyesight, hearing, and feeding
of other children, but this particular
class is neglected, and I think it is time
something was done for them.
Mr. BROWNE: On another phase of
this subject, I should like to ask the
Minister if he can tell us why the Haypark School was closed; if it is a fact that
the boarders at that school, those who
came up from the country, have now no
educational facilities; and whether he will
urge the Government to see that proper
educational facilities are provided for all
mentally deficient children, especially
those who are capable of being educated.
While I am on my feet I should like
to join Senator Cunningham in extending a very hearty welcome to Rev.
Professor Corkey on his first appearance
as Leader of the House. We all know
the keen and active interest he takes in
educational, social, and moral questions,
and I have no doubt he will prove a very
suitable successor to Mr. Robb. At the
same time, we all regret the departure of
Mr. Robb, who made an ideal Minister,
and I am sure every Member will join in
wishing him many happy years on the
Bench as a County Court Judge.
Rev. Professor CORKEY (Leader of
the House): I would first of all like to
thank very cordially Senator Cunningham.
and the other Members who have welcomed me on coming to this House for the
first time. I would, however, suggest
that they should not entertain too many
expectations regarding matters which
have at various times become associated
with my name. I shall endeavour to
conform to the customs of this House and
discharge the duties assigned to me to the
best of my ability, and in conformity
with the usages of the House.
In reply to the question I would
*like to say that the matters referred to appear to be within the

Internees in Londonderry Prison.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if the internees in
Derry Prison on

Saturday, 20th March,
1943, were searched and ordered to
strip to the skin, everything in their
pockets taken and thrown outside cell
doors, all collected later in a sheet without name or identification; whether on
the same date when internees were at
breakfast they were ordered to their
cells, locked up until 4 o'clock p.m.,
getting nothing to eat until 4-30 p.m.
that day; and whether the food left
when the men were ordered to their
cells and left uneaten at breakfast was
taken by the officials and buried in the
prison yard.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I should like,
on behalf of myself and my colleagues, to
join in the very hearty welcome given to
the new Leader of the House. I am sure
that under his leadership the business of
the House will be carried on as smoothly
as it was under the leadership of Mr.
Robb. We all regret Mr. Robb's departure. He is an Armagh man, and I am
very glad he is now a county court judge.
The question I have on the Paper
speaks for itself. I will simply quote
what the internees themselves say with
regard to this incident. Here is what
they say:
There is an incident I would like
brought to your notice which occurred
here on the day of the escape from here
on 20-3-42. We were at our breakfast
that morning when they found out
about the
escape. We were all put to
our cells and locked up and were not
let out until 4 o'clock. Most of us got
nothing to eat that morning or all that
day until 4-30 p.m. In the meantime
all the food for breakfast that morning
porridge, milk, bread and a kind of
imitation sausage we get on a Saturday
morning was taken away and buried in
the yard. In bread alone I would say
there was about three dozen loaves
destroyed. As a rule we keep some of
our half-pint of milk over for our
dinner; as we have only one mug we
always keep the milk in a jam jar. They
spilt the milk and broke the jars, not
leaving even one. I have seen where
people were prosecuted for destroying
food accidentally, so I don't think i
be
right that these people should
allowed to waste the ratepayers' money,
not to speak of food, in this way.
On the same day at 2 o'clock the
cells and men were searched. We had
to strip to the skin, and everything was
taken out of our pockets and the cells
-that is personal property-soap,
razors, pocket knives, scissors, tooth
paste, brushes, etcetera. My reason for
telling you this is that 25 per cent. of
this property is always lost, although
they just throw it outside the cell door
and all is collected later on in a sheet
without name or identification of any
sort put on it. The prison staff take
no responsibility for it.
Some time ago in Crumlin Road
prison there was a small billiard table
with badly torn cloth, etcetera. As no
one would play on it, it was taken away.
With the exception of two old dart
boards, two sets chess, three draught
boards, we have nothing else. We are
kept out of our cells from 8-30 a.m.
to 8-30 p.m. There are two tin huts,
60 feet by 25 feet, for 200 men, and a
yard to exercise in which it is a danger
to walk in as the building is falling
down. One of the wall ties, a large
metal plate, broke in two and fell just
as two men were passing under it.
These are the conditions, I am given to
understand, in Crumlin Road Prison,
Belfast, and Derry Prison. Such is the
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister

inhuman and unjust treatment of these
unfortunate men, interned for no offence,
but principally on account of the fact
that they are Irishmen. As long as they
are ill-treated in this manner I will protest and make it as public as I possibly
can.

Mr. M'LAUGHLIN: Some time ago
I raised this question with regard to the
granting of licences for the export of
goods to Eire, and I suggested it would
be a useful thing for the merchants and
manufacturers of the Six Counties to
have an office established in Belfast to
which they could apply for these export
licences. I know of one case where a
number of export licences were applied
for three weeks ago, and it was only this
morning that the firm got a certain number of the licences. They have not yet
got the full number they applied for, and
I do suggest it is unfair to the merchants
and manufacturers of the Six Counties
that when they have to get an order to
export goods to Eire they are obliged to
send to London and wait three weeks for
a permit.
It means that competition is further
increased by the very fact that an
Englishman can get his permit in a day
or two, whereas here merchants have to
wait 21 days. Speaking as a business
man, and from the point of view of the
prosperity of the Province, I suggest that
something should be done about it. The
Minister should ask the Board of Trade
for an explanation of the delay of 21 days
and request them either to establish an
office in Belfast or expedite the granting
of licences, so that a person applying for
an export licence to Eire should not have
to wait 21 days.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: Applications for export licences are dealt with

controlled goods the Export Licensing
Department must frequently refer appli-

First of all I would like to be associated
with other hon. Members who have congratulated the new Leader of the House.
I have served on various Committees with
the right hon. Gentleman and there is no
other person with whom I have served
whom I would prefer to see in this position.

give to the House will be well thought

the departure of the former Leader of the

regard for the Members, and we can congratulate him

on
I would ask the leave of the House to
speak on the three questions together. I
feel that I owe an apology to the Leader
of the House and other Members for raising these matters so early in the life of
the Government. I would willingly have
avoided doing so because the present
Government might have wished for time
to settle down before they are asked questions on general administration. I think
the House may very likely expect an explanation, not only of the questions, but
as to why I chose the present moment to
ask them. The reason is because of the
events which have led up to the new
Government coming into office. There
has been a statement by the Prime
Minister in another place on the policy
which the new Government intends to
adopt. I think it is quite fair to say
that as far as that statement is concerned
we in this House can take no cognisance
of it. The Government have not seen fit
to make any statement on their policy
in this House, and the only way of
getting the information we require to
enable us to form a view as to how far we
can support the present Government, is
by asking a series of questions.
But
The first question I have put on the
Paper relates to agriculture, and is purely
a matter of administration. Quite
rightly farmers have been compelled to
plough very large acreages of land.
it is obvious that the ploughing up of this
land will be of no value to the country
or the war effort unless steps are taken
to provide the necessary labour to harvest
it. The late Government made a statement some time ago that labour would
be provided. I wish to know whether
the policy of the late Government is to be
maintained, and what steps are being
taken to provide the necessary labour.
There are many operations of agriculture.
that can be done by unskilled labour, but
there are many more which do require
a considerable amount of skill, and unless something in the nature of the Land

benefit accrues to any class of person,

bear, and that as each right is created
certain obligations and certain duties are
created with that right. There is a tendency in this country, as in many parts
of Great Britain, to consider that if you
get a right or a new benefit granted no
corresponding obligation arises, and that
is a thing which I think cannot be realised without training.

exactly where they stand within the next
few weeks. It is most important not
only to the farmers, but to ourselves that
the crop which has been sown should be
properly reaped and saved.
I know some excellent farmers who
never until last year, or perhaps for a
generation in the family, lost a single
acre of oats. Last year, through the inclement weather, and particularly
through the want of labour, for the first
time in their lives, or in the lives of their
fathers and grandfathers they lost a substantial quantity of their crop. I knew
several instances of that happening.
I notice that the hon. and gallant
Member has very wisely framed this question. He asked what steps the Government are taking to enable the farmers to

[Rev. Professor Corkey]
THE SENATE
out, however, that in our technical schools
we have already, within certain limits, a
very efficient system of continuation
education; and the curriculum for these
classes is by no means confined to purely
technical subjects. There are also other
forms of continuation education for adolescents such as is provided in connection
