be practicable. Personally I do not see.
why it should not be practicable to adopt
the British standards of education so
far as both these older and younger
people are concerned. I may say that
the matter has been ventilated, led
very ably by a man who gave us distinguished service in Belfast for a number
of years-Sir Richard Livingstone,
former Vice-Chancellor of Queen's
University. His books on "Education for
the Modern World" and "For a World
Adrift," have recently been issued from
the Press. They deal with this subject,
and are well worth perusal.

Business of the House.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I beg to
move, "That Standing Order No. XXV.
be suspended for this day's sitting for the
purpose of taking the Second Reading of
the Workmen's Compensation Bill, when
it is brought from the House of Commons." This Bill is not retrospective,
and as certain people are likely to benefit
from the measure as soon as it becomes
law, it is desirable that it should become
law as early as convenient.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Workmen's Compensation Bill, to which they
desire the concurrence of this Bill.
Workmen's Compensation Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Rev. Professor Corkey.)
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time." This is an agreed Bill in
the sense that it has been accepted by
the trade unions, the labour leaders, the
employers and the employees in England.
It deals with special difficulties that have
arisen in connection with industrial accidents. The matters dealt with in this
Bill are covered to some extent by the proposals in the Beveridge Report, and the
whole subject will likely be reviewed in
new legislation at an early date. In the
meanwhile, however, with the consent of
the interests particularly concerned, this
Bill is designed to deal with a few immediate problems without prejudice to anything in the Beveridge Report.
The first of these arises in the case of a
man whose death was due to an industrial
127 Workmen's Compensation Bill--

8 JUNE 1943
Second Reading
accident and whose widow or dependent
housekeeper was employed on war work
before he died. At present the degree of
her dependency and, consequently, the
amount of the compensation she gets is
related to her earnings.
Clause 1 of the Bill proposes that the
tribunal shall disregard these earnings,
either altogether or to such an extent as
it deems right, if it is satisfied that the
work would not have been taken up but
for the war. If the woman had not been
in employment at all before she accepted
war work, the tribunal would probably
disregard all her earnings.
This Clause will not apply to deaths
which take place before the Bill becomes
an Act, and that is my chief reason for
being anxious that it should be passed as
soon as possible. It would be very
difficult to make a provision of this kind
retrospective, as it would interfere with
lump-sum payments already made, and,
in many cases, decided by courts; but, I
am sure, none of us wishes to have any
cases of further avoidable hardship.
arising.
I believe I can tell the House the intention of Clause 2 fairly clearly, but I
am not a lawyer, and I hope I will not
be called upon to explain the exact force
of every phrase that the legal gentlemen
found necessary. Its object is to allow
compensation to be adjusted whenever
changes occur in the rate of wages of the
class to which the injured workman belonged.
Under the existing law, compensation
depends on the worker's average weekly
earnings for 12 months before the
accident, or, if he was not employed all
that time, then for the time when he was
employed. This is called the relevant
period. He can at present get his compensation re-assessed if a change occurs
in the rate of wages, but only on two
conditions:-(a) That the change exceeds
20 per cent. of the former rate; and (2)
that the change is continued for at least
12 months.
Clause 2 removes both these conditions.
It provides for re-assessment of compensation whenever the rates change, and
the workman's average earnings during
the relevant period are deemed to be what
he would have earned if the new rates had
been in force throughout that period.
Sub-section (1) secures this if the change
of rates occurs after the date of the
accident.
Sub-section (2), has the same effect
where the change has occurred before the
date of the accident. Sub-section (3)
deals with the case of a juvenile. Under
the existing law an injured juvenile can,
up to the age of 21 claim to have his
compensation re-assessed when he reaches
a time when his wages would normally
have been at a higher scale, as, for
example, in the case of boys passing out
of apprenticeship. This sub-section gives
him a further right of review on the basis
of changes in the rates of remuneration
since the weekly payments was increased.
I should make it clear that all these reassessments may operate in the other
direction if wages come down. But the
Trade Union Congress was quite aware
of this, and, nevertheless, welcomed the
Bill in Great Britain.
There are only two remaining parts of
this Measure to which I think I need
draw the attention of the House. Clause
3 provides that a workman who requires
a medical certificate in order to get compensation for a scheduled industrial
disease can obtain it from the examining
surgeon of the district where he is living,
or to which he has gone for medical treatment. At present such a certificate can
be accepted only from the examining
surgeon of the district where the man has
been employed. It can easily be seen
how, in present circumstances, when
workmen are often employed long distances from their homes, and naturally
return there when they are ill, it may
cause hardship to insist on their getting
certificates from the surgeons of districts
where they were working.
It
Clause 4 also deals with a definite war
problem. When a ship is lost at sea,
presumably through enemy action, the
Ministry of Pensions give weekly payments to the dependants of seamen who
have lost their lives in consequence.
may subsequently turn out that the loss
of the ship was not due to enemy action,
but to one of the ordinarv hazards of the
sea, and, consequently the claim is for
workmen's compensation and not for
pension.
This clause proposes that in such cases
the advances made by the Ministry of
Pensions can be recovered either from the
employer liable to pay compensation, or,
C
129 Workmen's Compensation Bill-
THE SENATE

Second Reading
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
where the employer has paid the money
into Court, out of the money so paid.
I should mention that four Sections of
the British Act are not reproduced in this
Measure because they deal only with
disease arising from dust conditions in a
particular branch of the coal mining industry. I am advised that even in Great
Britain it appears only in certain areas,
and has never been diagnosed here, so
that there does not appear to be any need
for legislation on the matter.
There is another Clause in the British
Act which deals with Court procedure,
and here also I am advised-though on
this matter by my legal and not my
medical counsellors-that the question
does not arise here, and the Clause is,
consequently, unnecessary. There has
been such general agreement in favour of
this Bill that I do not think I need add
anything more in support of it.
I beg
to move.
Question proposed: "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: I am sure the House
has listened with much interest to what
I suppose may be called the maiden speech
in this assembly of our new Member. I
think they will have appreciated that the
Bill was carefully gone through and the
points well and truly made. The Bill
itself, as we have been told, is very much
modelled on the Measure which received
the Royal Assent across the water on 4th
February. Some of the United Kingdom
Bill has been left out for reasons just
given, and it is certainly the case that, as
regards these dust diseases, there was no
necessity to reproduce them in this
country. The Bill is interesting in more
than one way, and the only observations I
propose to address to the House refer to
Clause 1, relating to the new provision for
the widow and housekeeper.
For the moment, leaving Clause 1, I
want to say a word or two on two other
Clauses. Clause 2 is the Clause to which
the right hon. Gentleman referred as
being one which may be rather troublesome from the legal point of view, but I
do not myself think there will be any
serious difficulty in the interpretation of
the Clause. At the same time I should
not like to conceal from the House my
view that it is very possible that difficulties may arise in the application of the
Clause. It is all very well to get compensation for this or that under any set of
circumstances, but it must be remembered
that somebody has got to pay that compensation, and it does seem to me that if
you leave the amount of compensation in
a more or less fluctuating condition,
which is the effect of this Clause, the
question may arise-owing perhaps to the
chances of life-as to whether there is
anybody left to pay the Bill.
It is no use allowing a man to have his
compensation payable by an employer who
may have passed away, and whose estate
may have been dissipated. In many cases
the employer has protected himself
through the medium of an insurance company, but that does not affect the issue
because the direct contract is between the
employer and the employee. Whether the
responsibility of the employer has been
passed on to the insurance company is
another question. It is not impossible
that a serious question may arise on this
Clause, but it has received approval in
another place. I do not know to what
extent it was discussed, and it got through
across the water after, as we have been
told again this afternoon, consultation
with various bodies representative of both
employer and employee, so that I do not
think this House would like to put any
obstacle in the way of the acceptance of
Clause 2, even though we cannot conceal
from ourselves its dangers.
Clause 3 seems to be a most attractive
little Clause. It provides for the shifting
of the medical examination to where the
unfortunate sufferer of the accident
happens to be at the time. I believe that
Clause will be found to be of very great
use, rather more than one would think on
first sight, and possibly more so in Northern Ireland than in other parts of the
United Kingdom. In Northern Ireland
you move from one place to another perhaps relatively rather more than they do
in England. I do not say they move
their residence permanently, but they do
sometimes move from place to place in
search of employment. I observe that
some hon. Members are doubting the
possibility of anyone in Northern Ireland
looking for work anywhere, but I do not
think that is altogether a true picture of
the case. I think Clause 3 will be of very
great assistance.
Coming to Clause 3, the House cannot
but perceive, I think, how very much is

?
going to be left to the tribunal which is
to pronounce on the various questions that
arise. It will apparently have to decide
whether a man can have two widows.
There is nothing in the Clause to prevent
him having two widows. He can have a
natural widow, and a woman who is doing
housekeeper for him who, under certain.
circumstances, may pass as a widow.
Whether that will happen will be a
matter for the tribunal, but as the Bill
stands I can see nothing to stop the
benefit being shared in and being enjoyed
by two women. That, no doubt, will be
sifted at a later date, but, in any case, I
presume that there will be the same power
of appeal under this Bill as there is under
the existing main Act of 1927.
There is no reason to doubt that, I
think, and therefore I do not imagine the
House will be disposed to worry very
much about the possibilities that may
arise. The Clause follows that of the
English measure very closely; indeed, in
one case, I think, it follows it too closely,
because, I think, in one paragraph, by
following the letter of the United
Kingdom Statute, we are rather neglecting the spirit, but that is a simple
matter I shall ask the House to correct in
Committee. I do not think that will
cause us any great difficulty.
Incidentally, if we do make the correction I have in mind, we shall get rid of
a passage which seems to be most unclassical English. I do not see how any
Minister of Education could possibly like
it, although expressions do change. I
heard this afternoon from the new
Minister of Education the word "blitz,"
and I am glad to say that no such word
as that appears in any of our Acts of
Parliament.
But there is another point under this
same Clause which gives me a good deal
of difficulty. We are not given any definition of the expression, member of
the workman's family." That being so
-and I should like to be corrected if I
am wrong-and as we have no special
definition of (( member of a workman's
family," we are thrown back on the definition given in, I think, Section 4 of the
main Act of 1927. The House will, no
doubt, remember the definition. It is a
very wide one which covers a man's
grandmother, if not his niece. It contains, so to speak, a perfect staircase
saturated with the life blood, but it does
directly confine itself to consequenity. It
does not let in affinity anywhere, so that
when you get your new housekeeper and
she happens to be, as she may very well
be, the sister-in-law of the workman,
under that Clause of the main Act that
sister-in-law will not be able to come in
as housekeeper.
I think that is a matter we ought to
consider at a later stage. It rather brings
us up against the whole question of dependants. I will put something on the
Paper to enable the matter to be considered, and to enable the House in
Committee to have a definite reply as to
the attitude of the Government to that
question. I think it is probably a case
in which the House will decide that we
should stop at discussion, and should not
endeavour to introduce so cardinal a
change as that would be, but I do think
it is a'matter that ought to be considered.
It must be remembered that by our Act
of 1941 we extended the scope of these
Workmen's Compensation Acts to people.
who have a very much higher rate of income than was the case before, and
therefore the possibility of these housekeepers coming in is very much increased.
It is a question, as I have said, which
ought to be considered.
I do not think there are any other
points that arise on the Bill which are
likely to occasion discussion, and I agree
with the right hon. Gentleman that the
sooner we get it through the better, although I think that if we adopt the suggestion I made at the beginning of
getting back to the spirit of the United
Kingdom first Clause we shall prevent
ourselves from being in any way parties
to such an anomaly as would exist if the
Bill went through exactly as it stands.
As it stands I support the Second Reading.

Mr. MAGUIRE: I wish to draw the
attention of the House to a very
important matter at the moment. It
affects a great number of the community
in Northern Ireland. I refer to the distribution of ration cards. The Larne
Rural Council Food Office have notified
the residents in their area to attend at the.
Larne Food Office in order to get their
ration cards changed. The area extends
a very long distance. Glenarm is about
13 miles from Larne, and the people there
have to travel to Larne to get their ration
cards. Carnlough is about 15 miles from
Larne, and Glenariff, which is close to
me, is 25 miles from the same place. The
people in these districts are asked to go
to the expense of travelling to Larne to
get their ration cards changed. I think
that is a terrible. drain to put on the
people. They cannot afford the expense
of travelling such long distances. Most
of the people in these rural areas belong
to the farming community and can illafford the loss of a full day to travel to
Larne to get their ration books.
In my area, the Ballycastle Rural District Food Office made a very sensible and
very convenient arrangement. They
decided to allocate certain of the elementary schools in their area as centres, and
they sent two ladies
Office in Ballycastle
the books all over the
from the Food
who
who distributed
area. No one in
the Ballycastle area would have to
travel more than 1 miles in order to
get his or her ration book changed. Why
has the Larne food office not adopted a
similar attitude? It appears that a great
many other districts in Northern Ireland
have also been put to great inconvenience.
It is very necessary that something should
be done immediately because the ration
books are being issued at the present time
all over the Six Counties. I feel that the
Minister concerned should get in touch
immediately with the food office in Northern Ireland and urge the authorities to
facilitate the residents in the different
areas. In England Lord Woolton urged
the food office to facilitate the residents
in the rural districts and also to provide
mobile transport as branch offices in outlying districts. I urge the Leader of the

Major DOBBS: I beg to ask
Leader of the House-
Whether he can state if arrangements
have been made, or will be made by the
Ministry of Agriculture, to ensure that

by

farm machinery, from pulley belts for

Rev. Professor CORKEY: I propose
to give one reply to the two questions
which the hon. and gallant Senator has
asked about the provision of agricultural
machinery and spare parts.
The Ministry of Agriculture endeavours constantly to ensure that
adequate supplies of suitable types of
agricultural machinery and spare parts
are available in Northern Ireland when
they are required. Arrangements for
supplies from the United States of
America, from Canada and from production in Great Britain are made well in
advance of the times when they are needed
and, in fact, the programme for supplies
in the year 1944 has already been settled.
Some of the more important types of
machinery are in the country long before
they are required.
In view of the difficulties in connection
with production and transport it is not
always possible to obtain exactly the make
of machinery required, but the general
supply position with regard to machinery
and spare parts is not unsatisfactory, and
there is no doubt that in this respect
Northern Ireland is receiving its fair
share of production. Special attention is
always devoted to ensure that there are
sufficient spare parts for servicing agricultural machinery supplied to and
already in Northern Ireland.

questions together. I would like to say
I think the answer is entirely inadequate,

Delivery of Heavy Machinery.
Major DOBBS: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether the Ministry of Agriculture will ask the transport
authorities to deliver heavy
heavy farm
machinery on special low-loading trucks,
Finance Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
or in cases where only the ordinary 3 or
5 ton lorry is available, that long baulks
or skids will be carried to facilitate unloading.
as
Rev. Professor CORKEY: The question of the use of special low-loading
trucks or long baulks or skids for the
delivery of heavy farm machinery has
been discussed with the Northern Ireland Road Transport Board. It will be
appreciated that it is now of the utmost
importance that petrol and rubber should
be conserved, and that, therefore, the
Board's vehicles must be used
economically as possible. The Board has
a limited number of low-loading trucks,
but these are not used for the delivery of
heavy farm machinery, because they are
not suitable for the transport of general
merchandise. If they were employed for
the delivery of heavy farm machinery,
not only would they have to return unloaded, but the cost of transport to the
farmer would be much higher. The
Board has already in use a type of skid
to facilitate unloading heavy farm
machinery, but the use of such skids of
the type referred to by the hon. and
gallant Senator would also interfere with
the carrying of general merchandise.
I may add that neither the Northern
Ireland Road Transport Board nor the
Ministry of Agriculture has received a
single complaint regarding difficulty in
unloading farm machinery.

Belfast Corporation Bill, as amended by
the Joint Committee, without amendment; they agree to the Solicitors Bill
without amendment; they have passed the
Finance Bill, to which they desire the
concurrence of this House.
Finance Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time." This Bill deals only with
the changes in taxation announced by the
Minister of Finance in his Budget statement.
In Clause 1 no change in principle is
involved, the existing rates of Entertainments Duty being merely increased to the
higher rates recently introduced in Great
Britain.
Clause 2 gives legislative sanction to the
concession announced by the Minister of
Finance on the 25th March last. It allows
the owner of an agricultural tractor to use
his vehicle under a 5s licence to transport
agricultural produce or articles required
for agricultural land without any limitation in point of distance. Hitherto the
5s licence only allowed him to carry such
goods from one part of his land to
another. This measure is, of course,
purely a wartime one, and will be subject
to withdrawal at the end of the present
emergency, nor does it exempt a farmer
from compliance with the provisions of the
Road and Railway Transport Act
(Northern Ireland) 1935, which prohibits
the carriage of other person's goods except
under certain conditions.
Clause 3, which is very complicated,
provides that where a person who has a
limited interest (i.e., is a life tenant
only) in the unascertained residue of an
estate, dies, that limited interest will be
subject to Estate Duty. The Clause is
declaratory only, as it sanctions what is
and has long been, the practice in the
levying of Estate Duty. Fresh legislation
has become necessary, however, owing to
the doubt cast on this practice by a
decision given in the Court of Appeal in
Great Britain some few years ago.
Clause 4 gives to an adopted child
the privileges with regard to legacy and
succession duties which have, up to now,
only belonged to children born in wedlock.
In order to prevent any abuse of these
privileges the concession is not operative
unless the dispositions by the adopter or
by the adopted child have been made for
more than three years before the date on
which the property passes.
In Clause 5 we have a reduction of the
rate of interest charged on deferred
payments of death duties from 4 to 3 per
cent. The rate charged since 1919 has
been 4 per cent., but in view of the low
143 Workmen's Compensation Bill-
22 JUNE 1943

Committee
rates of interest now current a reduction
to 3 per cent. is considered justifiable.
The Stamp Duty referred to in Clause
6 is more generally known as Corporation
Duty. It is charged at the rate of 5 per
cent. per annum on the net profits arising
from any real or personal property vested
in any body of persons in such a manner
that the property does not become liable
'to death duties. There are certain exemptions from this tax, such as religious,
charitable, and educational bodies, and it
is proposed following the practice in Great
Britain to make exemption specific in the
case of Trade Unions and Trade Protection Associations.
These are all the remarks which I think
it necessary to make on this Bill. Some
of these Clauses will mean sacrifices on
the part of the Treasury, but in the light
of all the facts it has been thought advisable to make these changes.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Moved, "That this Bill be now committed."(Rev. Professor Corkey.)
On Question, Motion negatived.
Ordered, That the Bill be read the
Third time on Thursday next.

The following Amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie:
Clause 1, after sub-clause (2) insert
the following new sub-clause:
"(3) A female being related to the
widow, or to the deceased wife, of the
workman by a relationship to which
sub-section (3) of section four of the
Workmen's Compensation Act (Northern Ireland), 1927, would have applied
had the relationship been to the workman, may for the purposes of the foregoing sub-section of this section be
deemed to be a member of the workman's family."
Mr. LESLIE: It may be remembered
that on the Second Reading of this Bill
I dwelt at some little length-I am not
sure if I did not deal with it at greater
length than I ought to have done on the
Second Reading-with this question.
The point was that the Bill itself does
not give us any direct definition of the
expression "member of the workman's
family." The only explanation I could
myself find of that expression was that
given in the Section of the big Act of
1927, which is quoted on the Amendment
Paper. That is, in effect, that the relation to come in under this Clause must
be a blood relation of the workman himself; not a blood relation of the workman's widow or of the workman's deceased
wife. Whether that is what Parliament
intends or not is a matter which we must
make clear this afternoon, because it is
of the first importance that Parliament
should always know what it is going to
enact.
Whether we ought to extend the wording of the Bill-assuming I am right in
my interpretation of the meaning of the
expression "member of a workman's
family" is another question, but if I
am right, and if it ought to be extended,
it would mean, as I tried to warn the
House the other day, that we should be
going a little beyond the English Act,
and that might be the cause of difficulty
here. As to that the right hon. Gentleman will no doubt be able to instruct us,
but as it stands it does seem to me
to be a little hard that a man's deceased
wife's sister who may have come to the
house to look after young children and
stayed in the house as housekeeper when
the children grew up, and while acting
as housekeeper has taken a war-time job,
should be denied such benefit as is obtainable under this Clause.
It is not easy to get benefit under this
Clause. You have first of all got to be.
related to someone. Then you have got
to be a housekeeper, and you have got to
be engaged in a war job, and there has
got to be a sort of assumption that when

[Mr. Leslie]
the war job is over it was your intention
to return to the housekeeping job. But
on the face of things we ought definitely
to consider seriously whether the advantages we offer to the direct relation of
the deceased workman ought to be extended to the relation of the deceased's
widow or the deceased's wife, if that

even if the Committee did so decide, it is
not at all certain that those who sit in
another place will

agree
We

Rev. Professor CORKEY: The right
hon. and learned Gentleman has thrown
a good deal of light on the Clause concerned, and he has dealt with the point
with much more lucidity and ability than
perhaps I shall be able to do in giving
expression to the opinion of the Government in regard to this amendment.
The persons who are eligible to receive.
compensation after a workman's death are
such members of his or her family as
were dependent wholly or partially upon
him for the ordinary necessaries of life.
"Member of a family" is defined in the
principal Act (Workmen's Compensation.
Act (Northern Ireland), 1927) as wife,
husband, father, mother, grandfather,
grandmother, stepfather, stepmother,
son, daughter, grandson, granddaughter,
stepson, stepdaughter, brother, sister,
half brother, half sister. Under the
Adoption of Children (Workmen's Compensation) Act (Northern Ireland), 1934,
legally adopted children of either spouse
are eligible in the same way as natural
children.
If the amendment was accepted, eligibility for compensation would be extended
to
the deceased man's sister-in-law,
mother-in-law, and a good many other
in-laws. It should not be accepted, in
the opinion of the Government, chiefly for
two reasons-(1) the measure is one
which was agreed in Great Britain, and
it would be entirely unjustifiable here to
attempt to stretch it beyond what the
interested parties are prepared for; (2)
the definition of members of a family in
the principal Act is a highly important
one, and it would be a bad precedent to
extend it even for the present very limited
purpose.
The Government suggest that for these
reasons we should not amend this Bill,
and that we should not in any way make
our law in this matter different from the
law in England, but that we should go
step by step in all social legislation.
place
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: While agreeing to some extent with the second
argument advanced by the right hon.
Gentleman, I really must protest against
his first point. What has it got to do
with this House, what another House of
another Parliament in another
chooses to do? We cannot be bound in
any way by the legislation of another
Parliament in matters which constitutionally are within our competence.
In respect of matters reserved to the
Imperial Government we are bound, but
not in respect to matters which are not
reserved.
In regard to the right hon. Gentleman's
second point, I appreciate that we must
proceed with great caution in altering, in
a minor Bill, principles which have been.
established in the major Act. It seems
to me that while one might well hesitate
to extend in all cases the compensation
given to dependent relatives of the workman to relatives-in-law, that there is
something special about the position of a
relation-in-law who has perhaps given up
other employment and devoted herself for
years to the welfare of the workman and
his family. It seems to me that in such
a case it is very hard that she should be
deprived of benefit from the mere
accident that she is a relation-in-law and
not a blood relation. The question is
whether this is a sufficiently special case
to justify special legislation. I feel that
it is, though I do not feel it so strongly
that I would press the point against the
considered opinion of the Government.
Mr. LESLIE: I think I can only say

the case for the deceased wife's sister, the

Mr. LESLIE: This is another small
matter I also mentioned on the Second
Reading. It seems to me that if we do
not accept the Amendment I have on the
Paper, which will have the effect of
dating our Act back to the commencement of the English Act, we shall be
creating an anomaly. We shall be departing from the principle to which our
attention has just been directed, the important principle of keeping in step with
England, because the Act will take us out
of step with England if we bring it in at
a different date. I cannot see that there
is any insuperable difficulty in dating it
back to the 4th February, the date on
which the Act applicable to Great Britain
came into operation.
It is probably the case that there will
be very few accidents in Northern Ireland
that will be affected, but the fact that
there will be so few accidents simply
serves to accentuate any anomaly there
would be between the person whose husband died before the Act came into operation, and the person whose husband died
after the Act came into operation. It is a
very sharp distinction, and the amount of
time involved is not great. I think it
would be better, if we could, that
should make the date of our Act applicable in the same way as the date of the
English Act, and for that reason I move
the amendment. The amount of time involved is not long, and there is a precedent, so to speak, established in the following Clause of the Bill before us, where
retrospection is allowed, although no
we
doubt with a saving that that retrospection should not involve a re-adjustment
of sums already paid.
Rev. Professor CORKEY-This is an
amendment which at first sight is very
attractive, and the right hon, and learned
Senator has made out quite a good case
for it, if I may venture
to say SO.
Although it may be a bad precedent to
attempt to make Workmen's Compensation legislation retrospective, nevertheless
the effect would be merely to put employers and insurance companies here in
the position of having to pay as from the
same date as in Great Britain, namely,
4th February, 1943.
There are most serious practical difficulties, however, if the amendment is accepted. Fatal claims are usually dealt with
very promptly and the lump sum due.
paid into Court, and a memorandum of
the terms of agreement lodged. The
Registrar, if he considers the agreement
is in order, registers it, but if not, the
.case comes before the Judge. Once the
final decision is reached, it stands, however, and, in practice, fraud is the only
ground upon which- decisions are ever
upset. It might be possible to reopen
cases where money paid into Court had
not yet been paid out, and where the
money has not yet been paid into court
there is no difficulty. There is no way,
however, in which money paid into Court
and paid out by the Court could be collected again and redistributed.
There is the further point that under
present high wage conditions the lump.
sum paid is often the maximum under the
Act and in such case there would, of
course, be no more available for distribution-it would merely be a matter of reallocating the same total over an increased
number of dependants.
Personally I regret that the amendment cannot be adopted, for the reasons
which the right hon. and learned Member
instanced, but there are weighty reasons
on the other side. It is a case of choosing the lesser of two evils, and in this
case I think it would be choosing the
lesser evil not to accept the amendment.
Mr. LESLIE: Nobody else seems
anxious to insist on the amendment, and
therefore I think it is probably not desirable to pursue it. It is only fair to point
Adjournment-
Business of the House
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
out that, although the amendment would
relieve us of one possible anomaly, we
should still be left with an anomaly in
the case of the man who died before 4th
February and the man who died after
that date. I think we should accept the
explanation that, however desirable the
amendment might be, it is not practicable to accept it. Therefore, I think it
should be withdrawn.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.

QUESTION TO MINISTER.
Parts for Agricultural Machinery.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major
Dobbs :-
-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if it is a fact that
responsible firms in Belfast have only.
been able to get about one-half of the
supplies of British made spare fingers
and knives for reapers and mowers
which they consider necessary for the
coming season, and whether the situation with regard to spare parts for
both tractor and horse-drawn ploughs
has been and still is equally serious.
Major DOBBS: I do not think I need
apologise to this House for repeating this
question, seeing how very important the
question of the supply of parts and replacements is to every farmer in Northern
In
Ireland. New machinery is becoming
more and more difficult to obtain, so that
is an added reason why the machinery
that we do possess, whether it is horsedrawn or tractor-drawn, should be repaired at once, and that the requisite
parts for doing so should be available in
this country. Parts are wanted for replacing breakages and parts are required
to replace ordinary wear and tear.
1940, when I had still three-quarters of
my grain crop to eut, my binder suddenly
had a breakage. I rushed up to Belfast,
but I was quite unable to get a replacement, which was a very serious matter.
There was a tractor and a binder idle, and
there was the prospect of most of the
harvest being lost simply through lack of
a small piece of metal.
We had hoped that in the fourth year
of the war the situation would have
improved. I may say I touched on this
question of supply of parts when I spoke
in this House on 5th February last, and
a few days ago I asked a question about
supply. The answer was that special
attention was always devoted to ensure
that there are sufficient spare parts for
servicing agricultural machinery supplied
to and already in Northern Ireland. I
wonder if that reply was entirely correct.
I think it was a bit optimistic, and I can
give three reasons for that. First of all,
in my own experience I have personally
been unable to get small replacements
that I have required. Secondly, the local
smith in my area has his lane up to his
cottage and smithy so blocked that it is
almost impossible to drive up to his
smithy. Some of these machines have
been there for weeks, and the hold-up is
certainly accentuated by the fact that he
has not been able to get small parts for
the repairs which are necessary to those
machines.
Thirdly, I believe the situation is
equally bad in the case of farm implement repairers in Belfast. Machines require
certain repair, but they cannot be repaired
because the necessary parts are not in
Northern Ireland. Firms dealing in farm
machinery here are quite prepared to put
the
up money so that they may carry the
necessary stock of parts for servicing
machinery which they have supplied and
which they have handled, but they cannot
obtain such a supply of these parts as they
consider essential. Surely they themselves have a very good idea of the number

order.
I think the farmers in Northern Ireland

Professor Corkey): The following is the
reply from the Ministry of Agriculture
which has been put into my hand:
As mentioned in my reply to the hon.
and gallant Senator last week, the
Ministry of Agriculture is making every
effort to obtain supplies of spare parts
for agricultural machinery already in
the country. There has been difficulty
in obtaining spare parts, especially
fingers for mowers and reapers due to
a shortage of malleable castings, but
strong representations have been made
to the manufacturers concerned, and as
a result some supplies have been despatched to agents in Belfast and elsewhere. It is expected that further
supplies will soon be available.
position with regard to spare parts for
tractor and horse-drawn ploughs has
been and is generally satisfactory with
the exception of parts for a few makes
of ploughs which are not widely used in
Northern Ireland. Limited supplies of
these parts were received to enable
users to proceed with spring tillage
work.

Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I am glad this
matter has come before the House, because it helps quite strongly the argument
I put forward in regard to export licences.
I suggested that there should be a local
office in Belfast to deal with these things
on behalf of the British Government,
because it is a great drawback to business
to have to write to London for an export
or an import licence when it is required.
It gives the Englishman a jump ahead of
us, and enables him to export to Eire three
weeks ahead of the Six Counties' man,
owing to the fact that the Six Counties'
man has to wait for an export licence from
London.
I know a firm which applied to London
for an export licence to Eire, and at the
end of three weeks they had not had even
a reply. It was almost four weeks before
they got the licence. I must say this for
the Ministry. When the question was
raised in this House the Leader of the
House kindly said that the Ministry of
Commerce were taking the matter up with
London, and that they would do what
they could. I had a letter from the
Ministry of Commerce a few days afterwards stating that one of their representatives was going over to London, and
was to get in touch with the export licence
department. As a result of that, a few
days afterwards this firm had not alone
one of the export licences but all the
licences asked for, which shows the importance of keeping these matters before
the public.
I was surprised to hear the Leader of
the House saying that the Ministry of
Agriculture would not be surprised at the

are entitled to raise any question we like.
We try to help the public, and we try to
help the Ministry of Agriculture, too, and
I sincerely trust that the Ministry of
Agriculture will consider the position
from this point of view, and will request
the British Government to have at least
an agent or a local office here in Belfast,
where we can apply for these things.
I was amazed a short time ago when a
firm imported some stuff into the country.
The question of duty on the discount arose,
and the Customs authorities insisted on

he had been allowed the discount previously. The firm in question wrote to

Major PERCEVAL MAXWELL:
understand that on this occasion it is permissible for Members of this House to
raise questions on the policy of the
Government. I think I am right in saying that this is the one occasion in the
year that matters that are not purely
administrative can be raised, and therefore I would ask the indulgence of the
House to raise certain matters that I
believe are of great importance to the
welfare of the Province. As a rule we
have the power on this occasion to call in
question the past acts of the Government,
and to inquire as to its future policy. On
this occasion we have not the first
advantage, because while we were away
from this House the Government suddenly
changed.
The right hon. Gentleman, the Leader
of the House, has in this House and elsewhere stated that he took part in a revolution. I think the right hon. Gentleman
has rather exaggerated the position. I
should describe it more in words familiar
to those who fly as a case in which a
certain number of Ministers have done a
half roll and a side slip. I do not think
that any person has revolted, but I was
glad to hear the right hon. Gentleman
express the opinion that the storm which
placed him in the position of Leader of
this House, and certain of his colleagues
in office, and which displaced others, is a
revolution, because I think it is time that
something very drastic took place in the
Government of this country.
strain
We have been nearly four years at war,
and no serious effort has been made by
the Government to organise the country
as a country should be organised for war
purposes. Certain sections of the country
have been very fully and thoroughly
organised. The farmers, for instance,
quite rightly are compelled to
every effort to produce the food without
which we could not live. But nothing
has been done seriously to supply the
farmers with the necessary labour to till
their farms, or rather to save the crops
after they have tilled them. I trust I
am not taking the right hon. Gentleman
by surprise when I raise this point.
Some time ago, in answer to a question
on this subject, I received an answer
which I thought, and I believe other
Members of this House thought, was
ambiguous.
The hay harvest is coming on, and in
England camps are being organised, great
appeals are being sent out and office
organisation is going on apace. The
whole scheme is going ahead energetically,
but what is being done on similar lines in
this country? Ambiguous statements
about co-operation between certain unspecified organisations, and between the
Ministry of Education and the Ministry of
Agriculture, have been mentioned in this
House, but has any Member of this House,
in going about his ordinary daily business,
seen any signs of that co-operation? Are
any arrangements being made in areas
where a large amount of unemployment
exists? Has any arrangement been made
to transfer some of those labourers to
other areas requiring agricultural labour?
I think this House will agree that on the
surface nothing has been done, and I
think it is a most serious omission on the
part of a Government that has said this
Appropriation Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Major Perceval-Maxwell]
country must supply itself with food or
starve.
It is no good saying we have a million
acres under the plough. I do not know
exactly what the figures are, but it is
essential that those acres should produce
crops, and that those crops should be harvested. But unless something is done,
and done very soon, those crops, or a
large proportion of them, will be lost for
ever, and all the work that has been put
into their early culture will have been
destroyed.
I myself think the Government would have been better advised to
encourage farmers to produce better crops
on a smaller acreage than to plough large
areas they cannot cultivate. That is a
matter which only the Government, in its
knowledge of the requirements of the
people, can decide.
I would, however,
put it to them for consideration in future
years, would it not be better to take the
farmers into their confidence, find out how
much, with the available labour and
machinery, can be suitably cultivated to
produce the maximum yield of which the
land is capable, and then decide how that
shall be carried out, county by county,
and district by district. That is the proper way to organise the situation.
There is one other point, which affects
the Ministry of the right hon. Gentleman
who leads this House, to which I
would call attention. Recently this
country and England have fixed
their eyes on a great ideal, and,
of course, the stormy waters before
us have appeared to some to be a horizon
glowing with hope and ripe with promise.
But how many rocks and hidden dangers
lie before us before we reach that happy
horizon; rocks that are waiting to trap
and to destroy alike the unskilful navigator and the unwary seamen. I do not
know how skilful our navigators would
prove to be; I do not know whether they
would survive the effort of piloting our
ship safely into the harbour of social
security; and whether they will be able to
prevent, after this war, much of the
anxieties of life that exist to-day. But I
do know that, however skilful our navigators may be, unless they are supported
by seamen worthy of the task they will not
succeed, and it is the privilege and duty
of the Department led by the right hon.
Gentleman, the Minister of Education, to
provide the training and the skill for the
next generation.
There are two things which are going to
rule this world for some time after the
war. One of them is the Beveridge
Report, and the other is the Atlantic
Charter. I think the right hon. Gentleman has got a difficult task in engraving
on the minds of the young people of this
country a full consciousness of the fact
that they are not a self-satisfying unit,
but that they are part of a very great
collection of individuals, some of whom
are developing special characteristics. I
think the future is becoming very insecure. I doubt whether the education
authorities of this country are really
giving to the youth that which they will
need in the future for carrying them forward into this new and promising age.
Unless the people of Ulster of the future
are capable of taking a statesmanlike
point of view, their representatives at
future conferences all over the world will
not be able to carry their full weight, for
no man or woman who has any control of
the policy of a country can, if that
country is democratic, far exceed the
wishes and aims of those who have put
him or her into office.
It is very essential, if we in this
country are to walk in line with England,
that the minds of the youth of this country
should be attuned to the ideas prevailing
in England and outside the shores of this
country. There is nothing that will
damage our position so severely after this
war as a too narrow provincial outlook.
That is a thing that wants to be impressed
most strongly on young people of this
country who will be the future rulers of
the country. In conclusion, I would like
to say to the right hon. Gentleman that
unless something is done now, and done
consistently and continuously, what
heritage will we leave our children except
insurmountable difficulties which they
will be unable, because they will be unfit,
to overcome.
Mr. BROWNE: Like the hon. and
gallant Senator who has just spoken, I
should like to avail myself of the Appropriation Bill to refer to one or two
subjects. First of all I desire to draw
attention to the statement made in another place by Mr. Rowley Elliott alleging that thousands of gallons of skimmedmilk are being thrown away and destroyed
daily. That seems to me to be an
appalling statement, and, if true, I think,
immediate steps should be taken to have
Appropriation Bill-

29 JUNE 1943
the practice stopped. I am quite sure
that the Leader of the House, who takes
such a deep interest in social questions,
would not approve of destruction of that
kind taking place. Of course, I know the
Ministry of Agriculture has a big task in
taking charge of and controlling our milk
supplies, and there may be difficulties of
which I have no knowledge. We are told
that in five or six weeks a drying plant
will be in operation at some of the new
milk depots to deal with surplus milk.
That is very satisfactory, but in the meantime is this waste to continue?
I read a report of the Minister's speech
on the subject in the Belfast News-Letter.
I have not yet seen the report in Hansard,
which is verbatim, and perhaps a fuller
explanation is there given, but what
appeared in the Press caused a good deal
of disquiet on the part of members of the
public, who cannot understand why any
foodstuffs should be thrown away or destroyed. I was speaking to a farmer last
week, and, I am prompted to ask,
are the milk depots properly distributed
throughout the country, and are the
farmers who rear calves, young pigs, and
fowl given transport facilities by which
they can obtain this skimmed milk, or,
better still, can the skimmed milk not be
returned to the farmers in their empty
cans. In his own interests, and in the
interests of the Ministry, a statement
should be made through the Press setting
forth the facts. The Minister says but
for the factories which have taken the
place of the creameries 19,000 gallons of
skimmed milk in the Omagh district and
6,000 gallons in the Ballymoney district
would now be going down the drain daily.
That is a very disturbing statement, and
the sooner the whole position is clarified
the better.
Then there is the housing problem. We
all know the serious shortage that exists
not only in Belfast but also in the provincial towns, due, of course, to the air
raids. I am sorry to say that this
shortage has been seized upon by some
persons to engage in house property speculation and profiteering. I refer to the
sale of unoccupied houses. I may be told
that the law of supply and demand governs
the price. Quite so, but why should the
owners of unoccupied houses be placed in
a more favourable position than the
owners of occupied houses. The rents of
occupied houses of a certain valuation are
controlled. The rents cannot be raised.
If the Rent Restriction Acts were withdrawn, these houses could be let at almost
double their present rents. Perhaps it is
a good job they cannot be raised. Why
then should the owners of unoccupied
houses be permitted to take advantage of
the war situation, and to extort prices for
houses double or treble their pre-war
value. The price of nearly everything
we use is controlled. We are restricted in
our purchases, and yet we see houses, and
farms too, sold at these enhanced and
exorbitant prices. Would it not be possible to have the selling price of houses.
with possession related to the existing
controlled rents? I understand that the
British Government, in taking land for
aerodromes, pay only the pre-war value
or price in 1939.
The other day a very interesting letter
appeared in the Belfast News-Letter from
Mr. Roy McKee, an architect. I think
his object was to suggest that any money
secured for house property above its prewar value should be liable to taxation.
Among other things, he said:
Houses with a valuation of between
£20 and £40 per annum, and worth
between £500 and £1,000 in 1939 are
being sold to-day for prices varying
from £1,200 to £3,000. Whenever a
house becomes vacant it is the practice
not to let it again, but to offer it for
sale with vacant possession. Thus the
persons who can afford to rent such a
dwelling are precluded from occupying
it, while the property owner not only
puts a fat untaxable profit in his pocket
but further adds to the housing difficulties of to-day and the future.
What I have said about the sale of
houses with possession also applies to
houses vacated by the owners and then
rented. It is no uncommon thing to see
a controlled house let at £50 a year and
taxes, and the house next door, of a
similar size, let at £70 or £80 simply
because the latter house was not previously
occupied by a tenant. Surely that is an
anomaly. At present there are many
houses in Belfast which were only partially damaged in the air raids. Could
the necessary building materials not be
provided to put these houses into a habitable condition? If the Government could
see their way to lessen the restrictions in
that respect the situation might be eased.

Another subject that has aroused considerable discussion is the Government's

.--

country cannot even be said to be

Mr. McALLISTER: There are one or
two points I would like to raise on this
Appropriation Bill which vitally concern
an immense section of the people.
course, if one were to deal with all the
criticism to which this inefficient Government leave themselves open an all-night
sitting would not be adequate for the purpose, but there are one or two questions to
which I intend to address myself. With
regard to the question raised by the hon.
and gallant Senator opposite about the
provision made for the saving of the
present harvest, I think the House will
agree that a fair interpretation of the
answer handed by the Minister of Agriculture to the Leader of the House about
a fortnight ago was to the effect that
adequate and complete provision had been
made to secure the harvest and crops for
this present year, which is so vital to the
people of this area. What do we find
when the harvest is now in progress and
the war nearly four years in being?
We find that in another place the
Minister of Agriculture said they are
preparing a scheme for getting labourers
to move from place to place during the
present harvest. But that is not all.
He goes further and says that advertisements will appear in the Press at some
future time telling the farmers to whom
they should apply for these facilities for
labour, and how to go about it. Does
that answer not condemn any Ministry
after four years of war? The harvest has
already commenced, and out of a lone
mouth the Minister of Agriculture states
he is now preparing a scheme and at a
future date he intends to issue advertisements. I leave the answer as it is, beno criticism of mine could be so
denouncing as the answer of the Minister
himself.
cause
There is another question about which
a vast section of the people are vitally
concerned. That is that hundreds of the
youth of Northern Ireland are at present
lying in dungeons untried except in the
secret archives of their political opponents.
I am informed by men of the highest
honour, men of unimpeachable character,
men scrupulous as men can be, that they
know as a fact that boys are interned in
castles and jails here untried and uncharged who have never belonged directly
or indirectly to any illegal organisation
and were never guilty of a breach of the
law in their lives. Is not that a horrible
thought? Bring that home to ourselves.
Let any Member of this House think for
a moment of a close relation, a brother,
a father, a son, consigned to these
dungeons without trial. What would
your feelings be?
I am unable to make up my mind defi
nitely what is the purpose of all this,
whether it is a deliberate attempt to cause
unrest in this area, or whether it is done
through sheer stupidity. I am not prepared to answer that question, but I do
know this, that these dispirited youths of
Northern Ireland, who are not of the
same religious persuasion or politics of the
Government, are exasperated and driven.
without control by the untrammelled raiding and unceasing hunting, depriving our
people of their first rights of citizenship
and sanctity. We believe the family home
is sacrosanct. Yet here we have, day
Appropriation Bill-
29 JUNE 1943
Second Reading
after day, the nomes of decent, lawabiding people raided, perhaps on the
suggestion of some vicious neighbour, the
sanctity of their homes violated, no charge
brought against them that they can refute
publicly and deny. That is a state of
affairs which cannot continue.
I will say this, that if the Government
believe that by this unfair legislation, and
biased, partial administration they are
going to destroy in the people of Northern
Ireland their love of Ireland and their
Irish nationality, they are under a grave
misapprehension, because it is certain that
when this Government have passed into
oblivion and are forgotten by the people,
just as their immediate predecessors have
been, the spirit of Irish nationality will
live on and the soul of Ireland will survive. Having voiced my protest against
this unconstitutional action of a partisan
Government against political opponents
and men of different religious views, I will
pass to another and vital matter.
We have heard no discussion, no provision, no programme, no suggestion as to
what is to happen during the terrible
aftermath of this present war. We must
all see that chaos, and confusion, and
suffering, and want, and poverty are to
follow. Surely it is the duty of the
Government to tell us in this House, and
to tell the people through Parliament,
what, if anything, they are doing at the
present moment to meet that terrible time.
I remember, when a War Compensation
Bill was passing through this House, I
pointed out the instance of large linen
mills which had been burned down. We
know that in the areas surrounding these
mills, whether urban or rural, or in the
city of Belfast, lived families who for
generations have worked in that mill.
By their toil and labour and sweat they
have contributed capital, and have helped
to build up the business that kept the
mill going.
I pointed out, when that Bill was
passing through this House, that these
mills before the war had not been a highly
marketable asset, and I pointed out that
it was the duty of Parliament to secure
that the tribunal that paid out compensation for war damage should have the right
to take into consideration the necessities,
the needs, and the advantages of the
homes on the areas surrounding these
mills, but instead of that, Parliament
decreed that the mill which was unmarketable and could not be capitalised before
the war could now be paid for in cash to
the individual owner. If these mills are
not to be rebuilt, as I say they should be,
by means of compensation and generously
equipped, what is going to happen after
the war to the hundreds and thousands of
employees living in these districts. I
suggest that is a question that should
receive the urgent, immediate and exhaustive attention of the Government to see
what can be done, as far as possible, to
avert the terrible calamities of poverty
and distress that are bound to follow
immediately after this war before the
time of reconstruction takes place.

not only sentimental but also practical.

the last word does rest with the Ministry,
will refrain from condemning these gates,

Mr. MAGUIRE: I should like to refer
to the great loss and damage done by
foxes all over the Six Counties. I notice
that the Derry Committee of Agriculture
held a meeting last Saturday, at which
it was stated that during the past three
months claims for rewards had been received for the destruction of 720 foxes in
the county. The committee considered a
resolution advocating the employment of
skilled trappers to cope with the menace
caused by the great increase of foxes in
the county, and recommended that the reward for foxes should be increased. That
is the point I wish to speak on.
In one
area in County Derry during the past
four weeks' claims have been lodged for
97 cubs, 61 foxes, and 49 vixens, a total
of 207 foxes in the month. After putting
in their crops the farmers had a little
time at their disposal during which they
could endeavour to shoot or trap the foxes,
but now that the harvest is coming on
there will be no time for that work.
The County Derry Committee of Agriculture suggested the employment of
skilled trappers, and I think it is very
desirable that the Ministry should.
appoint some skilled trappers for a period
of a few months in order to eliminate
this menace. I myself have suffered in
this respect, and I have lost many nights
sleep in going out over the hills in search
of foxes. In my small area upwards of
100 have been destroyed, and there are
still a great number left. During the
past month, in spite of my watchfulness,
I have lost over 40 laying fowl, 20
goslings, and several young lambs, and
that is only one instance. During the
past five weeks, on my farm and adjoining farms, 25 foxes have been trapped
and shot, and they are still very plentiful.
I appeal to the Ministry of Agriculture
to do something in this matter, because
there is not very much encouragement in
the reward for a farmer or farm labourer
to destroy these foxes.
The reward is only 3s a head for cubs,
and 6s per head for dogs and vixens, and
when you compare that with the price of
3s obtainable for a rabbit, of which you
can catch a dozen without losing much
time, there is no encouragement to people
to destroy foxes. It is a very serious
matter, because foxes are multiplying
very rapidly all over the Six Counties.
In Derry they destroyed 207 in four
weeks, and other counties are similarly
affected. I therefore appeal to the Leader
of the House to bring the matter to the
notice of the Minister of Agriculture, and
if the Ministry decide to employ trappers,
I believe the farmers would be willing to
contribute to the cost in order to eliminate
the menace.

Major BOYLE: The hon. Senator opposite (Mr. Browne) mentioned the matter
of spilling skimmed milk at the creameries,
and gave it as something he had heard.
Yesterday, in the course of my work, I
was at one of these new creameries and
was astonished to find that the source of
the water supply was white.
I went up
to the creamery to inquire about it, and
they told me the procedure was that
hundreds and hundreds of gallons of milk
arrived at this creamery. They were
poured into tanks and then passed through
various measures. The cream
separated from the milk, and, as far as I
could find out, the farmers who wanted
the skim could buy it back at 2d a gallon.
There were farmers who did not want it
because they had no pigs to feed, so the
remainder of the milk went down the
drain, as undoubtedly it did, because the
mill pond was as white as a wig. The
men gave me all the information they possibly could.. Apparently what was happening was due to the fact that they have
no machinery for dealing with the skim
milk, but they hope to get it eventually.
I do not know how much milk they will
get out of it, but I do know that at the
present time milk is going back into the
rivers and is being wasted.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: In regard
to some of the matters that have been
raised by hon. Members, I am afraid that
all I can say is that I will refer them to
the various Ministries and ask them to
Appropriation Bill-
Second Reading
29 JUNE 1943
consider the points. There are a number
of points on which I can give a little
further information. First of all, the
amount of the reward that is given for the
destruction of foxes was arrived at after
consultation with the county councils. All
the councils were opposed to any higher
sum being fixed; in fact, several of the
county councils favoured a smaller reward.
I presume, however, that the matter can
be raised again, although it will have to
be done in consultation with the local
authorities who are interested.
Reference has been made to the rather
singular fact that skimmed milk cannot
find a market in Northern Ireland. There
are several things that should be mentioned in mitigation of any responsibility
the Ministry might have in that matter.
I am told that it is largely due to the
fact that the pig population has been
reduced by the war owing to the difficulty
of farmers obtaining food for pigs. Then
it is said that had it not been for the
existence of certain factories the loss
would have been still greater. There is
still a shortage of machinery, but when
it is made good this wastage of skimmed
milk, which is certainly very unfortunate,
will be remedied. I do not know if anythink further can be done, but it does
certainly seem to be an anomaly that milk
should be wasted when there is such a
scarcity of food for both man and beast
in the country.
I would like to make a remark or two
about the organisation of farm workers
for the harvest. The hon. and gallant
Senator (Major Perceval-Maxwell) mentioned this matter as part of a general
statement he made regarding the policy
of the Government in the future. A statement was made in the other House about
a week ago in regard to this matter.
Possibly the hon. and gallant Senator has
not seen the scheme which has been
drawn up.
The scheme is not merely in
process of formation, but it has already
been formed in outline and is in actual
operation in some centres such as Newtownards, Lurgan, Downpatrick, and
other areas.
The scheme is that workers
will be got together in small gangs and
will be sent out to the country districts
as and when required.
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I
had heard nothing of this scheme,
although I live in Downpatrick. Could
the Government publish a White Paper
or some other literature on the subject?
Rev. Professor CORKEY: That will

The hon. and gallant Member who introduced this debate referred to the
Ministry with which I am now associated.

demand, or what the people support. If
they are not educated up to the standard
of the Beveridge Report, they simply will
not want it, or they will refuse it in spite
of all its advantages. May I say that
those who pay income tax will have a say,
and a big say, in regard to the advisability of adopting this and other schemes
after the war, and I hope they will not
protest against the continuation of a fairly
stiff income tax for a number of years
after the war in order that we may win
the peace, which is just as desirable as
winning the war.
I myself will do my own little bit in
seeing that the benefits of the new Education Bill, which is likely to be introduced
at Westminster in the near future, are
extended to Northern Ireland. It is my
contention that we should have a standard
of education at least as high as the
standard that obtains in England. We
have the disadvantage of being a poorer
country than England is, and I think that

QUESTION TO MINISTER.
Roman Catholics and Public
Appointments.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether he can
state if it is a fact that no Catholics, who
are 45 per cent. of the population of
Armagh, were on the grand jury of
County Armagh assizes held on 13th
July last, and why proportionate representation is not given to the minority 35
per cent. of the Six Counties on any of
the following committees and boards, set
up by the Government during the past 12
months: -The Northern Ireland Planning Advisory Board (only one Catholic
out of 40), the Northern Ireland Planning
Commission (none), the Agricultural
Inquiry Committee (one), the Regional
Directorate of Laundry Services Advisory
Panel for Northern Ireland (none), the
Technical Advisory Committee on Electricity (none), the Linen Industry Post
War Committee (none), the Youth Committee of Northern Ireland (two out of
16), the Ministry of Education and
Queen's University of Belfast Joint Committee of Adult Education, Council for the
Encouragement of Music (none), the
House of Commons Select Committee on
Health Services (one out of 10), the Unemployment Assistance Board (none), the
Electricity Supply Board (none), the
Transport Board (none), the Eggs Marketing Board (none), the Pigs Marketing
Board (none), and the Internees Advisory
Board (none).
In putting this question I should like
to point out that the population of
County Armagh is 108,815, and of that
population the Catholics comprise 49,475.
Still as you will see in the question they
have no representation on the grand jury
of County Armagh. The offices of County
Armagh County Council, the county
secretary, the accountant, the valuation
office, the surveyor's office, the education.
committee, the library, and the rural
council, have at least 40 on their pay roll
with an aggregate salary of £18,000 to
£20,000 per year. Yet there is not one
Catholic employed in these offices or one
shilling of these salaries paid to papists,
although they are 45 per cent. of the
population and pay their rates proportionately.
Now dealing with these committees set
up by the Government as you will see the
representation given to the 35 per cent.
minority shows very clearly that there is a
bigoted discrimination against employing
Catholics here in the Six Counties. Moreover there is not a Catholic justice of the
peace on the County Armagh panel of
visiting justices to the prisons, and the
salaries of appointments under the
Government are something similar to what
exists under the County Council of
Armagh, which is the head line and in a
similar position to all the other county
councils. So much for democracy and the
Atlantic Charter. Catholics need not
apply.
I had a little girl in with me a short
time ago. She was looking for a position.
and she put the question to me, would a
Catholic get it. I had to say simply to
her she might apply, but it was only wasting her time. That girl was applying for
a public position. So much for democracy.
This state of affairs shows plainly as I
said that there is some planned policy
of bigoted discrimination against Catholics
as such, to see they are deprived of
appointments which by right they are
entitled to. I think anyone will admit
that if 45 per cent. of the population of
Question to Minister
Question to Minister
27 JULY 1943
Armagh are Catholics and pay their rates
proportionately they are certainly entitled
to consideration and entitled to their
share of any salaried positions that are
going. The Leader of the House is a
minister of the Gospel, preaching charity
and goodwill to men. He will, I trust,
urge the Government to follow his teaching. I make no appeal, but I simply
ask for justice and fair play for the
minority of this portion of the country.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE
(Rev. Professor Corkey): I do not propose
to follow the hon. Senator opposite in
regard to the new material which he has
introduced in his statement in putting
this question, but I should like to reply
to the question as printed in the following
terms:
In reply to the first part of the hon.
Senator's question, I may explain that
the Government has no responsibility for
the appointment of the Grand Juries, or
of Select Committees which are set up by
either House of Parliament.
Members: Hear, hear.)
(Hon.
The Grand Juries are appointed by the
High Sheriffs, whose prorogatives and
powers in this matter are very ancientdating, in fact, from the reign of
Henry II.
With regard to the second part of the
question, in the case of committees and
boards, for which the Government is
responsible, the main consideration in the
selection of members is not only their
suitability and qualification to handle the
business efficiently, but also their desire
to serve their country, and the hon.
Senator's suggestion that religion should
be made a determining factor is one which
the Government is not prepared to entertain. (Hon. Members: Hear, hear.) To
do so would be contrary to established
practice, not only in Great Britain and
Northern Ireland, but in other parts of
the British Commonwealth, and would be
entirely contrary to the public interest.
I would also point out that in the case of
some of the committees to which the hon.
Senator refers-for example, the Planning
Advisory Board and the Planning Commission-all the members other than the
chairman of the former were appointed
either on the nomination of some public
body or by virtue of some office.
With regard to the Internees' Advisory
Committee, I may say that a number of
qualified Roman Catholic lawyers were
asked to serve, but, for reasons which
seemed good to them and which the
Minister of Home Affairs understood,
declined to do so.
There is, therefore, no justification
whatsoever for the implication in the
question that there has been any discrimination on religious grounds.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: When
a question like this is put down, one feels
that one must examine the position in
Southern Ireland to see how it compares
with Northern Ireland. I am able to
our
say to the hon. Member that so far as

Catholic along with
friend Mr.
In the
Maguire as visiting justices.
Belfast Corporation we have one Roman
Catholic chairman of a Committee and
two Roman Catholic vice-chairmen of
Committees, and only recently our friend,
Senator Lynch, was appointed chairman
of the Water Board. Surely no one could
attribute bigotry to the Protestant people
of Belfast and the North of Ireland when
these appointments are taken into consideration. I think it is a pity that a
question like this should be put forward
to blacken the position of Belfast and
make it appear that we, and we only, are
a bigoted people. If we were to examine
the position with regard to Southern Ireland, I am afraid it would not compare
favourably with the position in Northern
Ireland.
Mr. MCALLISTER: The fact remains
that we represent one-third of the population of Northern Ireland, and I would
like to ask the Leader of the House is he
serious in asking us to believe it is a
mere coincidence that one-third of the
population, which is a substantial
minority in any population, are not represented on these committees? I think
that fact is self-obvious. With regard to
the remarks of Senator Cunningham, I
am not in a position to contradict what
he has said. It is not the Belfast Corporation we are indicting here, but the
Northern Government. From my general
Question to Minister
Question to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
information, which is not very detailed,
of the Belfast Corporation, I must say

There is one remark of Senator Cunningham's to which I would like to reply.
He tries to compare the treatment of the
minority of one-third of the population
here with the minority of one-seventh of
the population in Southern Ireland. If
he would read the statements of many of
his distinguished co-religionists-some of
them dignitaries of the Church—he would
see that they pay tribute to the fact that
religion is no test, that the test is merit,
and merit alone, in Southern Ireland.
That I am glad and proud to say is the
only test required for the advancement
of an individual in Southern Ireland.
As I have always said, it is a humiliating position for any Irishman to have to
deal with matters of this description, but
the question on the Paper speaks for
itself. It is an appalling indictment
against any body acting as a Government
in these times, when we are told we are
fighting for democracy, freedom, liberty
of religion, legitimate liberty in all things,
to have such a state of affairs disclosed in
the question on the Paper to-day. That
question speaks for itself. No sidetracking, no special pleading can take
away from the fact that the Government
of Northern Ireland, on that question,
stand indicted of partisanship, bigotry
and administration, and when real democratic boards get into power at some
future date after the war, certainly they
will play a more generous part and more
Christian part than the Northern Government have been playing with regard to
the minority since they were set up.
The Leader of the House has stated
that the only test of the selection of these
members is their fitness for the position
and that religion does not interfere at all,
that it is never thought of. We represent
one-third of the population. In the professional, commercial and business life of
this community we built up in conjunction
with the other sections the prosperous
Northern Ireland that existed prior to
the partition of Northern Ireland. This
portion of Ireland was as prosperous as
any small country in the world prior to
being placed in the hands of the Northern Government in 1920, and it cannot
be denied that since the Government took
over this country-its industries, business
and everything-has gone back step by
step until before the war we had the most
colossal unemployment in Europe.
