1.1
Second Reading
Pensions Bill-

THE SENATE
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time." Before dealing with the
Bill there are two matters to which you
might allow me to make very brief reference. I understand that to-day is the
99th birthday of the Rt. Hon. H. B.
Armstrong, H.M.L., a former Member of
this House. I think he is to be congratulated on that happy event. It is a
good omen for the rest of us. We all
hope that he will complete the century
and, perhaps, more before he is dismissed.
from the field where he has played such
an able game.
Perhaps I may also be pardoned for
doing a rather unusual thing and saying
in my own name and in the name of hon.
Members that we sympathise with a
Member of the Senate, Sir Roland
Nugent, who has suffered a grievous loss
during the war.
There are some observations of a preliminary sort that I should like to make
in introducing this Bill. First of all,
as perhaps the House know, it has already
passed the Imperial Parliament, and it
is desirable that in this matter our legislation should be in line with the legislation across the water. There are reasons
why this Bill should come into force in
Northern Ireland on the same day as it
comes into force in Great Britain, and
therefore this House is asked to give it a
Second Reading to-day.
The Bill presents a very formidable appearance to the
ordinary man, and I venture to say that
without the help of the White Paper
which has been issued would be unintelligible to a layman. But its first appearance is the worst. It is really a good Bill
when one comes to read it more closely
and in the light of the information given
to us on it by those who are conversant
with the whole subject.
The Bill marks a further stage in the
development of our social services. Its
purpose is to effect improvements in the
existing system of social legislation pending the introduction of the more comprehensive scheme envisaged in the Beveridge
Report. I am glad that since the war
began in spite of great national financial
strain, it has been found possible on
different occasions to make further provision for those members of the community who, by reason of old-age or
widowhood or other hard circumstances,
have a special claim to our consideration.
It will be remembered that the scheme
of supplementary pensions was introduced
in 1940; that the household means test
for unemployment assistance and supplementary pensions was abolished in 1941;
that the rates for health insurance benefit
were increased in 1942; and that only
last August the rates of unemployment
assistance and supplementary pensions
were substantially improved.
Amongst those who benefited from the
increase in the rates of supplementary
pensions were widows over 60 years of
age. As, however, supplementation of
pension under the terms of existing legislation was confined to male pensioners
over 65 and to women pensioners over 60,
the increase did not improve the position.
of the widow under 60 with dependent
children. Her claim to be put in the same
position as the older widows in the matter
of supplementation of pension was
that merited consideration, and I am glad
to say that the measure before the House
recognises the justice of that claim.
one
Provision for this will be found in
Clause 3 of the Bill. I regard this provision as one of the most important
features of the Bill, and I am confident
that this further contribution to the welfare of widows and children will bring
happiness into many homes where hitherto
there has been difficulty in making ends
meet. The proposal briefly is that widows
under 60 should be eligible for supplementary pensions if they have children in
respect of whom allowances are being
paid. Thus, a widow with three children
would normally, under the present law,
obtain about 21s in pension and.children's
allowances. The present Bill will increase
that to about 40s.
The Bill further provides that, if the
widow is in receipt of a supplementary
pension at the date when she ceases to be
eligible to draw children's allowances,
there will be no question of her becoming
ineligible for continued supplementation.
Her augmented pension will continue
until she either ceases to be entitled to
her basic pension-for instance, on remarriage or her resources reach the
point at which supplementation is no
longer admissible under the scales laid
down. There are in Northern Ireland
about 4,000
4,000 widows with dependent
children in receipt of widows' pensions,
Pensions Bill-
Second Reading
27 JULY 1943
T
and, as their pensions include allowances
for approximately 8,000 children, the
House will realise the importance of the
provision which is being made for their
further assistance.
Another important feature of the Bill
is that which provides for modification of
the provisions governing the treatment of
capital assets when calculating supplementary pensions, unemployment assistance and financial assistance for the blind.
Provision for this change will be found
in Clause 1, sub-clause (1) of the Bill.
In order to encourage thrift, it is an
established principle that people who
have been able to save in better times,
should not in times of adversity be expected, in all cases, to use up all those
savings for their maintenance before the
State or the local authority comes to their
aid. Various examples of this principle.
will, no doubt, occur to the minds of the
members of the House. The present rule
regarding capital assets in relation to
supplementary pensions, unemployment
assistance and financial assistance granted
to the blind by a local authority is, first,
that the Assistance Board or the local
authority takes into account in full any
capital, other than protected war savings,
in excess of £300, and, second, treats
sums up to that amount as producing a
nominal income of 1s per week for every
complete £25 after the first £25.
The Bill proposes, first, that in future
only capital assets in excess of £400 will
be regarded as available in full to meet
the applicant's needs, and, second, that
the national income for sums up to that
amount should be regarded as 6d per
week for each £25 after the first £25
instead of the former 1s. I regard this
as a very considerable concession to those
who have a little money past them, and a
very big encouragement to save.
In Clause 1, sub-clause (2) a further
concession is made in the matter of
supplementary pensions. This is the provision that in computing resources, superannuation payments in respect of former
employment may be disregarded, up to a
maximum of 10s 6d per week. The present rule is that only the first 7s 6d a
week of a superannuation payment is disregarded. This will mean to many people
an increase in income of 3s a week.
local
The question of financial assistance
given to a blind person by a
authority has also received consideration.
Proposals on this matter will be found in
Clause 4 of the Bill. The Bill provides.
that the financial assistance given by a
local authority to blind persons will, in
future, be left entirely out of account in
computing a blind persons means for the
purpose of a blind pension. It will no
longer be possible for an increase in the
local authority's financial assistance to
result in a reduction of the blind persons' pension, and the local authority will
thus be enabled to make its awards without any fear of the State pension being
reduced on that account.
In Clause 2 of the Bill a further concession is made in the interests of blind
persons. The way is also being opened
for local authorities to apply the
principles of the Determination of Needs.
Act when granting financial assistance
under the Blind Persons Acts.
A further provision in Clause 2 also
calls for mention. In the matter of outdoor relief, it is being provided that
boards of Guardians, in determining the
resources of the person seeking relief,
shall not take into account any amounts
received by any other member of the
household by way of supplemenary pension, unemployment assistance or financial
assistance given by a council in the case
of a blind person. In other words, if
there is a person in a household who is
destitute and outside the scope of the
scheme of State assistance, the boards of
guardians, in considering his case, may not
reduce or refuse the grant of outdoor
relief to him on the ground that a member
of the same household is receiving unemployment assistance, supplementary pension
or financial assistance under the
Blind Persons Acts. The equity of this
provision cannot be gainsaid when it is
remembered that unemployment assistance
or a supplementary pension is the sum
adjudged necessary to maintain the recipient and his or her dependants, and does
not include a margin for the maintenance
of
any other person in the household.
In view of the memorandum which has
been circulated, I need not refer specially
to a number of other small points covered
by the Bill, and it only remains for me to
commend it to the House as a further
measure to improve social services in
Northern Ireland and keep them on a
level with developments in the rest of the
United Kingdom. I beg to move.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Pensions Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: As a
Member of this House I wish to say I am
very pleased to see this Bill introduced.
The major portion of the Bill really deals
with the removal of the penalty that has
been imposed on the thrifty workman.
As I have pointed out here several times,
unfortunately all such legislation has
evidently benefited the wastrel in opposition to the thrifty workman. However,
evidently both the Imperial Government
and our own Government are beginning
to recognise it is an unfair position. When
the first Pension Bill was introduced, the
engineer who had paid for 40 years to get
10s a week superannuation did not get a
penny out of that pension scheme, but the
man who never paid to a society or trade
union got the full amount, although he
probably worked only half of his years
and hung about the corners the other half.
It was an unfair position, and it is a
pleasure to see that it is to be somewhat
improved. Now the trade union person
who is in receipt of 10s per week will only
have 2s 6d of that taken into consideration
under this Bill. That is a great concession,
and a further great benefit is that the
widow who has children is entitled to the
supplementary pension, which is certainly
something both Christianlike and charitable; indeed, in my opinion, it is something she is entitled to. I am extremely
pleased to see this change taking place and
the thrifty workman recognised and given
a little bit-although it is only a portion
yet of the State benefit which other
workers get.
Mr. LESLIE: I suppose we may take
it-indeed I think the right hon. Gentleman
SO indicated that corresponding
legislation has already come into force in
Great Britain; that is to say, that the
necessary legislation has been passed in
the Parliament of the United Kingdom
applying what for convenience I may call
the principles of this Bill not only to
Great Britain, but to the Isle of Man. I
should like to be assured that is the case,
because there is no reference to such an
occurrence in the Bill before us, and an
investigation of some of the details rather
suggests that any reference of that sort
would be out of place if it could be
avoided. The only way it can be avoided,
it semes to me, is on a definite assurance
that not only is it desirable, in the words
of the White Paper, that legislation
should be passed, but it has, in fact, been
passed, and as to that I really must press
for information. I fancy the answer is
in the affirmative.
Arising out of that comes the question
as to what the Bill is going to cost the
Exchequer, and how is that money to be
provided? If I am right in assuming
that the English measure has already been
passed, then the corresponding expenditure, whatever it may be, that is incurred
on this side will come directly or indirectly off what is described as our
Imperial contribution. It will still have
to be met by the general body of taxpayers, but so far as we are concerned
we shall be allowed our corresponding expenditure off our share of the Imperial
contribution. So far as that goes it will
not hurt us in any way except in our
capacity as general taxpayers and whether
the amount involved will be sufficient to
make an appreciable difference or not in
that respect I do not know, because there
is no information given to us as to what
the Bill is likely to cost.
It is intimated, or suggested, I think,
that some of the passages in the Bill will
result in a reduction of the amount that
has to be levied off the poor rate in some
districts of Northern Ireland. That
would seem a result that ought to apply,
but we found, as may be remembered, in
an inquiry in this Province some few
years ago that the amelioration introduced
by various insurance schemes and so on
had not had any effect, so far as could be
ascertained, on the expenditure of poor
relief. The hope was expressed during
that inquiry that sooner or later the
natural law would assert itself, and that
that effect would be produced, although
perhaps not to a very great extent.
Whether the same hope is being entertained here or not one does not really
know. One cannot help but feel that
sooner or later the fact that demands
which were formerly met from poor relief
funds, and are now to be met from the
Exchequer should have an effect, and we
can only hope that from one point of view
it may be so, and that the relief there to
the taxpayers in their capacity as ratepayers may be appreciable.
As regards accepting the Bill as an
instalment of what is called the Beveridge
Report, I should like to safeguard myself.
People talk, as I think I said once before, rather too loosely about the
Pensions Bill-
Second Reading
27 JULY 1943
Beveridge Report as though the Report
itself was something sacrosanct, something
to be taken as a whole. They speak of
the Report rather than the proposals contained in the Report. I should like to
guard against being required to accept
further various Beveridge proposals that
may come before us, because before I do
that I want to know what those proposals
are, and what their effect is likely to be.
But that question is not before us this
afternoon. The only points that are before us are, whether corresponding legislation has been passed across the water
which will bring in Great Britain and the
Isle of Man; if any information could be
given to us as to the cost; and whether I
am right in assuming that that cost, as
far as we are concerned, will re-act on the
Imperial contribution. I do not propose
to oppose the Bill.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I would like to
welcome the Bill and say that I am delighted to see the widows and orphans are
at last to be looked after, because I have
raised the point in this House several
times, and I am glad to see that the
Government have taken an interest in
this matter. I would also like to see the
blind people taken out of the hands of
the county councils and made a State
charge, because I do believe it is time the
Government realised that blind men are
equally entitled to the old-age pension and
supplementary pensions without having
to go before a county board. It should
be a State charge, and I would urge the
Government to consider that point.
Mr. BROWNE: I think the Government are to be commended for introducing this Bill. It is one that should be well
received by the working classes, and
especially by those who will benefit. The
other day I was looking up some reports
of Select Committees set up about 1900 to
consider and report on pension schemes for
aged persons in need, and in the light of
what has subsequently taken place they
certainly make strange reading. One of
the schemes recommended was estimated
to cost about £10,000,000 a year, and yet
some prominent statesmen at that time
belonging to both political parties declared
that the State-I mean the United
Kingdom which then included all Ireland
-could not bear such a heavy financial
burden. Perhaps here I should state, in
view of what was mentioned in another
place, that Mr. Joseph Chamberlain was
one of the first to recommend old-age
pensions.
It was also stated at that time that oldage pensions would not encourage the
exercise of thrift, and might even bring
about evils much greater than those it was
desired to remove. Well, in the whirligig
of time all those doleful predictions have
been falsified. We have now old-age pensions on a liberal scale, while the thrift of
the working classes so far from decreasing
has certainly increased. Indeed, the
accumulated savings of the working
classes are phenomenal, and I can scarcely
believe that thrifty workers will be disposed to slacken their efforts in order to
secure supplementary pensions. After
all they are patriotic citizens interested
in the welfare of the State. Of course
we cannot have much regard for the
spendthrift, or the wasteful person who
has done nothing either to make provision for himself or his family.
As pointed out by the Leader of the
House, wonderful strides have been made
in contributory and non-contributory old
age pensions since the establishment of our
Ulster Parliament, and as this Bill indicates, the extensions and improvements
have not ceased. It would have been
interesting if the Leader of the House had
been able to tell us the total amount of
pensions payable in Northern Ireland
under the different schemes and also the
additional expense involved in consequence
of this Bill. The provision dealing with
blind persons is particularly welcome. We
cannot do too much for those afflicted in
that way. The means test is a debatable
subject, and while I know there are many
cases of hardship, at the same time it is
the duty of sons and daughters who are
in a position to do so to give all available
financial help to their parents. The
Government has dealt with the whole
matter in a very sympathetic and generous
way, and I cordially support. the Bill.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: In reply to
a question asked by the right hon. and
learned Senator (Mr. Leslie), I can say
that the Bill has been passed through the
Parliament in Great Britain, but that it
will not come into force until an appointed
day, which will be later in this year, and
it is the desire of the Government here
that our appointed day shall synchronise
with the appointed day in Great Britain.
The cost of the Bill will, I am sure, be
considerable, but the Government in

£3,700,000 per annum.

QUESTION TO MINISTER.
Smoke from Domestic Chimneys.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major
Dobbs:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether his attention has been called
to the tendency of chimneys in dwelling
houses in Northern Ireland, particularly
those erected by public authorities, to
emit smoke otherwise than through the
recognised orifices; and, if so, are any
investigations being made into this
question, with a view to its amelioration.
a
Major DOBBS: This question seems to
be rather obtusely worded, but to boil it
down to a very simple question it is,
"Can you tell me how to cure a smoking
chimney?" We all know there are
great number of smoking chimneys in the
country, but I never realised until quite
recently how serious it was. I am sure
that my question will have the sympathy
of most of the Members of this House.
Two things came to my notice quite
recently. A man asked me if I could
tell him how to cure a smoking chimney.
He said it did not matter so much before
the war, but since the black-out restrictions came into force he was not allowed
after dark to keep either the window or
the door open, so that it meant that if he
had his window and door shut there was
not enough draught to prevent his
chimney from smoking, and he had either
to be in the light without a fire or be in
the dark with a fire.
the
The other case was that of a lady living
in one of those semi-official institution
cottages. She has the same trouble. She
cannot live in her living-room with a fire
when the wind is in a certain direction,
and unfortunately that is the prevailing
south-west wind. The chimney, I know,
has been heightened, special chimney pots
have been used, and one of the pots had
special louvres in it to regulate
draught. Another time one of those
iron chimney pots which moved round
with the wind was used, but the trouble
has not been cured. I think it is a
terrible thing to think there is this discomfort, because no builder and apparently
no architect can tell you the theory of
chimneys, and how to build a chimney
that will not smoke, or how to cure a
chimney that is smoking.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON : In the
absence of the right hon. Gentleman, the
Leader of the House, I have been asked
to reply. The answer to the first part of
the question is in the negative. With
regard to the remainder of the question
if the hon. Senator will indicate the area
in which dwelling-houses are for the
reasons stated alleged to be defective to
such an extent as to cause a nuisance or
be injurious to health, I will have representations made to the appropriate sanitary authority. I may add that if the
condition of the dwelling-houses to which
he refers does not constitute a nuisance
or is not injurious to health neither the
sanitary authority nor the Government
can intervene.
Mr. LESLIE: Arising out of that, one
would like to ask whether in any schemes
more or less of an extended character
through the rebuilding of houses that
have suffered recently, particular attention will be paid by the Government to
this matter of smoking chimneys. In
Question to Minister
Adjournment
4 AUGUST 1943
large towns the question of smoke is really
a very considerable evil if it is not properly regulated, and, although one individual chimney may escape being
brought into the category of a nuisance,
a number of these chimneys, in none of
which the smoke is properly regulated,
may be a great nuisance. So I would
like the Government to make sure that
in any scheme of rebuilding particular
attention is paid to this matter, particularly in towns.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: While a
certain amount of smoke is due to defective equipment and apparatus, I think
the real problem-I have had a good
deal to do with industrial smoke in the
past is that at least 75 per cent. of
the cause is inefficient stoking. In
industry it can be cured by training
stokers and other people in charge of
plants, but it would be a formidable task
to train all the people in domestic houses
to be efficient stokers. Unfortunately you
cannot make a domestic chimney foolproof. I think you will find that the real
source of the trouble in the domestic cases
to which the hon. Senator has referred is
often unwise selection of fuel, or
unskilful poking of the fire, or shovelling
of fuel into it.
an
Mr. BARCLAY: With regard to the
smoking of chimneys, the deficiency is in
some cases due to the building of the
chimney, and I think it would be advisable for the Government in the future,
now that there is going to be a revival
of house building, to have the advice of
an expert in this respect, so that all
chimneys would be built to a specified
drawing, drawn up by this expert. I am
afraid the cause of a great deal of the
smoking is like it was in the case of the
clergyman's chimney where I live. The
chimney was smoking and would not
draw, and the kitchen was always full of
smoke. The clergyman got a man to
have a look over the range, for the people
of the house blamed the smoking on the
range. When this man had examined
the range he said he could do nothing,
and they would have to do it themselves.
The clergyman's wife asked, "What have
we to do?" and the answer was, "Get the
sweep and have the chimney swept." If
the chimneys were well swept there would
not be so much smoking.
Royal Assent.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I have to
report to the House that his Grace the

name and on behalf of His Majesty the
King, has been pleased to give his assent
to the following Bill agreed upon by both
Houses:
Pensions and Determination of Needs.

opportunities for questions which are
likely to arise in the more or less immediate future, and possibly for suggesting
action in other matters. Therefore, one
rather hopes it may be possible to give
Parliament an opportunity of assembling
at a not too distant date. I do not mean
this week or next week, but one does not
like the idea of both Houses being, so to
speak, discharged from their duties for
too long a period under existing
circumstances.
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: The

Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: Really this practice of
taking the First and Second Readings
together is becoming all too common.
This particular Bill that we are met to
consider is not at all an easy Bill. It
refers to a very complicated code. I
venture to say that of all the Members of
this House a very large proportion do
not know where to find Section 5 of the
Imperial Act of 1920, which is referred to
in the Bill, and in any case I do not
think this is a Bill that should be rushed
I
in any way whatever. For myself,
cannot find it at all possible to agree to
the Motion.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I am very glad
that at last some attention is being paid
to this practice of passing Bills through
this House without discussion. This is
a very important Bill, and I join with
the right hon. and learned Senator in
expressing disapproval at the way Bills
are rushed through this House without
discussion. A number of Bills have been
passed during the past Session and the
previous Session far too speedily. The
First and Second Readings have been
taken on one day, and practically the
next day, without the Members of the
House receiving any notice, the Committee stage and Third Reading have
been taken. The Government should

Committee stage of Bills. I should like

can speak as being perhaps the only Member of this House who has a great deal of
practical experience of the administration

give the House an idea of how difficult it
is to come to a conclusion and a determination on these various Rent Restriction
Acts. I remember talking in my own office
to a solicitor who, like myself, was continually engaged in cases under the Rent
Restriction Acts. He said to me that the
code was so complex, and the sections
dealing with cases where alternative

confusing, that he would never advise any
person on any question under the Rent
Restriction Acts without then and there
referring to the Acts.

complex this code is, not one of them

QUESTION TO MINISTER.
Damage Caused to Fish Life by
Discharge of Flax Water.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie:
To ask the Leader of the House whether
attention has been directed to the serious
loss of fish life which has been and is
occurring in North Antrim owing to the
discharge of flax water, and whether any
action is being taken in the matter.
Mr. LESLIE: The question standing
in my name, as regards the serious
damage done to fish owing to the discharge
of flax water, is, I am afraid, one that is
exercising the minds of those interested
in the subject. A local newspaper circulating in my district states:
The dam-retting of flax brings in its
train each year great destruction of
fish. Hundreds of pounds worth of
salmon and trout have been floating
dead in the River Bush for a week past.
Surely it is not beyond the wit of man
to prevent-especially in war timesuch destruction.
That is one statement. The next one
states:
The destruction of the fish in the
River Bush by flax water continues.
Men have been engaged removing dead
salmon from the river, and no fewer
than 150 were taken out recently, besides large quantities of dead trout.
When it is considered that the spawn
of each fish averages 8,000 eggs, the
food value of the destroyed fish would
run into thousands of pounds. In the
present low state of the river it is
necessary to remove the carcases in
order to prevent further pollution.
I beg to submit that damage of that
description does call for effective action.
It is understood that various attempts at
Rent Restriction Law Bill 218
Question to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
prosecution have been made, but for
some reason or other nothing has been
done. Whether any proposals for prosecutions have been held up by one or
other of the quarters responsible I do
not know. I think it would be sufficient
for me on this occasion to ask the question standing in my name.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Rev.
Professor Corkey): I have been given the
following information by way of answer
to the question which has been raised by
the hon. and learned Senator.
Under the procedure laid down in the
Flax Water (Fisheries) Act, 1941, Boards
of Conservators and the Police are required to notify the Ministry of Commerce and the Ministry of Agriculture
of their intention to apply for the consent
of the Attorney-General to the institution
of a prosecution in respect of a flax water
offence. I understand that the number
of such notices so far received by the
Ministry of Commerce this year is considerably greater than the number received last year, and that many of them
relate to the North Antrim district.
comparing the figures it is necessary, of
course, to bear in mind this year's greatly
increased flax acreage, not only in North
Antrim, but generally throughout the
Province.
In
Whilst the giving of consent to the
institution of a prosecution is a matter
falling entirely to the discretion of the
Attorney-General, both the Ministry of
Commerce and the Ministry of Agriculture are afforded an opportunity of
furnishing their observations on each case
to the Attorney-General, and I am at
liberty to assure the hon. Senator that
neither Ministry will take a lenient view
of any case in which the offence is either
deliberate or due to negligence on the
part of the grower concerned.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I should like to
ask the right hon. Gentleman the Leader
of the House whether it is not a fact
that the opinion is generally accepted
that more fish is destroyed from deleterious matter coming from industrial
works in the various districts of Northern
Ireland than from flax water released by
farmers.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I should
like to ask the Leader of the House
whether in the opinion of the authorities
concerned the procedure under the 1941
Act has proved as effective in preventing
the unnecessary discharge of flax water
as the procedure under the previous Act,
which was very effective for that purpose.
Mr. LESLIE : Arising out of the

the Attorney-General.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I cannot

heard the allegation made in the House
of Commons and elsewhere, but I cannot
say anything definite on the question.
Perhaps I should add that I will call the
attention of the Minister of Agriculture
to the questions raised here to-day.
Rent Restriction Law (Amendment)
Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I am gratified to have this opportunity of making
to this House observations on the Second
Reading of the Bill which I would have
been pleased to make on Tuesday last.
The principle, as far as both parts of the
Bill is concerned, is very simple and intelligible, and I think will be recognised
as generally desirable, whatever may be
said in regard to some of the details and
perhaps also some of the implications of
the Bill. The Bill, as hon. Members will
have recognised, deals with two main
questions. The first problem has reference
to the profiteering in connection with rents
of houses or parts of houses which is taking
place at the present time. As hon. Members know, there is a great temptation for
much profiteering at the moment owing to
the great shortage of housing accommodation. That shortage has been brought
about by circumstances with which we are
all familiar.
All building has been stopped. A great
number of houses in Belfast have been
destroyed by air raids, and in addition to
that people who were evacuated owing to
the air raids in 1941 have steadily drifted
back again into the city. Further,
workers have come not only to this city
but to other industrial centres, drawn by
the offer of work and large wages.
Workers have come from rural areas and
219 Rent Restriction Law Bill-

9 SEPTEMBER 1943
Second Reading
from Eire, and to some extent we have had
evacuees from Great Britain. One fact
may be mentioned in this connection. The
Minister of Home Affairs was responsible
in 1941 for the care of 67,000 evacuees.
Practically all these people have come back
again to their homes. The number in
August, 1943, had fallen to about 10,000.
The figures give some indication of the
extent to which persons have returned to
Belfast.
A start was made in dealing with the
problem by the passing of the Excessive
Rents (Prevention) Act in 1941, but that
Act enabled only those persons who had
lost their homes on account of war damage
to place their grievances before a Rents
Tribunal. The object of the present Bill
is to enable any persons who are aggrieved
by reason of the amount of rent which
they are paying to appear before a Rents
Tribunal, provided the house was let to
them since the outbreak of war.
The personnel necessary for the constitution of
tribunals has already been appointed, and
the machinery for setting into operation
new tribunals as required already exists.
Once the Bill is passed aggrieved persons
will be able forthwith to appear before a
Rents Tribunal.
are
The second part of the Bill is to provide
increased fixity of tenure for people
similarly placed in houses that
subject to the Rent Restriction Acts.
It deals with the question of providing
alternative accommodation in
instances.
certain
Under existing law, if a landlord owned
a dwelling-house before the commencement
of the Rent Restriction Act, 1940, i.e.,
1st May, 1940, and he requires the
dwelling-house for his own occupation he
can obtain possession on giving the tenant
six months' notice to quit, without being
obliged to find alternative accommodation.
If, however, the landlord acquired the
dwelling-house after 1st May, 1940, he
must satisfy the court that alternative
accommodation is available. This position
has inevitably led to great hardships and
anomalies.
The
with
of the Bill is,
purpose
specified exceptions, to require landlords.
to show to the satisfaction of the court
that alternative accommodation is in all
cases available regardless of the time at
which they came into possession of the
house. I come now to the part of the
question which was to some extent before
us on Tuesday last perhaps in an
irregular way. When the Bill was under
discussion in another place certain aspects
of the matter were brought to the attention of the Ministry of Home Affairs, and
the original proposals have been modified
in certain respects. It is proposed to introduce during the Committee stage
amendments to Clause 2 of the Bill
whereby the categories of persons who
need not provide alternative accommodation are to be extended. Hon. Senators
will observe that as the Bill stands at
present, if the landlord requires the
dwelling-house for the occupation of a
person engaged on work necessary for the
proper working of an agricultural holding
the landlord need not provide alternative
accommodation for the tenant.
It is proposed, in Committee, to ask the
Senate to agree to amendments which will
render the provision of alternative accommodation unnecessary in the following
cases, viz., where the tenant was in the
employment of the landlord and the
dwelling house was let to him in consequence of that employment and he has
ceased to be in that employment; or,
where the landlord gave up the occupation
of the dwelling-house in order to join
H.M. Forces or to proceed on work of
national importance. I think hon.
Senators will agree that it would be unfair to deprive such landlords of the right
to resume possession of the dwelling-house
in the cases indicated. I commend the
Bill to the favourable consideration of the
Senate, as it deals with two very important problems. I beg to move.

[Mr. Leslie]
not say that there is no Member of this

Both those propositions are, I think, selfevident. But we have to remember that
in this matter it is our heads that have
to rule our actions. We cannot invariably
allow ourselves to be swayed by our heartfelt sympathies, especially in cases where
there is a risk that when we try to help
one man we may be injuring another.
We have always to keep both sides before
us, and we have, I think, when we come
to legislate to make perfectly sure that we
have heard both sides.
As to
This Bill does not seem to me to give
any indication that the other side has been
heard. Something was said, I believe, in
another place about the intention to
institute an inquiry into the general working of the Rent Restriction Acts.
that I can only say that I submit
that inquiry should precede legislation.
Further, let us look at the Bill itself.
There are two remarks to make-one as
to what the Bill does not do, which I
propose to take first, and the other is what
the Bill proposes to do directly or indirectly. The Bill gives no suggestion,
certainly no promises, of the provision of
further houses. I believe that it is
generally accepted that more houses are
required in Belfast.
The MAYOR OF LONDONDERRY:
And elsewhere.
Mr. LESLIE :
And as the hon.
Senator says elsewhere, I do not suppose there is any objection likely to be
taken to the suggestion that more houses
are required in Belfast. As we know
there is a suggestion that in future replanning there may be more distribution
of factories than there has been in the
past. That affects Belfast to a very
serious extent. It could not fail to have
an influence on the number of houses
required. In any case, for one reason or
another more houses are required at present. There are also, as the Mayor of
Derry indicated, demands arising in other
parts of the Six Counties-perhaps the
city of Derry is one of the most glaring
instances. As to that I cannot speak.
The Bill does not provide any more
houses. It does not do anything towards
the prevention of sub-letting and overcrowding those two concomitant evils
which are at the root of a great deal of
what I may describe as housing dissatisfaction. If there is successive sub-letting
it leads to the creation of a vicious circle.
The shortage in Belfast has been
aggravated, as the Leader of the House
has told us, by the increased number of
people in Belfast. He spoke very fairly
of people who came here to draw higher
wages and he also said that some of these
people were those who were evacuated
during times of stress. He did not explain whether the evacuees left because
their houses were destroyed. Perhaps we
may be told something about that. I do
not think, however, it is material to the
case before us. The Bill does not do
these things.
Let us see for a moment what the Bill
does do indirectly. I say that the Bill
if passed in its present form will seriously
undermine, and still further undermine,
public confidence. The effect will be to
discourage private enterprise from building houses of a certain class. If the
Government contemplate the provision of
a sufficient number of houses in the
future entirely by State or local government enterprise and wish to disregard
such assistance as they can get from
building societies, private capitalists and
so on, then this Bill is taking the very
best way about it. I do not think that
is a proper way to approach the problem.
I am sufficiently old-fashioned to
remember what has been said so often in
this House and other assemblies-that
the cure for Rent Restriction Acts,
which will always be regarded as a transient evil, rests in the provision of more
houses. I think I have seen it stated,
though more across the water than in
this country, that when you come to the
provision of more houses in quantity-it
is thought and generally agreed that more
houses in quantity are wanted not only
throughout Northern Ireland, but elsewhere you want the resources of the
private individual and the company just
as much as State and local government
enterprise.
Then, again, the Bill does not really
seem to me to provide any satisfactory
remedy for the extortionate rents which it
is stated are being asked for.
It is easy
enough to put something on paper and
pass it into law if Parliament allows. You
Rent Restriction Law Bill-
Second Reading
9 SEPTEMBER 1943
can expel nature with a pitchfork, but, as
remarked many years ago, it comes back
again. I think it is beyond the resources
of Parliament to convert the whole world
from the habit of making a bit where they
can. Where they come to the evil side
which is suggested, perhaps more on Sundays than on week-days, you cannot make
people virtuous, or even charitable, by
Act of Parliament.
Passing from that, the Bill is intended
to be retrospective in character. The first
Clause suggests that where a house has
been let since, I think, the 1st of September, 1939, two days before war began, the
tenant of that house shall now be put in
the same position as he would have been
put if he had been a person who, since, I
think, the 1st of April, 1941, had been
bombed out of his house. I think that is
a fair résumé of the effect of the first
Clause. That is to say, whether a man
has been bombed out or not, he is to have
the privileges, such as they are, of a man
who has been bombed out.
One of the arrangements made for the
bombed man was that he might take his
case to a newly constituted court, a
tribunal, and the suggestion now is that
for two years before that time cases are
to be dragged before a tribunal if the
tenants so desire. That is to say, that the
arrangement between the landlord and the
tenant, which was entered into in accordance with the law as it then stood, is to be
raked up and reinvestigated. That is a
position which, I think, we cannot stand
over. If you pass that, it means that no
contract is safe. You cannot put money
into building houses, because if you do
you cannot tell what might happen a year
or two after you build the houses and pay
the bill. That is one reason why I say
that public confidence is being undermined.
I am afraid I must add that the mere
fact that such a proposal has been made is
not conducive to confidence in the present
Government as a satisfactory vehicle for
post-war reconstruction. If you are not
going to have regard to contracts, where
are you going? Are you going to build
on Socialism, or whatever form of spoliation you suggest, or are you going to stand
on the old rule, which the House will
remember is protected by the Government
of Ireland Act? Are you going to have
regard to private rights, or are you not?
This new court is, as I say, a newfangled thing created for a temporary purpose-purely to deal with bombed houses
-and it is now sought to extend it wider.
I think that, possibly, in view of the
difficulties which now exist it might be
possible to do something with this Bill if
Clause 1 could be converted from the
retrospective to a purely prospective one,
just as was done with the Excessive Rents
Act of 1941, which was generally known
as the Bombing Act. Something might
be done if you followed those lines, but it
is not done in the Bill before us.

Something might also be done with
Clause 2, that is the Clause in regard to
which amendments were adumbrated in
the speech moving the Second Reading.
Clause 2 proposes a further extension of
the limitation on private property.
say that war time is not the time to bring
in a highly controversial proposal of that
kind. I do not see how this House can
properly accept the Bill with Clause 2 in
it.
There is one particular passage in
Clause 2 which, I am sorry to say, has
not made its first appearance, that is
allowing a subsequent court to revise the
order of another court on the lines that
the first court would have so proceeded
had the law been other than it was when
the first order was made. We have done
something like that before, but I do not
see any reason why we should do it again.
Two wrongs do not make one right. In
war time we should be especially careful
lest in doing one thing for someone's
benefit we may do something else which
will be an injury to another man—a man
who may be on foreign service, and so on.
Something might, I think, have been done
on the lines of a very drastic amendment
of those two Clauses, but this Bill does
not lend itself to an amendment of that
drastic character.

From
duced this Bill in order to strike at those

out of their houses, but in doing so they

my own knowledge I believe that in
Belfast and in some of the larger towns
it exists, but it is mainly confined to
Belfast. During the time the military
occupation was at its height and a large
number of houses had been destroyed by
enemy action, there was an attempt on
the part of certain landlords to overcharge tenants. That, of course, is no
reason why the scope of this Bill should
be taken back to 1939 when there was no
military occupation and the Germans had
not bombed any houses-a time when war
industries had hardly started to function.
During the course of house-hunting in
Northern Ireland-I had to do it in the
course of my military duties-I found
a large number of empty houses which I
filled very effectively as it was my duty to
do, and that was one of the reasons for
the scarcity of houses.
Before we pass this Bill we should hear
a clear statement from the Minister of
Home Affairs as to the amount or extent
of the abuses of the privileges of a landlord to contract with his tenant. The
Minister of Home Affairs will appreciate
what I mean when I say, as he has done
so often in the past, I have no doubt,
that hard cases make bad law. This is
a very good example of that. There are
some people who, having been brought
up in a hard school, having made their
money during a time of intense competition when no one showed them any
quarter and when there were no Acts of
Parliament to protect them if a trade
rival ruined them-there are people of
that sort who believe that it is right and
just for anyone who has something to sell
to sell it at the highest price
he can get. That, I am glad to say, is
not the attitude of the majority, or even
a large minority, of property owners in
this country. They appreciate that there
are times when it is their duty and their
privilege to forego some of the profits
which they might otherwise receive.
think that is the attitude of the majority
of the landlords in this country.
I
If by this Bill you were going to strike

houses empty for reasons that are purely
capricious I would support the measure

instead of that the Bill will strike at the
just as well as the unjust. Every tenant

landlord. There seems to be no reason
for a prima facie case to be made out,
because all a tenant has to say is that he

some of the tenants I have seen in houses

property or houses: This Bill has been

So far as this Bill is concerned, not a

and not a house will be repaired. It will
not relieve the congestion that has already
been referred to. What I would suggest

demand will come into operation again
and there will not be any necessity for a

if houses which could be repaired were
repaired by the Government, provision.
would be made for cases such as those

duty of the Government not to bring in
a Rent Restrictions Bill but to bring in
a Bill whereby house congestion such as
this would be remedied, and the people.
supplied with a house where at least they
will get a roof over their heads.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I think this
Bill is like so much other rather hasty
war-time legislation. It puts every
Member of this House in a very great
difficulty. I have the most heartfelt
sympathy with its object, which is to prevent extortion, but I have the most profound dislike for the methods adopted to
carry it out. I have also a possibly
unworthy suspicion that it is in some
degree a smoke screen. The Government,
for some reason or another, are reluctant
to make every effort possible towards the
repair of houses, as the hon. Senator
opposite has just stated. But they feel
they must do something about this housing
business, so they have another whack at
the poor old landlord-always a very
popular thing to do. However, as I say,
this Bill puts one in a very great diffi
culty. I do feel that in so far as there
are exorbitant rents being charged as a

should be done, and for that reason I am
very reluctant to oppose-in fact, I shall
not oppose the Second Reading of the

I should like at this point to say how

this will be a precedent for the Minister
responsible for Bills appearing in this
House more frequently. In saying this,

nurse the extremely unattractive offspring
of other people. To come back to the
first Clause of the Bill, there is one point
I would like to put forward merely as a

Affairs. You are tackling admittedly a
very dangerous thing under pressure of
war circumstances. You are tampering
with the sanctity of contracts. In other
words, you are putting an axe to a very
noble tree and you are not sure how much
harm you are going to do. If you are
doing that, I think you should see-if
we may vary the metaphor-that you pull
your punches a little; in other words, put
every safeguard you can around it.
Would it not be a possible and wise safeguard to say, "We are giving the tenant
leave to go to a tribunal if he is dissatisfied with his bargain. We will also
give the landlord leave to go, too?" I

jealous of the sanctity of contracts than I
am, and anyone who has been associated
with the law all his life appreciates the
value of the law. But as I understand
the law, a valid contract is one entered
into by the parties on equal terms. Where
a man is turned out of his house and
home, as has unfortunately happened, he
has to get a roof for his wife and family,
and his hands are tied. He has to accept
whatever terms an unconscionable landlord may put before him. In my opinion.
that is not a contract with any sanctity
in it at all.
With regard to the argument used by
Senator Perceval-Maxwell,
that speculative solicitors may adopt this Bill, when
it becomes an Act, as a kind of refuge,
I must say that I cannot see the possibility of that occurring. If the hon. and.
gallant Senator had to carry out the duties
of a solicitor under these Rent Restriction
Acts and wait all day in the courts at the
remuneration provided by these Acts, I
think he would see that there was absolutely no substance in his argument.
One would think that the decision
regarding eviction rested with the speculative solicitor instead of with the
tribunal. The tribunal is the body that
will examine and determine the contract.
I have always had and expressed the
view that when a Government take office
they take upon themselves very serious
and onerous duties of trusteeship, and
surely one of the first duties of any
Government is to see that the people over
whom they rule are suitably, comfortably
and reasonably housed. I mention that
because of the fact that in my opinion—
and I think in the opinion of the House
-the housing question has become such
a tremendous embarrassment and difficulty
in Northern Ireland that local authorities
no longer discharge their duties to
house the people in reasonable comfort.
Therefore I respectfully suggest that it is
the bounden duty of the Government to
at once take upon themselves the task of
providing houses for the people.

[Mr. McAllister]
not exist in the city of Belfast, where

not for 999 years, not for ever, but for
61 or 91 years. Year by year the asset

It is the duty of the Government to do
that not only from a Christian and social
point of view, but in the interests of the
health of the people, and for the benefit
of the State. If the Government desire
to furnish evidence of their bona-fides
let them not hide behind the suggestion of
a shortage of material, because they can
take immediate steps to get hold of building ground, map out lands, plan drains,
sewage, and fences, and have everything
in order so that when the war is over
they will be in a position to provide
houses for the people over whom they
have jurisdiction.
The LORD MAYOR OF BELFAST:
I rise to say that I intend to support this

rents. Rents are restricted to-day. I
should have liked to have seen
a Bill
brought in under which the price of

The price of houses to-day is out of all
proportion. People are giving extraordinary prices simply because they want
accommodation. They are simply forced
to buy houses to live in. As regards overcrowding and sub-letting, the Bill does
not touch those matters at all. One of
the greatest evils is the case of people
who take a house and then sub-let it out
as rooms or flats. I should have liked to
have seen a Bill brought in to put a
check on those people.
As I have said, this Bill does not touch
rebuilding at all. That is a tremendous
problem, and undoubtedly it will have to
be faced. There is no doubt the Government will have to step in and do it,
because no private individual or corporation can build houses to-day. Apart
altogether from the shortage of materials
and the restriction as to the amount one
can spend on rebuilding or repairing a
house, there is the high cost of building.
Private enterprise can certainly
undertake it. Then again you have to
take sites into consideration. Belfast has
been mentioned, and it is really the most
important place in the Province.

241 Rent Restriction Law Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Barclay]
no harm if it does not do any good.
Personally, I have not the least doubt

like to cite a case I know of. It relates
to the larger subsidy house. At the time
the house was built the Government gave
£100 and the local authority £50. That
was £150. Then when the house came
to be sold it was disposed of at £400.
That was £550 for a house that was put
up for £100. Now that house was occupied for 13 years, and the man who
bought it died. It was put on the market
again and sold for £550. The man who
bought it only occupied it for a short
time and he put it on the market again.
It was then sold for £750. You see the
way the thing goes on. That house was
built practically by the ratepayers and
taxpayers.
There are certain classes of
people making capital out of this sort of
thing and something should be done in
that respect. The man who bought that
house at £750 has two houses-one in the
district in which I live and another down
below Larne. It is not fair that a man
should have two houses when so many
people are looking for houses. I think
the Government should put a stop to the
like of that.
There is another case in the same
district. A woman there has two houses
-one in Greenisland and the other in
Belfast. She lives part time in Belfast
and part time in Greenisland. The cases
I have quoted are not the only two. There
are scores of people who have two houses
and are preventing other people from
getting houses to live in. Then I would

become empty will not be let, but are put

sold. Very few working men have £500

I would like to see something in the Bill

much pleasure in supporting the Second
Reading of the Bill.

The MINISTER OF HOME AFFAIRS
(Mr. Lowry): Before I endeavour to clear
away some misconceptions as to the scope,
object and effect of this Bill, may I pause
for a moment to express my deep appreciation of the mostly kindly and courteous
reception I have received from the right
hon, and hon. Members of this House? On
Thursday last I was much touched by the
kind expressions that I heard from
Members of the House, and I am also
touched by what I have heard to-day. I
am forcibly reminded of what Edward
Hyde, First Earl of Clarendon, said when
he was writing in exile his story of the
great rebellion, and was lamenting from
his point of view the condition of his
country. You may remember he said:
"I wonder when the old good
humour, the old good temper, and the
old good manners will return to this
unhappy country?”
No one coming here as I have done--
I am a stranger to your House and not
one of its Members-could make any such
complaint, because the old good humour,
the old good temper, and the old good
manners of this country are exemplified
in your honourable House, and, consequently, while fair criticism has been
advanced and information has been
sought, it has been sought in such
a manner that it is a great pleasure for
me to endeavour to give it. Let me
thank once again your Lordship and the
Members of this honourable House.
In the first instance, I should like to
clear away some misconceptions, and I
shall then return to the two main objects
of the Bill. Before discussing the two
main objects which are (1) to put an end
to profiteering on the part of people who
are not what I would call bona fide
landlords, and (2) to remove anomalies
in the existing law with regard to the
question of providing alternative accommodation, may I say that the object was
not a Housing Bill in the sense of providing additional houses, although in a way
it would have that result as I shall explain in a moment? Further, it is wrong
to think that the Government have done
nothing with regard to this housing
acute
problem since it became so
account of enemy action. There does
seem to be an idea that the cataclysm
which overtook Belfast in April and May,
1941, has not been dealt with.
on
I should like to give the House some
figures on this point. In the city of
Belfast alone 56,662 houses were hit. Of
those 3,200 were completely demolished,
and you can see some of the sites for yourselves as you journey to and from this
House. There are 3,200 houses gone
completely. The badly damaged houses
numbered 3,962, and the damaged 49,500.
If you tot those figures together you will
find they give you the number I gave in
the first instance-56,662. So far from
there being any supineness or delay in
dealing with those cases, the House will
be pleased to hear that the Belfast Corporation alone, acting for and on behalf
of the War Damage Commission, has repaired the latest figures have been
given to me by the right hon. the Lord
Mayor since I entered this Chamber-
45,244. Some thousands more have been
repaired by private owners or their
agents.
The badly damaged houses come into a
different category, although a very large
proportion of those have been repaired.
Owing to the insistent pressure of the
Corporation and my Ministry, and also
with the goodwill of the Ministry of
Finance, who represent the War Damage
Commission, those badly damaged houses
have been re-examined and a list has been
drawn up of 227 that are presently under
consideration. But more striking still is
the fact that the Corporation have spent
-the money was, of course, provided by
the War Damage Commission-no less a
sum than £1,970,000, almost £2,000,000,

who have the interests of the bona fide

Bill. It is aimed against what I might

value of the accommodation provided.
These are the people who are struck by
the Bill, because, if the House considers
it, seeing that practically all houses up
to a valuation of £75 a year are controlled, a really bona fide landlord cannot take advantage of a shortage of
houses. A complaint that I understand
has been made by the hon. Senator from
Derry and the hon. and gallant Member

chance and no consideration, while everybody knows that everything he has to
pay out has gone up tremendously,

aimed at the extortioner; it is not aimed
at the landlord, because, even if he were
bad enough to do it-and I do not think

Acts prevent him from taking any such

are
Therefore the suggestion that the

not venture to interfere with the existing
code so far as permitted increases
concerned. That is outside the scope of
the Bill, which is very narrow in its scope,
which is, firstly, to put an end to extortion, and, secondly, to deal with this
matter of alternative accommodation.
As it stood, you had a very strange
anomaly in this matter. The last Rent
Act that was passed by the Parliament of
Northern Ireland was in 1940, and it came
into force from 1st of May of that year.
The position is that where a man was a

landlord before that date he could get

There is another complaint, too, which is
very common. Somebody in the same

the Act of 1940, whereas a neighbour
cannot be touched. That naturally leads
to discontent, and considering the shortage of houses and that building practically
stopped after the outbreak of war, it does
seem reasonable and I commend this
consideration to this House-that a house
acquired after 1939 and a house acquired
immediately before that date should be
in exactly the same position.

would like to correct a misapprehension

This Bill does not go back, with regard

If the right hon. and learned Senator will

Act of 1941 he will find that the people
who are now going to be protected are not
being put in any superior position to
those who were bombed out, because,
when the Act is invoked, it is only for
the preceding twelve months that the
remedy offered by this Bill will operate,
namely the recovery of the excessive rent
that has been paid during the twelve
months immediately prior to the institution of proceedings. So the Bill does not,
in that respect, do exactly what the right
hon. and learned Senator first thought on
what I am sure was a hasty perusal of the
Bill.
There is just one other matter that was
referred to, and that is that this Bill may
open the door to speculative litigation.
The hon. and gallant Senator who spoke
secondly seems to have some apprehension
on that account. As I think he observed
himself, there is no provision for costs
either in this Bill or in the original Bill,
the Excessive Rents (Prevention) Act,
1941, SO there is not that inducement, although I can assure the hon. and
gallant Senator that solicitors do not
yield to that temptation with any great
alacrity. I think that before any man
gets out of the office and before the law
is invoked there is something in hand.
(Laughter.) Besides, I do think, and I
have heard one of the greatest of Irish
judges saying it, that but for the speculative attorney very often a poor man could
not get his dues. But I do not think
this is a real or likely danger or one that
should deter this House from doing its
best to put an end to the two matters
which we consider are abuses.
There is another matter that has been
raised, and that is that we may be interfering with the sanctity of contract.

Rent Restriction Law Bill-

THE SENATE
[Mr. Lowry]
think it is very late in the day to begin
to talk about the sanctity of contract,
because while the right hon. and learned
Senator might have said that with some
degree of novelty about the year 1860, I
do not think that in the year 1943 it
carries the same weight as it once did.
Be that as it may, contract pre-supposes
equality on both sides, and it cannot be
said that as regards the great evil dealt
with by this Bill there is anything like
equality on both sides. In any event,
this Bill is for a very limited period, and
by the time the proper solution of our
housing difficulties begins to get under
way, namely, the provision of houses,
whatever danger has been threatening the
sanctity of contract will have gradually
disappeared. Therefore I doubt if that
argument should deter this House from
placing us in a position to deal with two
considerable evils.
notice, if I may respectfully say so,
that a certain degree of latitude is permitted by your Lordship in dealing with
There are two matters
some matters.
that I think are foremost in the minds of
Members of this House. One is the provision of houses, and the other is the need
for revision of the existing rent restriction
code. I feel that I am here very largely
for the purpose of giving information,
and, without repeating exactly what I
said elsewhere, I may say that it is the
intention of the Government to set up a
strong Commission, presided over by a
competent chairman, to investigate fully
the effects of the rent restriction code; in
fact I am engaged in the preliminary
steps for this purpose. The rent restriction code started as a temporary measure
in 1915, and it has now lasted 28 years.
It was a temporary measure, but 28 years
seems a long time for a temporary
measure to remain in force. As time went
on attempts were made to improve the
code by tinkering with it here and there,
but the Government feel that the time
has now come for a thorough and new
examination of this code with a view to
seeing that as far as is humanly possible
there may be fair play between landlord
and tenant. It does seem absurd that
rents should be fixed with regard to the
standard prevailing in August, 1914, that
repairs should be charged at such and
such a figure, and so forth.
It is the intention that the examination of these matters should be made
thorough and minute and impartial, and
that the personnel of the Commission
should be thoroughly representative of
every possible interest involved. It is
desirable that particular attention should
be paid to this aspect of the question,
namely, to what extent, if any, this restrictive code of rents has affected what I
would hope would be the natural action
of private enterprise so that it may come
to the aid of the State, whether through
the central government or the local
authorities, with regard to the provision
of houses. I think an examination of
that sort should commend itself to the
Members of this House, and with that
announcement all I can do is to suggest
to the Members of this House that what
they heard from the right hon. Gentleman, the Leader of the House, in moving
the Second Reading is worthy of their
very careful consideration. With renewed
thanks to your Lordship and to this House
House I will sit down.

Question to Minister.
RELIGIOUS DENOMINATION OF
TEACHERS IN A BELFAST SCHOOL.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Joseph
Cunningham:—
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he is aware that 301 children
out of a total of 307 attending St.
Joseph's School, York Road, Belfast,
are being taught by Roman Catholic.
teachers, and will he arrange for these
children to be taught and religious instruction given by Protestant teachers.
I
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
think that if the question which I have
put down on the Paper is examined it will
be found to be interesting. Our people
are continually being represented or misrepresented as being bigoted and intolerant-
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Who are your.
people?
At
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM-by
our political opponents, but the facts contained in the question certainly convey
something different and are really a contradiction of those accusations. The
question probably is not very clear.
this particular school there are 146 pupils
on the roll in the boys' school, of whom
141 are Protestants, and there are 161
pupils on the roll in the girls' school of
whom 160 are Protestants. That means
there are five Roman Catholic boy pupils
and one Roman Catholic girl pupil..
would almost appear that the Roman
Catholic parents had taken their children
away from the school for fear they should
be contaminated by the large number of
Protestants in the school.
It
The real cause of parents having to
send their children to this school is lack
of accommodation in this district. The
Grove School, which is a new school and
quite convenient, has been taken over by
the Admiralty, and therefore cannot be
used as a school. The other schools are
some distance away and so the parents
have to send their children to this school.
It is more convenient, and, I suppose,
safer because it avoids crossing the road.
Some years ago there were quite a
number of accidents there. In my
opinion the question I have put is quite
reasonable, that Protestant teachers
should be appointed to this school so that
they can teach the children.
It is very important that they should.
be teachers who would be capable of
giving these children proper religious instruction. If this cannot be done, some
arrangement should be made with the
Roman Catholic Church. Surely they
would not have any objection to letting
the school to the Protestant people or
even to selling it to them. We had a
case not very long ago in Belfast of a
certain district ceasing to be a Protestant
area. The Roman Catholic Church
bought a Protestant Church in that
district and turned it over for religious
purposes for themselves. If that can be
done with a church something surely can
be arranged for a school. I would be
very glad if the Leader of the House was
able to give us some satisfaction, because
there is a feeling among the people in
this district that if something could be
done-of course, another school cannot
be erected now for the accommodation of
these pupils to utilise this particular
building and have it so arranged that
Protestant teachers should be appointed
for the teaching of the children everything would be satisfactory. I would be
very glad if the Leader of the House
could give us some promise in this
direction.
Mr. LYNCH: I know a little about
this school. It was built for Roman
Catholic children. It was the only school
in that neighbourhood for many years,
and children of different religious persuasions went to it. Owing to unfortunate
circumstances that have arisen in York
Street during the past number of years
the Catholics in that area left, through
persuasion or for some other cause, and I
have no reason to doubt that the figures
given by the hon. Senator are correct.

different denominations had free access to

The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Rev.
Professor Corkey): The facts are as
stated in the question, and they have
been repeated very fairly by the hon.
Senator who has just sat down. The
position in this school is as stated in the
question. The power of appointing
teachers to a voluntary school, such as-
St. Joseph's School, rests with the
manager, under the provisions of Section
66 of the Education Act (Northern Ireland), 1923, and the Ministry has no
power to require that the
the teachers
appointed by him shall be of any
particular religious denomination.
The manager, however, is obliged by
the Ministry's regulations to allow those
clergymen or teachers or other persons to
whom the parents do not object to have
access to the children in the school for
the purpose of giving them religious or
Bible instruction there during the time
or times set apart in the time table for
such instruction. The Ministry is informed that this obligation has been and
is being discharged to the full extent to
which application for such access has been
made.
In regard to the possibilities suggested
by the hon. Senator who asked the question, I have no information, and so I am
not in a position to make any further
statement at the present time with regard
to those possibilities.

authority?

my knowledge goes it is entirely the
responsibility of the local education
authority.

CLAUSE 1 (Extension of excessive
rents jurisdiction).
(1) The right to apply to a Rents
Tribunal, and the powers of such a
tribunal to determine whether a rent is
fair and reasonable, shall not be restricted to the cases mentioned in subsection (1) of section one of the Excessive Rents (Prevention) Act (Northern
Ireland), 1941 (in this Act referred to
as "the principal Act"); and, accordingly, the principal Act shall have effect
subject to the amendments made by the
following provisions of this section.
(2) The following sub-section shall be
substituted for sub-section (1) of section
one of the principal Act:-
"(1) Subject to the provisions of
this sub-section, where a dwellinghouse has been let after the first day
of September, nineteen hundred and
thirty-nine, and the person to whom
it has been let (in this section referred to as 'the tenant') is aggrieved
by reason of the amount of the rent
which he is paying or has contracted
to pay in respect of the dwellinghouse to the person from whom he has
taken it (in this section referred to
as 'the landlord'), the tenant may
apply to one of the Rents Tribunals
constituted under this Act (in this
Act referred to as 'the tribunal'), in
accordance with rules made by the
tribunal under the Schedule to this
Act, to determine whether the rent
payable by the tenant is fair and
reasonable having regard to all the
circumstances of the case including
the poor law valuation (if any) of the
dwelling-house; and the tribunal,
after giving to all persons who appear
Committee

to it to be interested an opportunity
of being heard, shall determine the
question:
Provided that this sub-section shall
not apply in any case where the
dwelling-house is one to which the
Rent Restrictions Acts apply and the
standard rent of the dwelling-house
is, for the purposes of those Acts,
calculated by reference to a date
before the second day of September,
nineteen hundred and thirty-nine.'

261 Rent Restriction Law Bill-

THE SENATE
Committee
[Major Perceval-Maxwell]
on the Second Reading of the Bill. I
am convinced that, although the Bill is
a good Bill in principle, a considerable
amount of amendment is required in detail to make it really mean what it says.
The right hon. Gentleman when replying
to the last Amendment said he thought
that the word landlord "" was an inappropriate title for the lessor in this
case, and I agree with him. There are
obvious reasons for that, and I think the
better way of putting this landlord
matter right is to pass this Amendment
which I am putting before the Committee.
There are two theories about Acts of
Parliament. One is that it does not
matter how obscure it may be if it can
be intelligently construed by those who
are experts in construing Acts of Parliament. The other theory, and one which
I hold, is that whenever possible an Act
of Parliament should say what it means
in clear language so that the ordinary
person can understand it. After all, Acts
of Parliament are as much a part of
literature
as The Times" or the
""

It (the Bill) is aimed against those

Later on he said:
It is aimed against what I might call
the creation of tenements, and the
extortion of rents far in excess of the

These are the people who are struck
by the Bill, because, if the House
considers it, seeing that practically all
houses up to a valuation of £75 a year
are controlled, a really bona fide landlord cannot take advantage of a
shortage of houses.-(Official Report,
14th September, 1943, Vol. 26, col.
246.)
In the original principal Act a house
is quite rightly defined as being a whole
dwelling-house or any part let as a
separate dwelling-house. My Amendment simply re-defines what, in fact, the
Bill is aiming at.

view of the learned and gallant Senator

a committee to inquire into the matter,

was an Act such as the one of 1897 which

always used to look to a continuation of
that Act with no amendments. We all

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, on page
3, line 2, to leave out the words " thirtynine (nineteen hundred and thirtynine) and insert the words " forty-three."
This is an Amendment which is not a
drafting Amendment; it is used in a
more principal matter. I do not wish to
detain the Committee longer than is
necessary, and particularly I do not wish,
if I can help it, to say what other people
said on the occasion of the Second Reading. I may say briefly that the object
of the Amendment is to transform the
new sub-clause which it is proposed to
add to the principal Act, the Excessive
Rents Prevention Act, 1941. I want to
transform it from the retrospective to the
prospective. That seemed to me to be
the general feeling of this House when it
considered the Second Reading. That is
a matter that I really ought not to raise
at the moment.
Let us look for a minute at what the
Bill proposes to do. May I digress to say
that the Clause I am discussing is not a
rent restriction Clause. It has nothing
to do with the Rent Restriction Act. The
Act has something to do with the Clause,
but the Clause, as such, is not a matter
of rent restriction; it is a matter of protection, so to speak. This Clause goes
far beyond anything that has been done
under rent restriction procedure before.
It deals with houses over £75 valuation.
They come in. The Committee will remember that the 1940 Act limited itself
to a house of a valuation under £75.
There is no such limitation here. This
Clause will deal with a house whether
furnished, unfurnished, or how much the
rent. It proposes to establish a new
court, or rather what they call a
tribunal. That tribunal was established
in 1941 to deal with special cases of unfortunate people who had been bombed
out of their houses and it was alleged
that a large number of unscrupulous
persons took advantage of them in order
to extract personal profit. We let that
Bill go through. We find now, although
two years have passed, that there have
been only some twenty-five cases heard
by the Tribunal, so that the evil of bullying the bombed people could not have
been so bad as we were given to understand.
Then when we dealt with that we dealt
with the misfortunes of the people
bombed and we stopped there. Before
one could get any relief under the 1941
Act one had to show that one's house had
been bombed and become uninhabitable.
If we had thought then that the problem.
of overcrowding was so very acute there
was nothing to stop us dealing with the
matter then as we propose to do now,
but we did not do it. Parliament is
bound to ask what the excuse is for interfering now. We are interfering now.
If the Bill is allowed to go through as it
is there will be interference not only with
the sanctity of the contract which,
whether it be before 1860 or after, is still
