269 Rent Restriction Law Bill-

THE SENATE
Committee
[Major Perceval-Maxwell]
If it goes out to the people that any succeeding Government can inquire into contracts which have been executed or partly
executed and make people pay over what
some Government official or some unknown tribunal decides is in excess of the
amount that should have been paid then
nobody will ever be willing to risk his
money in the ordinary commercial course
of business again.
The right hon. Gentleman rather
stressed the point that since 1860 contracts
have been constantly interfered with. The
right hon. Gentleman did not have as
much to do with the law of property as I
have had. I do know that the greatest
difficulty was experienced over this question of contracts. I believe the view
commonly held in business circles, and
quite rightly held, is if you take away
from a person something which is his by
a right legally exercised you should compensate him for the loss of that right,
otherwise you are confiscating something.
We have always prevented any Government, and I hope always will, from confiscating anything from somebody else
without paying compensation.
an
In these days there are many things
judged to be contrary to public policy.
If there are I would prevent them from
being committed. To go back on
executed contract is like trying to unscramble an egg. As was pointed out the
person to whom the money was paid may
not now have the money. He may have
committed himself to some obligation on
the ground that he had more money. He
may have increased his life insurance: He
may have done something that he cannot
get out of. It seems to me if you are
going back for a number of years on what
people have done and are going to make
them pay for it, although what they did
was perfectly legal you raise all sorts of
difficult questions. Are you going to
release the persons penalised from all sorts
of contracts entered into owing to their
increase of income? There is no end to
the number of transactions you would
have to overhaul.
So I have to support this Amendment
because it is a point of principle, not a
drafting amendment. It is a principle
that cannot be overriden, unless the Government say clearly and explicitly that
something is being done that is dangerous
to the interests of the State. The Government have not done so, although they referred to some secret report that they
have received and that has not been laid
on the Table of the House which compels
them to take this action. Until they make
this report public I regret to say I cannot
accept their judgment of the report they
have mentioned. The right hon. Gentleman the Minister of Home Affairs has
said the whole question of rent restriction
is being examined. I think it would be
very advisable for him not to deal with
this matter hastily, but to withdraw this
Clause until such time as an examination
has taken place over a wide area, and we
know the result of rent restriction on the
alleged excessive rent charged by other
people. Until we know that we cannot
act. Any time Parliament acts blindly in
the dark it generally regrets it.

Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I
beg to move, in page 3, line 15, to leave
out all the words after the word "including " (including the
poor law
valuation) to the end of the line, and
insert the words "the rent which the
landlord pays to any other person in
respect of, and the cost of renovation and
repairs to."
This is really rather more than a mere
drafting amendment. The tribunal is required to consider what is fair and what is
reasonable, and if the words were left
there the tribunal would have before it
all the material necessary to make a fair
decision. The Government then suggest
that the poor law valuation, if any, of
the dwelling-house should be a matter for
the tribunal to consider. I am completely at a loss to understand why, when
the tribunal has already been told to
consider everything put before it to
arrive at a reasonable solution, the
Government should come along and make
the tribunal consider something which
can be of practically no value at all in
coming to that decision. There are many
things which they might consider, one of
which is the rent which is being paid to
the head landlord, and another the cost
of repairs.
Of course, when you are letting part of
a house, perhaps one room or two rooms,
to ask the tribunal to take into account
the rateable valuation of the whole house
is submitting something which has no
bearing whatever on the whole situation.
A house is not rated according to the
separate rooms, but according to the whole
house, and for those who are particularly
anxious and we all are to make sure
that no one pays an exorbitant rent, I
put forward the suggestion that this particular provision may result in a very
extortionate rent being charged, because
someone who has given up his kitchen and
his best bedroom may claim that the
tenant is paying the valuation of the
whole house, and I cannot see how the
tribunal can fail to agree to that.
I think that that will result in very
great hardship to the very people whom.
we in this House are trying to protect. I
cannot understand why the Government
put in this provision. If they do not like
my Amendment, which is merely intended to suggest to them that they
should omit these particular words and
put in some words which are appropriate,
such as the cost of repairs, which have
gone up considerably since the war began,
and want to leave out the words mentioned, I quite agree. If you stop after
the words, "having regard to all the circumstances of the case,'
"that is quite
enough, and gives the tribunal everything
it wants to know. The tribunal should
judge the case according to the evidence,
which is all that it can do.
There is a particular danger in putting
in special words, because if words are inserted in an Act of Parliament there is a
presumption on the part of judgesadmittedly not a very fair one-that Parliament meant something when they put
in those words, whereas Parliament very
often meant very little, but, in actual
fact, the court will undoubtedly try to
give a special meaning to those words.
The judges will say, in effect, "If we had
been left to decide the matter for ourselves we would not take into account the
rateable valuation, but since Parliament
has expressly told us to do so we must
take it into account whether it is fair on
the person making the complaint or not."
Therefore, I beg to move.
The MAYOR OF LONDONDERRY:
I should like to appeal to the Government
to allow these few words to go in in fairness to the persons responsible for the
maintenance of houses. The cost of
repairs is very considerable, and I think
they should be considered. I would ask
the Government to try and give some
consideration to the landlord. He is
going to suffer, not the tenant.

will do any harm, and I agree with my

valuation does not matter one way or
another.
Mr. LOWRY: I greatly regret that it
is not possible to accept this Amendment.
First of all, let me say that the valuation
is a factor in taking these matters into
account. As Members of the Committee
will be well aware, in fixing the valuation
there is a very rough rule about the
valuation being four-fifths of a fair rent.
It is a good and ready guide in arriving
275 Rent Restriction Law Bill-

21 SEPTEMBER 1943
Committee
at what is a fair rent. Instead of
the rent being only one-fifth greater
than the valuation it is something like
four hundred times the valuation, and
sometimes more. Although I dislike
quoting evidence not laid on the table of
the House, I can give one instance where
the valuation amounted to £63 and the
rent to £429. The valuation has a value
in indicating a fair rent. With regard to
the cost of renovation and repairs, I am
not sure if I am doing the hon. and
gallant Senator an injustice in regard to
the Amendment with which we are dealing. Are we dealing with the rent payable to the landlord or to the cost of
renovations and repairs, or do they all go
together?
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: The
two are together.
Mr. LOWRY: I think the inclusion
of the poor law valuation is a factor
that is a guide to begin with. Now
as to the rent which the landlord
pays, I think that is sufficiently covered in,
"having regard to all the circumstances."
With regard to the amount spent
on repairs, with the indulgence of Mr.
Speaker, I previously announced the
appointment of a Commission which is
going to consider this question of the rent
restriction code, and I assured one of your
hon. Members that that was going to be
done with all possible speed. To drag in
this question of repairs would, I think, be
a very unsatisfactory way to approach this
very big question. This is a temporary
Bill restricted in its scope and dealing
with a very small aspect of this question.
I do suggest that an examination by a
thoroughly representative Commission,
representative of all interests, which will
go into the changes that have occurred
during the past 20 years-the cost of repairs and so forth, will in the long run be
a more satisfactory way of dealing with
this vast and complicated question than to
endeavour, in a purely temporary measure
confined to one small matter, to cure it
by bringing in the question of repairs.
Let me reiterate this; that the people
who are profiting by this system at the
present time are not people who are doing
repairs or anything else. I think there is
still confusion between the legitimate
landlord and the man who has turned
himself into a landlord ad hoc, and who
takes advantage of the needs of people who
have suffered through purely war-time
conditions. By that I mean active wartime conditions, not merely a state of war,
but conditions that have arisen directly or
perhaps indirectly through direct enemy
action on this city. Consequently the
Government cannot accept this Amendment.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I cannot help

could mention dozens of instances within

Mr. LOWRY: This sub-section was for
convenience sake re-cast entirely. It does
not alter in the slightest degree the principle of the Bill as it left another place,
but it was thought more convenient to
have under the one Clause the law as it
then stood under the Act of 1940. The
only substantial addition that is made is
this. If the Committee will look at paragraph (a) of sub-clause (3) it says:-
where the tenant was in the employment
of the landlord or a former landlord,
and the dwelling-house was let to him
in consequence of that employment and
he has ceased to be in that employment;
or
That was the law as it was down to
1940, and in the Bill as presented else-
Rent Restriction Law Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
•
[Mr. Lowry]
where only the position of a man working
on a farm, an agricultural worker, was
preserved. After consideration, and representations of various sorts, it seemed
to the Government that the law as it
existed up to 1940 should be preserved,
because that man has his house by virtue
of his employment. Perhaps he himself
wants to leave to better his employment,
or his employer is not satisfied with him,
and I think it is quite impossible that that
man should retain his house until he is
provided with another. Great anomalies
and great inconveniences would be produced in that fashion.
It
For my own part, in looking over the
draft of the Bill I confess that the only
things I had clear to my mind were the
things of which I had direct knowledge,
for instance the case of the ploughman or
cattleman who had a house and perhaps
he and his master did not get on.
would be quite impossible that he should
still retain the house when either party,
master or man, was dissatisfied. I think
that experience has shown during all these
years of restrictions that this Clause has
worked well enough, and that it would be
a danger to change it.
The other additions are made in (c) and
(d). There is, first, the case of what I
may call the Service landlord who has
given up his house to serve in one of the
armed Forces. It would be a great hardship when he returns that he should not
have his house back. Of course I quite
see that hardship may fall the
upon person who is in occupation of the house at
the time. Down to 1940 in regard to
houses with valuations up to £26 this
person has been protected, and this
Clause only extends the protection up to
£75. Seeing the changes that have taken
place in the value of money, I think it
reasonable that that protection should be
carried on. The Service tenant, if I may
term him so, is protected by the Act of
1940 repeating the provision of the Act
of 1920. The protection is up to £26 at
the present time, and I should think it
I would be the wish of this Committee that
protection should be extended to all other
Service landlords who want to resume
possession of their houses.
The other category that was added was
this:
Where the court is satisfied that the
landlord, during the period of the present emergency, gave up possession of
the dwelling-house in consequence of
his having undertaken work certified by
the Ministry of Labour to be work of
national importance.
These are some cases-I do not know
how numerous they may be of people
who went away to do work of national
importance, such as civil servants, or
people who went away to engage in the
production of munitions or something of
that sort, and they have let their houses.
Perhaps the occasion may arise through
the exigencies of their service that they
have to return home. In such circumstances, if the Ministry of Labour
certify that the work was of national importance, although its hazards may not be
comparable with service in any one of
the armed Forces, I think this is an
exception which might reasonably be
grafted on to this matter when the
obligation of furnishing alternative accommodation arises. Except that this Clause
sets forth in one Clause without legislation by reference to other clauses what
is contained in those clauses, those are the
only distinct alterations.
Two of those Amendments were foreshadowed, namely (c) and (d), that is
those with reference to Service tenants
and tenants engaged on work of
national importance, and the restoration of the law as it existed in the case
of a man who has a house which he enjoys
as part of his terms of employment; in
other words, he is to be in the same
position as an agricultural worker. I
think the Amendment makes the proposal
of the Bill much clearer, and that it
extends a benefit to two very deserving
classes, namely, the Service tenants and
thoes who have gone away to engage in
work of national importance.
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I
entirely sympathise with the Government.
on this point, but I do think that they
want to think again. What do they mean
when they talk about men engaged in
work necessary for war purposes? From
the point of view of war work, it is very
invidious to label certain work as being
war work and certain work as not being
war work. When a person leaves his
house to perform any particular class of
work, how is he to know whether the
responsible Government official after the
war will say that his work is war work
or not?

The MAYOR OF LONDONDERRY:

Major PERCEVAL
MAXWELL:
How does he know that he is going to

whether his work is going to be considered
as of national importance or not. Then
there is another case which I think will
open up a certain amount of hardship.
Many of these men and women who have
come into Belfast to take up these rooms
are themselves war workers. Then you will
have the case of one war worker trying
to turn out another war worker. I
think the Government are landing themselves almost into a nest of wasps, and
that it would be very much better for
them to leave out this Amendment with
the exception of the part that deals with
the people who are actually serving in
the armed Forces of the Crown, and
to allow me to insert my innocent
little Amendment which is next on
the list and which would give
the tribunal complete discretion to decide
in each case whether there was hardship
or not. I do not oppose the Government's
Amendment; I merely warn them that
they may land themselves in a very serious
lot of complications over it. I think that
this Amendment will seriously affect the
mobility of labour. If a man is to go to
another part of the country to engage in
war work he will want a certificate before
he leaves his house that the work is of
national importance. He will not want
to wait until the war is over.

Mr. LOWRY: In order to reassure the
hon. and learned Senator who spoke first
on this question, the matter which causes
him some apprehension is the certificate
of the Ministry of Labour that the work
is work of national importance. At the
present time there is a very large category
of works so defined under various Defence
of the Realm Regulations, and the
Ministry of Labour are constantly giving
certificates and declaring what is work of
national importance and what is not. I
do not think that there is the slightest
difficulty. It is almost as easy to define
and recognise as service in one of the
armed Forces. Therefore there need not
be that apprehension which I understand
the hon. and learned Senator entertains.
As I have said, it is almost a commonplace
at the present time.

Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL:
beg to move in sub-clause (1) (a) at end
of line ("the tenant and his family'')
insert "or."
This is a very innocent Amendment and
one which I am sure the Government will
accept. This Bill was very hastily drafted.
It passed through another place with great
speed. There was very little said in
favour of or against it in that place, but
there was one Member who said he would
like to see the word "or" inserted in this
sub-section. He did not explain why. I
believe that the right hon. Gentleman has
actually admitted that the Government
would be sympathetic with this word
"or."
I put the Amendment forward
now partly to enable the Government to
consider the matter publicly and state
their views publicly on it, and partly because I think the Government would be
the last body of men to say they are infallible.
In the course of this Bill going through
reference was made to the inequities that
might arise in the case of Service men and
men doing work of national importance.
I would suggest to the right hon. Gentleman that there are other cases of hardship
which would arise if a landlord was not
able to take up a house or part of a house
for his own purpose or that of his family.
There is the case of the woman about to
have an infant. One might easily imagine
cases of extreme ill-health or old age. My
Amendment simply gives the tribunal
power in every case to say, "This is a case
of hardship; we are giving our ruling in
accordance with the circumstances of the
case." The Minister has thought of two
cases. I suggest with great humility that
the right hon. Gentleman is not infallible
and that other cases of equal hardship may
arise in the future, and that my Amendment will enable the tribunal to deal with
them as they occur. Many of them cannot be foreseen.

Mr. LOWRY: The Government does
ask for the re-insertion of the consideration, among all the circumstances that are
to be considered, of the poor-law valuation. The fact is the hon. learned and
gallant Senator brought forward the
Amendment in order that the tribunal
should take into account rents payable
and moneys spent on renovation and repairs. These are circumstances, and, as
I think was fairly pointed out, having
mentioned one circumstance, namely,
poor-law valuation there was no reason
why the other should not be mentioned.
Of course if one begins to try to set down
in an Act of Parliament all the circumstances that should be taken into consideration the matter would be almost
interminable.
For my own part I am very ready to
admit this, that over two years ago when
I agreed, believing that it made not very
much difference, that poor-law valuation.
should be taken into account as a circumstance I was not prepared to start
enumerating all the circumstances that
may be taken into consideration. But I
do suggest with great respect to the
House that when we have agreed to and
will accept readily the hon. learned and
gallant Senator's Amendment in this
matter I would ask the House still to
keep in this other circumstance which
has stood the test of two years and three
months. To enumerate all the circumstances that ought to be taken into consideration I think would be a work
without end. But two having been
mentioned that are outstanding-I do
think poor-law valuation to be an outstanding circumstance too-I would ask
the House to assent to this addition to the
hon, and gallant Senator's suggestion.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: After the
statement made by my learned Friend,

move that the Amendment be accepted.
Major PERCEVAL MAXWELL:
First of all may I thank the right hon.
-
Gentleman the Leader of the House for
so courteously informing me by letter before I saw the Order Paper that he was
to move this particular Amendment. I
must correct the right hon. Gentleman the
Minister of Home Affairs. He says the
Government accepted the Amendment
which I moved. I think the Government
were very reluctant to accept it. They
were forced. I think that in this case I
cannot do better than to leave their
Amendment to the same tribunal. I
would hesitate to say on behalf of the
House, being merely a Member, that I
could accept or reject anything you put
before this House. In fact having moved
the Amendment leaving out the words
which are now proposed I feel I cannot
take any part in the vote on this matter.
I must leave it to the discretion of the
House.
As regards the other remarks of the
right hon. Gentleman that the words of
my Amendment were really unnecessary I
do not agree with him. If hon. Senators
will look at the Bill they will see that the
reference is to the circumstances of the
case. The right hon. Gentleman no
doubt accidentally left out the words "of
the case" when he was talking just now.
I think these words make a lot of difference. What is the case? The case is
that a certain tenant has been charged too
much rent. There is nothing about
repairs in the case. There is nothing
about the rent the tenant may be paying.
These circumstances of the case should be
brought into account. Some persons may
think it would be going outside the terms
of the section to consider extraneous
matters which may be important, but not
actually circumstances of the case.
For that reason I think these words
should be inserted to make sure there
should be no risk of relevant circumstances being omitted. I see no
objection whatever to the words which
the right hon. Gentleman wishes to insert
being inserted as well, although I do not
like these words any more than I did last
time. I think they can do no harm. I
am quite sure they will do no good. I
leave the matter entirely in the hands of
the House.
Mr. LESLIE: I confess I am in some
difficulty about this matter, too. What
the Government is asking is, in effect, that
the House should go back on a deliberate
decision taken by the Committee against
THE SENATE
Rent Restriction Law Bill—
[Mr. Leslie]
The
the opposition of the Government.
Government objected to certain words
being added and these words being taken
out. The Committee insisted
on the
words being taken out and words added.
Now the Government comes back more or
less, I should say, cap in hand, and entreats us to reinsert the words taken out.
The words relate to poor-law valuation,
which has really not much to do with it.
I am inclined to think that is correct.
Poor-law valuation is a poor basis for
rent, but rent is a good basis for poor-law
valuation. I think it is that way rather
than the other. On the other hand, it is
fair to remind the House that the words
poor-law valuation do occur in the Clause
for which this Clause is to be substituted.
It therefore would seem to be a very open
question whether the House should agree
with the Government in asking the Committee to go back on a plain and successfully asserted decision, or whether they
should not. Like my hon., gallant and
learned Friend opposite, I do not feel disposed to divide the House on the subject,
but I should listen with interest to what
other people have to say. The Government are asking the House to go back on
a decision deliberately taken. That is not
usual.
Amendment agreed to.
CLAUSE 2 (Amendment of Rent
Restrictions Acts as to provision of
alternative accommodation).
*

*
*

(4) Where, in relation to any dwelling-house to which the Rent Restrictions
Acts apply, any order or judgment has
been made or given by a court before
the commencement of this Act, but has
not been executed, and, in the opinion
of the court, the order or judgment
would not have been made or given if
this section had been in operation at
the time when the order or judgment
was made or given, the court may, on
application by the tenant, rescind or
vary the order or judgment in such
manner as the court thinks fit for the
purpose of giving effect to this section.

not. It seems to me that those who

me
I have no doubt that the right hon.
Gentleman the Minister of Home Affairs
will put forward arguments in favour of
this Clause, and that he will be assisted
in doing so by the remarks made on the
last occasion. I should like to add one or
two more which have occurred to
since. It is a very common practice for
tenants to accept the terms of their
tenancy, if it is right, on the conditions
which they have signed for, then to leave
their house and find another house. If
the landlord is allowed by Act of Parliament to take up his house they may go
out.
reason
There must have been no
why any of those tenants should have
taken the case to court at all, but there
are some tenants who are simply out to
give as much trouble to the landlord as
they can. They are willing to accept all
he has to give them, but they are not
willing to accept any of the obligations
which go with the benefits. The tenants
are willing to sue the landlord if he slips
up on any of his conditions, but they are
not willing to conform to their own conditions.
I think there are very few of those
tenants, but there are some, and I am a
little bit anxious about this particular
sub-clause of the Bill, because it seems to
me that it is inclined to confirm the
action of such tenants, and encourage
them, and give them a benefit by their
action, whereas the good tenant who recognises his obligation without forcing
the landlord to go to the expense of an
action will get off without any benefit at
all. He has had to find a new house for
himself and will get nothing out of this
Bill. It seems to me that the Government have gone out of their way to give
their sanction and approval to an action
which, I think, is really dishonest.
am not for a moment suggesting that the
Government are dishonest; I think they
have done this accidentally..
I
I believe
that the action of a tenant who deliberately forces a landlord to go into
court when he has not got any case is
definitely dishonest; he is trying to get
something for nothing, and is doing it at
the expense of his landlord.
•
What do the Government propose to
do for the landlord--when I say landlord
he is probably a tenant as well—who has
been forced to take the case into court in
order to get possession of the house for
himself or one of his children? He has
gone to a certain amount of costs, and it
is improbable that he will get any of
those costs out of the tenant. If the
Government are going to take away the
benefit of his action, the landlord having
got possession has to give it up simply
because he was fairly decent and allowed
the tenant time to find another house, or
because the magistrate thought it was a
case in which the tenant should be allowed
time to get another house. The landlord is not going to get a penny of his
costs back. The right hon. Gentleman
shakes his head. We shall hear in a
minute. I am going through this piece
by piece, so as to give him plenty of
opportunity to say that I am wrong, if
I am wrong.
It would seem to me that every time
you pass legislation which is moved by the
heart rather than by the head in order
to obviate and put right certain hard
cases, you create far more hard cases or
potential hard cases than you did before.
I am not at all sure that we should not
send the Bill back to another place for
reconsideration of this very difficult and
contentious question because in the other
place when this Bill was passed it was
passed extraordinarily quickly; in fact, I
should say it was rushed through. I
have no doubt there were reasons for that
and I would be far from criticising the
fact that it was rushed through. We in
this House have not had the benefit of the
collected wisdom we are accustomed to
have from the other place to guide us in
our deliberations. I must confess that I
should like this Bill to go back to another
place with the sub-clause excluded in order
to hear what might be said there. What
should be my action when the Bill should
come back to this House I should not like
to sav now. I reserve that for further
consideration.
I do feel in this particular case that
there is a possibility of this Clause doing
great injustice. I cannot see why when a
court of law has thought it right to give
possession to the owner of a house-I say
owner of a house because he is not really
a landlord, he may be a tenant-the
Government should say in every case that

Mr. LOWRY: May I say at the start
that the Government are not prejudiced
in favour of any colour of eyes, least of
all those whose eyes are somewhat light
coloured, and especially those whose eyes
are blue, even though they are dark blue?
This is an Amendment which we are bound
to resist. First of all, may I point out to
the House that on four separate occasions
in the Imperial Parliament this particular
Clause in this code has been approved.
Twice it has been approved in our own
Parliament here, and I think that the
House would take somewhat of a risk if
on the seventh time they should depart
from what had been found to be good six
times. I have not had experience of .
this form of legislation, but in other
forms of legislation the county court
judge very often says that it is a great
pity, but if the law had been so and so
a month earlier the litigant's condition
would have been different. If this subclause were deleted and supposing there
were still more delay in securing the
Royal Assent there would be further cases
coming before the court for consideration
under existing legislation and tenants
may be affected who if the Bill had become law could not be affected without
the provision of alternative accommodation.
This is a standard Clause, and it has
stood the test of 27 years. I do think if
this House were to depart from what has
been looked to be good for 27 years, it
would be a far more dangerous precedent
than the one suggested by the hon. and
gallant Senator. Consequently, I would
ask the Committee, and especially the
right hon. and learned Senator, to trust his
innate beliefs in what has been for a long
time a prescriptive right and not insist
on dividing the Committee on this matter.
True it is, as the hon. and gallant Senator
behind me says, there was a considerable
degree of haste accompanying the introduction of this legislation. There was
only one person in another place, who did
not really believe much in what he was
saying, knowing full well that this is
almost a common form. I do not think it
would be right to send the Bill back to
another place with this great change made
in the Code, remembering always that
this is but a temporary measure.
There are two matters I might mention.
First of all, the judge who is asked to take
a second thought on this matter is not,
after all, a beginner on these questions.
There is one matter on which I should like
to set at rest the mind of the hon. and
gallant Senator-that is the subject of
costs.
The court may, on application by the
tenant, rescind or vary the order or
judgment in such manner as the court
thinks fit.
The matter of costs is well covered.
Nobody can come along to work, if I may
use the phrase in the precincts of this
House, a quick one. There is always the
subject of costs, which will keep him
in bounds.
Mr. LESLIE: I may say that I am
not in the least impressed by the argument used. Because another Parliament
has been wrong six times is no reason
why this Committee should join in doing
wrong the seventh time. Nor do I agree
with the proposal which is to give the
unsuccessful litigant the spoils of victory
as a consolation for defeat. That is what
it comes to. I can see nothing to justify
doing a thing which may or may not work
injustice under these Acts. I am sorry
I cannot possibly withdraw the Amendment. As the Committee know, I object
to retrospection of any kind, and I do
object to a judge-it may not be the same
judge who heard the case first-pronounc
ing judgment on the assumption that the
law when the previous judgment was
pronounced was something different. We
must take the law as it comes. We must
not interfere with the due course of the
law. I cannot withdraw the Amendment.

Tuesday, 5th October, 1943.

Ministry of Education:

QUESTION TO MINISTER.
Banning of "Irish Freedom."
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Lynch:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state why the periodical
"Irish Freedom," which is printed and
published in London and has a circulation in Great Britain, the United States
and the Colonies, is banned in Northern Ireland, and whether the British
Censor no longer exercises jurisdiction
in Northern Ireland.
Mr. LYNCH: This paper has been
publishing short articles on gerrymandering in the Six Counties, on the penalising
of Catholic ex-Service men, and on the
failure of the Northern Government to
build any cottages under the Labourers
Acts since 1921. This must be the real
reason for the paper being banned in this
area, because it is permitted to circulate
in England. The Northern Government
has prohibited the circulation of the paper
in the Six Counties because it contained the
articles I have referred to. Its criticism
of the treatment of the minority in this
area by the Northern Government is
probably another reason why it has
been banned. This periodical is published in support of the war effort, and
contains nothing seditious against the
British Government. It is a very childish
and petty act on the part of the Northern Government to prevent its circulation in Northern Ireland, especially in
view of the recent statement of the
British Minister of Information that the
freedom of the Press was something that
should not be interfered with, and that
criticism was always helpful. I shall
await with interest the reply of the Leader
of the House to my question.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE
(Rev. Professor Corkey): The following
is the answer given to me regarding the
banning of this particular periodical:-
The circulation of the periodical referred
to is prohibited in Northern Ireland under
the authority of an Order made under
Regulation 26 contained in the Schedule.
to the Civil Authorities (Special Powers)
Acts (Northern Ireland), 1922 and 1933.
The Order at present in force was made
by the civil authority on the 18th
December, 1942, and is operative until the
31st December next, when the list of
banned publications will be reviewed.
The British censor does not exercise
any functions in Northern Ireland under
the Civil Authorities (Special Powers)
Acts.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
1, sub-clause (2) to leave out the words
"September, nineteen hundred and
thirty-nine" in lines 1 and 2 of page 3,
and insert instead thereof the words,
"April, nineteen hundred and forty-one."
At this somewhat late stage in the
proceedings of this somewhat ill-starred
Bill I do not propose to make a lengthy
speech, and I certainly do not propose to
go back on anything that has been said or
done in this House before. All I am
endeavouring to do is to elicit from the
Government, if I can, whether they are
prepared to make the date of the operation of this new Statute to be the same
date as that which was applied to what
we call the Bomb Act; that is the excessive Rents Protection Act of 1941.
will be remembered that we have hitherto
discussed the question of retrospective as
opposed to prospective legislation, and the
House has decided on retrospection. With
that, at all events, I do not propose to
quarrel, but I have not yet heard any
reason, valid or otherwise, for making the
date for the new Bill still further retrospective.
It
I suggest that it would be in accordance
with the feeling of the House that the
date should be assimilated to the date of
the Bomb Act; that is to say, to alter the
date September, 1939, to the date April,
1941. Whether the alteration will have
any serious effect in preventing the evils
anticipated I do not know, but it will
have some effect. It will not give the Bill
an attraction to the man residing in an
over-rented hen coop, as it was described;
it will take away one object of his
remaining there; and it will perhaps tend
to prevent people rushing in to occupy
these hen coops. It will also prevent any
undue congestion in the tribunal which
may or may not be called upon to decide
this matter. I need not detain the House
any longer, but I will simply move the
Amendment on the Paper, which is to
take out the date intended and to substitute a new date.
Question proposed, "That those words
be left out.'
The MINISTER OF HOME AFFAIRS
(Mr. Lowry): This is an Amendment
which the Government cannot accept. The
House will recollect the two matters that
are aimed at in this Bill. I agree with
what the right hon. and learned Member
has said regarding the date inserted in
what is called the Bomb Act of 1941,
but there is another matter aimed at in
this Bill. That is the case of the house
that has been let for the first time since
1st September, 1939. I think I remember that on the Second Reading debate
on this Bill, Senator McLaughlin pointed
out that anyone who had let a house for
the first time, thereby creating a standard
rent, between 1st September, 1939, and
1st April, 1941, has nothing whatever to
fear if he fixed a fair rent. But there
are cases where, subsequent to September,
1939, after all building had ceased, a man
let a house. for the first time and did not
act in a fair manner. It is impossible to
get an exact figure as to the number of
these houses, but that there are some is
undoubtedly a fact, and the effect of the
amendment would be to remove any protection whatsoever from the person who
is paying what is an exorbitant rent
compared with the standard rent fixed at
a much earlier period.
It might be said that perhaps the
proper remedy was to enable those whose
rents were fixed as far back as August,
1914, to get up to within a sort of hailing
distance of the others,
others, instead of
penalising them. I should imagine the
House would not take that point of view
about the matter, but rather that a man
who has taken advantage of the terrible
shortage of houses, and the demand for
them, to charge an exorbitant rent should
be put in exactly the same position as
someone who has let single rooms at the
prices we know are prevalent in this city.
For these reasons the Government cannot
accept the amendment.

Mr. LOWRY: I should like to clear
up
one misapprehension. This Bill will not
necessarily apply to every house. The
right hon. and learned Gentleman attributes to me the suggestion that every
tenant of a house let for the first time
after 1st September, 1939, will go to
court to have his rent reduced. That is
a very wrong assumption. If a tenant
gets a house at a reasonable rent he will
be content to pay that rent and have a
roof over his head. This Clause is aimed
at a very limited class those who have
extorted extravagant rents. It is
purely war measure, and it may be noted
that it will have to come before Parliament for renewal at the end of 1944.
The war may not be over by then, but
the Bill is not an invitation to everybody
who has become the tenant of a house for
the first time since 1st September, 1939,
to have recourse to this tribunal. Those
who have acted fairly towards tenants
need have no apprehension, and why
should those who have acted unfairlyand I hope they are not numerous-be
placed in a so much better position than
the man who is restricted by numerous
Acts of Parliament starting over 30 years
ago. I think the fairness of this Bill
ought to commend itself to the Members
of this House.

The people concerned with the linen

many of them have mills, yards, and even

built and intended for the occupation of

say that owing to the serious depression
in the linen industry of late years production had gone down by 30 per cent.,
and is only now recovering itself, and
that owing to that depression they let
those houses, chiefly on request, to almost
anybody they could. With the increased
work falling to the linen industry now,
largely through the war, and with the
work which it is hoped will fall to the
industry in increasing volume after the
declaration of peace, they find they are
already beginning to be hampered by inability to retain possession of their own
cottages, and they ask this House to consider the advisability of putting them in
the same position as the farmer.

produced by agriculture, and a very

Mr. LOWRY: This matter, which the
right hon. and learned Gentleman has
raised, has already been brought to my
attention. On the last day this House
met I had the benefit of a lengthy conversation with a Member of this House,
who is very largely engaged in this industry, the hon. and gallant Senator
from County Tyrone, who I am sorry.
to say is not present to-day. I am sure
the House will agree that no gentleman
could have a better knowledge of the way
in which mill property ought to be
treated. Another hon. Senator, who is
also engaged in the same activity, supported his views. The difficulty which
has arisen is this: It was also brought to
my attention by a representative body of
the Mill Owners' Association, who took
counsel's opinion on the matter and discussed it with me.
It would seem that this difficulty has
arisen, perhaps, in the circumstances
referred to by the right hon. and learned
Senator. There might be a mill worker
in a cottage who dies. His widow or
some other members of his family continue to live in the house, because there
is no demand for it. In those circumstances I do not think the mill owner
ought to change his mind, and that was
the view of the hon. Senators to whom I
have referred. If the cottage had been
let in the ordinary way, it should have
been treated as though it was an ordinary
dwelling-house, and that mill owner
should be under the onus of providing
alternative accommodation if he seeks to
resume possession.
The original insertion of paragraph (a)
was intended to cover every case of mill
worker, and to enable the mill owner who
was the landlord to get possession. Say
a foreman or other key worker left. It
would be as necessary to get possession of
that cottage to install a similar man as it
would be in the case, of a farmer replacing
his ploughman. I do submit that where
a house was let by a mill owner because
it could not be otherwise availed of, he
should not be allowed to change his mind
and dispossess someone, when, if he had
been far-seeing and had hoped and prayed
for a revival of the linen industry, he
would not have taken such a step. I do
not think there can be many people. in
mill cottages who are not actively engaged
in the production of linen. We already
Rent Restriction Law Bill
Adjournment
THE SENATE
[Mr. Lowry]
have four categories of persons exempted
from the obligation to provide alternative.
accommodation that have been inserted in
the Bill since it came to this House,
namely, the ordinary case of the man in
employment, which was really designed
to cover mill workers; the agricultural
worker; the person who gave up his house
for service in the armed Forces of the
Crown; and those who are engaged in

to those categories the process would be
endless, because I am sure there, is not a
Member of this House who could not
think of at least one class that might be
inserted in these exemptions and, instead
of having these four, we might exhaust
the alphabet. The Government cannot
accept the amendment.

very carefully borne in mind in the

importance to the war effort.

QUESTIONS TO MINISTER.
Disposal of Grain Crop.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Maguire'
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the Minister of Agriculture
has instructed farmers to offer their
crop of oats, barley, wheat, etc., to a
licensed merchant within seven days of
threshing, and in the event of the
a
merchant failing to purchase the crop,
the crop must be offered within
further seven days to the Ministry
of Agriculture; and whether he can
state what provision, if any, he has
made for the feeding of horses and
other livestock belonging to the farmer.
Mr. MAGUIRE: Farmers have. been
sent forms on which to make a return
of all grain threshed this year.
The
notice sent to them says they must submit their grain to a licensed merchant
within seven days of threshing. If the
licensed merchant refuses to accept the
grain, then within a further seven days
the farmer must offer it to the Ministry
of Agriculture. There is going to be
great hardship caused to the farmers,
who are also going to be put to a great
deal of trouble. if they are made to comply
with the terms of this Order. The
eircular says:
YOU ARE REQUIRED:
(a) To furnish immediately to the Ministry, on the enclosed form BD.8
the information asked for thereon
about the threshing of your grain
crops.
(b) To supply to the owner or operator
of the threshing tackle which threshes
your crops such particulars as he
requires to enable him to complete
the returns he has to make to the
Ministry.
YOU MAY ONLY DISPOSE OF YOUR THRESHED
GRAIN AS FOLLOWS:
(a) You may offer it for sale to an
Approved Buyer or a Licensed Seed
Merchant within seven days after
threshing, or
(b) If it is not accepted within seven
days of your offer you may offer it
within a further seven days (i.e.,
within 21 days of threshing) to the
Port Area Grain Committee, 93 Ann
Street, Belfast.
(c) TAILINGS: You may use for feeding
to your livestock the tailings from a
threshing of your grain so long as
such tailings do not exceed 5 per
cent. (one-twentieth) of the total
weight of grain threshed (i.e., head
corn and tailings together).
Five per cent. is a very small percentage. Some of the farmers get their
grain threshed by contract, but a great
many farmers thresh their own corn, and
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. Maguire]
they thresh it only as required in order
to have the straw fresh. They may
thresh a stack once a week or once a fortnight, spread over the winter period. If
farmers are to be required to make
weekly returns for threshing a stack, and
they have to do that throughout the
winter, it will impose great hardship on
the farmers who, at the present time,
have sufficient work to do to save the
harvest. It has been a very difficult
season; in fact, the harvest is not yet
saved in all parts of the country. This
will mean that endless returns will have
to be made by the farmers, who are
already overworked by the number of
forms required to be filled in and returned. These forms should be simplified,
because most of the farmers are not in a
position to make the detailed returns required by the Ministry. You have to
make monthly returns of potatoes, stock,
sales, what you have put into feeding and
what you have retained for seed, and
those particulars have to be recorded
monthly. If you keep a bull or a boar
you have to keep records of every service,
the colour of the cow that was served by
your bull, as well as the age of the cow
served. That applies throughout the
farming industry.
Many other farmers get a portion of
their corn threshed by contractors in
order to give them grain for feeding
stuffs at an early period. How are these
people going to make returns? They will
have to make a partial return repeatedly.
If the farmer is not to be allowed to
retain a good portion of his own grain
for feeding stock he will find himself in
a difficulty. There is no ration allowed
for the feeding of farm horses or for
fattening stock, or for feeding stirks for
beef, or for pig feeding. If all their
grain is to be taken away from them
what position will the farmer be in to
raise beef and cattle, pigs and poultry.
It must inevitably entail a reduction of
stock. If it is the intention of the Ministry of Agriculture to purchase all the
grain produced by farmers in Northern
Ireland, and then to sell it back to the
farmers, it is a very unwise policy, because it will entail several handlings, and
the cost of transport will be enormous.
Another point in which I think the
Ministry are a little behind is this.
I
think the Ministry should let the farmers
know what their future policy is going to
be and give them timely notice, because
this matter of the disposal of grain was
sprung on the farmers. This circular to
which I have referred was received by the
farmers only during the past week, and
they had no prior notice of it at all. A
great many of the farmers retain their
grain in order to fatten cattle and pigs,
because otherwise they could not have
carried the stock they have been carrying
since the war started. Under one system
of farming that has been carried on in
Northern Ireland farmers retain all their
produce and, as the saying is, they walk
it off the farm. In other words, they
feed it to stock and sell the stock in the
form of meat.
I myself am one of those who are guilty
of that practice. It is the system I
adopted in pre-war days, and it is the
system I have carried on as far as possible during the war period. We farmers
feel that the Ministry of Agriculture
should be more precise in their instructions to farmers and facilitate them as
much as possible, because these returns
that have to be made are getting very
complicated. I think something should
be done by the Ministry to ease the position, and I appeal to them to take some
steps in the matter.
Major DOBBS: I am sure the farmers
of Northern Ireland will be very grateful
to the hon. Senator for asking this question. Farmers do not know what they are
allowed to do and what they are not
allowed to do about this question of grain.
I was present at a committee meeting of
farmers about three weeks ago when three
views were expressed as to what they
could and could not do. All three views
were different. I did not say anything,
but I disagreed and agreed with
all three views. We have a lot of
information, but we do not know
what we are allowed to do. Last
year we were not allowed to feed oats
except they were seconds. We were not
allowed to sell seconds. On the 28th
September I received Form BD.5.
the top it says "Disposal of your 1943
Crops of Wheat, Barley, Rye, and Millable Dredge Corn." Below it says in
very heavy print:
At
You may not dispose of threshed
grain in any other way whatever (even
for sowing). . .
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
26 OCTOBER 1943
Oats is grain. There is no question
about that. Is oats included in this Order
or is it not? We do not know.
Last
winter, if you sowed wheat, oats, barley,
dredge corn, or rye for use as seed you
had to get an undertaking from whoever
you sold it to. Oats was definitely not
allowed to be sold except, of course, to
an authorised buyer. You could not sell
it to a neighbouring farmer unless you
had this special undertaking, and you
certainly could not sell it as oats for
feeding. Therefore I would like to ask
the Deputy Leader of the House this
definite question-can oats be sold to
another farmer for feeding?
Senator Maguire asked what he could
do with regard to feeding his horses and
livestock. Since I saw that question on
the Paper I went very carefully into the
subject. I am not going to say in this
House how it can be done. I could not
do it because it would not be fair, but
there are certainly four ways in which you
can use your oats or other grain for feeding purposes, although it is distinctly
against any proper use of your grain. I
hope the right hon. Gentleman will be
able to answer the questions I have asked.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: In the unavoidable absence of the right hon.
Gentleman the Leader of the House I
have been asked to reply to the question.
Farmers who intend to sell barley and
wheat are required by the respective
Cereal Orders to offer the grain to an
approved buyer or to a licensed seed
merchant within seven days of threshing,
and if the offer is refused they are required to offer the grain to the Northern
Ireland Port Area Grain Committee,
which acts on behalf of the Ministry of
Food. Wheat and barley of millable
quality may not be fed to livestock. These
arrangements do not apply to oats which
farmers, if they so desire, may retain for
feeding their horses or other livestock. In
addition to their home-grown feeding
stuffs, owners of certain priority classes of
livestock receive supplies from the
Ministry of Agriculture through the
Livestock Rationing Scheme.
Wheat and barley are required for the
dilution of flour, and farmers must therefore sell their crop to the Ministry. Oats
may be retained by the farmer and used
for any purpose, including the feeding of
livestock. In reply to the hon. and
gallant Senator who asked if oats could
be sold to other farmers, the. answer is
yes.
Major DOBBS: I hope publicity will
be given to that reply, because, as I have
said, three weeks ago we were talking
about this question and there. were three
different views expressed, based on what
had happened the year before. Therefore, I hope it will be put out to the
farmers generally that they need not sell
their oats to the Ministry, but they can
use them or sell them. Up to now the
position has not been clear to the farmers
of Northern Ireland, and it should be
made perfectly clear to them.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: This question is.
a very important one not only to the
public generally, but to the farmers.
There has been considerable misunderstanding with regard to this circular
issued by the Ministry of Agriculture,
and I am very glad to learn that oats
are not included in it. When I read the
circular I thought it did not include
oats, and I am glad to have that opinion
confirmed. I would like to ask, who is
to judge the quality of millable wheat or
barley? If the farmer wants to retain
any of that crop for himself, what has
he to do? I must say I found the Ministry very reasonable in regard to this
circular. I had a small quantity of
wheat I had threshed, and I was not sure
about the position. In the circular the
Ministry stated that a farmer might get
a licence to have a portion of wheat, not
exceeding 10, hundredweight, milled for
use as human food in his own household.
Within a short time I had applied for a
licence, and practically by return of post
I had that licence. That shows the
Ministry are reasonable if one understands the circular.
was
mis-
This particular circular
leading, but the answers given to-day
clear the air to a great extent, and they
let the farmer know where he is. I
agree that at the present time the farmer
has too many regulations to obey, and he
has not the, time to fill in the many forms
he is required to return to the Ministry.
He considers the filling in of these forms
worse work than he has to do in the fields,
because he is not used to forms.
I agree
with the hon. and gallant Senator, when
he suggested that the answer given to-day
to this question should get as much publicity as possible.

Mr. McLAUGHLIN: It reminds me

oats to judge it. I was so angry that I

Transport of Fruit and Vegetables.

whether consideration has been, is being
or will be given to the possibility of

Channel markets.

questions that would not be put except on

the Order Paper, a particularly busy
afternoon. In fact, I am afraid the
House may regard the question as not
only being somewhat nebulous in itself,
but dealing with a nebulous question.
The point is, of course, that during
the past few months a certain amount of
attention in Northern Ireland has been
directed to the possibility of establishing
a post-war trade in such fruit and vegetables as it is possible to grow in Northern Ireland. În considering those points
the question has been raised as to the
feasibility of transport by air, and as to
the possibility of growing the things and
then getting them to the English market
in such a condition as will induce people
to buy them. The whole thing is, as I
say, a little nebulous, but the point does
seem worthy of attention. Therefore I
ask the question as to whether anything
has been, is being, or is about to be done.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: As the right
hon. and learned Senator is aware, a
series of Committees is considering postwar planning in all its aspects, including
agriculture and transport. The terms of
reference of at least two of these Committees cover the matter raised by the
right hon. and learned Senator. I think,
therefore, it may be taken that the practicability of the suggestion made by him.
will be thoroughly investigated.

Voluntary Registration-
Women
THE SENATE
THE SENATE.

Wednesday, 3rd November, 1943.
The Senate met at a Quarter before
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
the
Voluntary Registration of Women.
The following Motion stood on
Order Paper in the name of Rev. Professor
Corkey:-
That this House approves of the
intention of the Government to institute
forthwith a voluntary registration of
women not at present registering at the
Employment Exchanges who are willing
to undertake work of national importance in Northern Ireland, and pledges
its support to this effort of the Government to stimulate the war effort and to
attract additional work to Northern
Ireland.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Rev.
Professor Corkey): Mr. Grant, the Minister of Labour, has kindly come along to
explain to us the nature of this Resolution, and with the consent of the House
I will ask him to address hon. Senators.
The MINISTER OF LABOUR (Mr.
Grant): In another place which I must
not mention by name, it is the custom to
be
very merciful to a Member when he is
making his first speech. I have not the
honour of being a Member of this House,
but I am making my first speech in it, and
I hope you will give me a large share of
the same charity, for I have come to ask
for your help.
As you know, the number of women
aged 18 and upwards now signing our
registers as wholly unemployed is down to
about 3,000. That is for the whole of
Northern Ireland. Some of these, of
course, from one infirmity or another are
not really fit to take a full-time job.
Others have been at one kind of work for
so long that they would find it very hard.
to turn to another.
But even so, these are only a minority
--and I may say a small minority-of our
unemployed women. The big difficulty in
finding work for the majority is that
about half of them are in the Belfast
area, and the rest are scattered all over
the country. Our Prime Minister has been
able to get a great deal of war work
placed here because he was able to tell
employers: "If you start a branch of your
business in Northern Ireland we can find
workers for you.'
""
I need not say that was an argument
that appealed strongly to cross-Channel
employers faced with a shortage of labour
in their home districts. But we cannot
give this promise at all so freely now.
There are many places in Northern Ireland with attractions in the way of good
water and electricity supplies, and rail or
road transport, where the number of
women registering for employment is far
too small to staff a factory.
It may be asked, then why not bring
them from other areas, as we do with
men? The answer is that women are
tied to their homes much more closely
than men.
Here in Belfast there are
many women perfectly able and willing
to do a full day's work in a factory close
at hand, who cannot take a job on the
other side of the city because they could
not get home in time to cook a mid-day
meal for their children. Even unmarried
girls have often to help in the housework
or look after younger brothers or sisters,
or attend an invalid relative. It must
also be remembered that a very high proportion of our women who were free to
leave home have enlisted in the Services
or gone to war work in Great Britain.
No one can deny that our women are
just as anxious as our men to play their
part in the national effort.
It may bring
a storm on my head for saying it, but I
believe they are more anxious-more
ready to put up with annoyances and
forget grievances so long as they know
they are helping towards victory. The
Government are,
are, therefore, going to
appeal for volunteers to those women and
girls who are not signing at the exchanges,
but feel they could do a full-time job if
there were one available within reach of
their homes.
If they come forward and fill up the
ranks, we will be able to say again to
employers, "Give us the work, and we
will find willing hands to do it." I want
to make this perfectly clear, the object
of this special registration is to get more
war work in Northern Ireland-here, and
nowhere else.
I must not, however, raise false hopes.
We cannot expect to get a new factory or
Voluntary Registration-
Women
3 NOVEMBER 1943
a new branch in every place where there
are girls and women ready to work.
There will be women dispersed here and
there in the country in groups too small
to staff a new undertaking. It may be
impossible for other reasons to bring
further employment to places where there
are enough women. Some must be disappointed. On the other hand, we are
asking very little of them unless we can
find the work. What we are looking for
at the moment is what I might call, a
reserve army of volunteers.
We shall want their names and
addresses and a very few particulars—age,
previous occupation if any, are they prepared to accept war work anywhere in
Northern Ireland, or only within daily
travelling distance of their homes. The
form for registering will be available at
all our exchanges. It is very simple and
will cause no headaches in filling up.
I realise that many people who would
like to volunteer may not find it convenient to go to the local exchange, but
if they will write to the manager of the
nearest exchange he will send them a
form so that they can fill it up and post
it back. I would like to explain that
particular point. It is not because we
want to make any class distinction about
people going to exchanges, but hon.
Members will appreciate the point that
some of these people may live four or five
miles away from employment exchanges,
and it would not be convenient for them
to come in. But if they write to the
manager of the nearest exchange he will
send them a form and they can post it
back to him. After that they need not
do anything more unless we are able to
offer them suitable employment. If we
can do that, we will ask them to call at
the exchange for an interview.
In the meantime they will not be bound
to us in any way. If a girl picks up a
job off her own bat, she is perfectly free
Even
to accept it, and good luck to her.
if we can offer her work she will be quite
free to refuse it. Naturally we do not
want people to volunteer with no intention of taking work, and I do not expect
anybody will. But circumstances change,
and a girl may put down her name in
perfect good faith and yet not be able to
take a job later on.
If so, good luck to her also; there will
be no hard feelings on either side. I
should like to point out at this stage that
when there are jobs available, of course,
these girls and women who are signing
the register in Northern Ireland will have
the first consideration. They will have
the opportunity of going to these jobs, for
hon. Senators will realise that women are
not as mobile as men. We cannot expect
women to travel 25, 30 or 40 miles from
their own homes to work or live in billets
or lodgings just the same as we can expect
men to do. For that reason the real
intention of this Resolution is to give the
Government power to have a voluntary
register of women who will be available
to work in their own districts, or, if they
feel like travelling further, to give them
the opportunity of employment in those
factories which we hope we will succeed
in getting in Northern Ireland.
There will, of course, be advertisements
in all Northern Ireland newspapers, and
we will try to make the scheme known as
best we can in other ways also. It was
to ask for your encouragement and support in this effort that I came here to-day.
We will certainly not get factories unless
we have more women available where they
are needed. Even if we find the women
we cannot guarantee that we will get the
factories, but we will improve our chances
enormously. Surely the experiment is
worth trying to help Northern Ireland,
and to help to win the war. We are all
agreed that the war effort is our first consideration, but each and everyone of us
has also in mind that we would like to
try and induce new industries to come to
Northern Ireland for the post-war period.
The Government have a feeling that if we
succeed in getting some new industries
established for the war effort with the
labour available in provincial and rural
areas, as well as in the county boroughs,
we may be able to persuade these people
to make their homes in Northern Ireland
in the post-war period, and thus provide
new industries for our country.
Hon Senators who meet here are all
I ask for that inpersons of influence.
fluence to be exerted to get women to
volunteer. It is not for me to say how
to do it. You know much better than I
where you can say a useful word, or what
organisations you can approach with hope.
of success. All I ask is that each of you
will give whatever help he can, where and
how he thinks best. I know that my
appeal will not be in vain.
Voluntary Registration-

Rev. Professor CORKEY:

by the Minister of Labour.

Mr. LESLIE: I feel that it would

I must confess that I rise to some extent

this afternoon. I should like to, so to

having done that I propose, with the

in the nature of a pat on the back, for I
do think that in bringing forward this
Resolution this afternoon the Government
is asking the House to do something
material towards not only the war effort
itself, but towards making it understood
across the water that this country is prepared to do everything it can.
There are some things we know we cannot do, although we would do them if we
had the opportunity, but here is a thing
we have a chance of doing. That is, we
are able to afford
women not already
engaged in war work the chance of putting
their names on a basic register from which
they can be called if and when employment is available for them. I do not
think the House would for a moment
hesitate to give the right hon. Gentleman
such support as is obtainable from the
passing of the Resolution.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
would like to join with the right hon. and
learned Senator in saying how pleased we
are to see the Minister of Labour attending this House for the first time. He has
put the purpose of the Motion very clearly
before us. I feel I have every reason to
give it all the support I possibly can, not
only in this House but outside. For a long
time the workers throughout Northern
Ireland complained that they were being
sent across the water to work instead of
the work being brought to them. In the
present case it appears that the Government are going along the right lines. They
are setting up a register to find out how
are
many workers they can enrol so that, if
possible, they can bring work to the
workers here without having to send them
across the water. I think that is a wise
and proper policy. I have been told very
often that the workers of Northern Ireland
very capable. They have been
accustomed mostly to linen work in our
mills and making-up businessses. They
are nimble in their fingers, and I think
they have some intelligence, too. I believe
these new industries are so suitable that
the workers become very competent to do
the work that has to be done in these
factories. Therefore, I am very glad there
is some effort being made to provide more
work for them, and I am extremely glad
indeed to learn of the number of factories
that have been started in Northern
Ireland.
Only a very small proportion of the
people realise just what the Government
have done to establish factories. When
I heard the figures, I was both surprised
and pleased. If there were more of these
factories established in Northern Ireland
there is a great possibility, I am sure, of
some of them remaining here and operating in the post-war period, which would
be of great benefit to Northern Ireland,
and especially to the workers. The unemployment figures among women which
the Minister of Labour mentioned are
very small. I thought they would have
been much larger, and that they are so
small must be due to these factories that
have been set up in recent times absorbing
the unemployed women from the linen
mills and factories. I am very glad to
know such progress has been made.
I am also very pleased to see the
Government taking this action, and I
will do anything I can to advertise the
scheme and persuade women to enrol. I
would like to congratulate the Government on the number of factories that have
been established and the amount of work
they have been able to bring to Northern
Ireland, especially in view of the fact
that all of them are engaged on war production and are thus doing something to
bring about victory as quickly as possible,
which is our chief concern.
Mr. BROWNE: I should like to join
in extending a very cordial welcome to the
Minister of Labour, and I trust we will
see him here on future occasions. After
his
very clear statement, I have no hesita-
Voluntary Registration-
Women
3 NOVEMBER 1943
tion in heartily supporting this Motion.
I hope there will be a generous response.
We gladly pay tribute to the magnificent
services which Ulster women are rendering in connection with the war. Thousands of them have joined the auxiliary
Forces, and others are engaged in munition factories. Mr. Herbert Morrison,
British Home Secretary, and other
British statesmen have spoken in the
highest terms of the way in which many
war workers normally engaged in textile
production for home and overseas markets
have made. themselves proficient in the
manufacture of a wide range of products
for the fighting Services. While thousands are doing splendid work, there are
still many women who have not yet
realised their responsibilities to the
nation, and we would make an earnest
appeal to them to take up work of
national importance.
As we all know, Ulster is famed for
many industries, and there is a great
opportunity now of breaking out in
other directions; not only in extending
our existing industries, but in establishing new ones. As we all know, many big
industries across the water have suffered
severely through air raids, and
our
Prime Minister is making herculean
efforts to induce employers there to open
factories and other industries in Northern
Ireland. The first question that those
employers naturally ask is, can the necessary labour be obtained? If women in a
position to do so will only register now,
the hands of our Prime Minister will be
strengthened and he will be able to
guarantee all the labour required. That
should be a great inducement, and the
opening of new industries will add considerably to the prosperity and happiness
of the whole community. I am sure
that the women of Ulster will not be
behind their men folk in coming forward
after they are told about the position.
There is no compulsion. It is purely
voluntary, but if we want a satisfactory
response the women must understand
about the whole business. There must,
therefore, be publicity, and as a newspaper man I am a great believer in
publicity. Newspapers, as they have done
in the past, will encourage registration,
but since the outbreak of war newspapers
have been sorely restricted, and owing to
the shortage of paper they have been
unable to increase their circulation; in
fact circulations have had to be reduced.
I am glad, however, to hear the Minister
of Labour say that he intends advertising
in newspapers, and I would also suggest
that leaflets upon the subject should be
issued, and perhaps throughout the
country meetings, which might be called
recruiting meetings, could be held. I
think if something like that is done no
difficulty will arise. We shall thus arouse
the necessary enthusiasm, and all the
registration of labour required will be
secured.

very largely due

this holy of holies this afternoon. Now
I have no desire to leave it. (Laughter.)
I feel that I have been made more
welcome here than in some other places I
do attend. (Laughter.) What I have

exhibited. I think that altogether we
are going to do all the things that have
been suggested by hon. Members this
afternoon. I do want to say to all the
Members of this House that I am very
grateful for their kindness and support.

"(
