Youth Welfare Bill [Senate].
Order for Second Reading read.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: This is not
a large Bill, but it is, I think, an important one. As hon. Senators are aware,
there is a widespread desire to-day to
assist youth organisations in their endeavour to further the interests of our
young people. The circumstances of our
time have made it very difficult for many
of our boys and girls. I need not do
more than mention several relevant facts
which will indicate the desirability of
strengthening our youth organisations.
The black-out, for example, necessitated
by the war has made our city streets much
more dangerous for young people than
they used to be; indeed, it has been
stated that at the present time the street
corner during the hours of darkness is
even more perilous than the middle of the
road. The hold-up in the building of
houses has resulted in overcrowding, and
home life for many of our young people
is much less attractive than it normally
would be. In addition to all that the
spirit of adventure and daring engendered
by the war, while in itself absolutely
sound, is liable, unless our young people
are rightly guided, to result in forms of
activities which are anything but good.
Further, while the educational work
being done in our elementary schools is
splendid so far as it goes most people feel
that it is inadequate and all too brief.
Young people are turned into industry at
fourteen and in many cases left without
any adequate guidance just at a time of
life when they would need a kindly voice
to advise and a kindly hand to guide.
On all these grounds there is obviously
an urgent need for more youth organisations in which boys and girls can spend
their spare time in occupations which
appeal to their tastes and which will be of
benefit to them in the formation of good
social habits. There is room for more
clubs which will provide meeting places
where boys and girls can take part in
activities which will be beneficial to their
moral and physical development, and
where, in association with their fellows,
they may prepare themselves for playing
their part in the wider social activities of
life when they have reached adult years.
We have been slow to realise the necessity
for this provision, and it has taken the
seismic disturbance of war to destroy our
attitude of complacency.
The importance of this matter cannot,
I think, be too much stressed. The boys
and girls of to-day are the citizens of the
future. Inevitably, in the process of time,
they will be conducting our businesses and
industry, they will be managing our cities
and towns, and they will be governing our
country. I believe it is absolutely essential that we should do all we can to fit
them for future responsibilities and to prepare them to play their part in the post
war world-a world of greater complexity
than we have known.
My Ministry has, since early in 1940,
made available grants under the Physical
Training and Recreation Act, 1938, to
assist youth organisations to extend and
develop their work, and it is certainly true
that much more is now being done to
afford boys and girls opportunities for
healthy activities in their leisure time.
Grants have been and are being given to
organisations of long-standing tradition,
such as the Boys' Brigades, Boy Scouts,
Girl Guides, to existing and newly-formed
youth clubs and centres in which varied
activities are conducted, such as physical
training, outdoor and indoor games, handwork, lectures, discussions, and to Cadets'
Associations-Sea Cadets, Army Cadets,
Air Training Corps and Training Corps
335 Youth Welfare Bill [Senate]- 3 NOVEMBER 1943

Second Reading
for Girls-which have a definite "preservice" bias, but provide also valuable
social training.
May I say in passing that while progress
in youth welfare work has not been
as rapid as it might have been
we do owe a great debt of gratitude to
the youth organisers and youth leaders--
the great majority of whom are voluntary
workers who are rendering invaluable
service to the community. There are.
some 16,000 boys and 10,000 girls in the
brigades, scouts and guides. There are
3,500 members in our youth clubs, many
of which are open three, four or more
nights each week. There are about
6,500 members of the various cadet
organisations. These figures will help
hon. Members to realise the volume of
splendid welfare work that is being done.
voluntarily for our young people, and I
am happy to say that there are at present
signs of an even greater public interest
in these movements and a greater willingness on the part of individuals to give
and serve as leaders.
But the extent of the problem is huge.
In Northern Ireland there are nearly
150,000 boys and girls between the ages
of 14 and 20 years. About 30,000 of
these are usefully occupied, either in fulltime studies or in evening attendance in
technical schools or other classes. But
their remains a total of about 120,000,
of whom only 36,000-or roughly 30 per
cent.-have attachments to the youth
organisations I have mentioned. Much,
therefore, remains to be done.
Hon. Senators will have noted the
variety of type of the voluntary youth
organisations. These differ greatly in
their methods of appeal to boys and girls.
Some base their activities on drill and
more formal training, some on physical
exercises, some on recreational, and some
on social training. This is as it should.
be, for young people vary greatly in their
inclinations. But one thing I must
stress is, that while the organisations have
these diverse means of dealing with boys
and girls they all, even those which are
known as pre-service" organisations,
have one common aim-the making of
the good citizen.
A service of this complexity obviously
calls for careful and delicate administration. There cannot be found in it one
clear-cut road to the final objective.
At-
Rather there are many winding paths,
diverging from one another in places and
coming together again in others.
tempts have been made, as hon. Senators
are aware, in certain European countries,
to regiment their youths, to drill them
into one type. This is entirely repugnant
to our ideas, and my Ministry has no
purpose other than to see our diverse
youth organisations developing along
their own individual lines, and by their
own methods, and to encourage them to
expand and extend their activities so as
in time to reach and capture as many as
possible of our young people. This, to
me, is preferable to a system of "State"
clubs conducted indirectly
indirectly by
Ministry. Accordingly I have not
accepted the recommendation that the
Youth Committee-the so-called Thompson
Committee-proposed in this Bill should
itself initiate and conduct youth clubs.
Furthermore, it is an accepted axiom in
this work that part, at least, of the expenses of youth organisations should be
defrayed by the organisations themselves. This is an additional argument
against the formation of clubs by the
Youth Committee.
my
As hon. Senators are aware, my predecessor in office, now Judge Robb,
appointed a Youth Welfare Committeethe Thompson Committee-to consider
this and other problems affecting youth
welfare in Northern Ireland, to report to
the Ministry, and to make recommendations as to their solution. That Committee
reported in December, 1942, and the
various proposals made in their Report
have since been under consideration. Of
these a number were designated by the
Committee as impracticable of achievement
until after the war. The Committee,
however, singled out as of urgent and
immediate importance the proposal that a
permanent Youth Committee should be
set up, with both advisory and executive.
functions, to review the whole field of
youth work, to advise the Ministry, local
education authorities and voluntary
organisations, and to encourage existing
agencies.
When we consider the diversity of the
many forms of youth welfare work and
the variety of organisations which are
making their contributions to it, the
advisability becomes apparent of forming
some body representative of the different
interests involved, which will be in a
position to survey the field, to give advice.
Youth Welfare Bill [Senate]-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
to those working in it, to help in securing
co-operation, and to guide in a general
way the progress and development of the
work.
The Bill now before the House proposes
to extend the Physical Training and
Recreation Act (Northern Ireland), 1938,
so as to include in its objects the promotion of youth welfare, and Clause 1 of the
Bill provides accordingly. Here I might
say that the powers given to the Ministry
in that Act are wide, enabling the
Ministry to make grants towards expenses
of local authorities and voluntary organisations in providing or aiding the provision of facilities, including gymnasiums,
playing fields, swimming baths, bathing
places, holiday camps, camping sites, and
other buildings and premises. When
youth welfare is included by Clause 1 of
this Bill among the objects of the 1938
Act, it will be found, I think, that
sufficient authority will have been given
to my Ministry to make grants in aid
of many of the projects which have been
recommended by the Thompson Committee.
Clause 2 forms the main part of the
measure and sets forth the functions
which it is proposed to invest in the Youth
Committee, and the manner in which it
is to be constituted. I do not think I
need add any comment on the functions
of the Committee, except to say that they
are sufficiently wide to place it in a
position to exert a very real influence on,
and to give impetus to the development
of this work.
Two factors have influenced me in
determining the character of the Committee:
(1) The wide variety of interests concerned with youth welfare which
should be represented;
(2) the advisibility that those Ministries
which are concerned with questions
of welfare and employment of the
young should be available to assist
the other members in their deliberations.
Hon. Senators may inquire what I propose should be the terms of office of the
Committee, and I should explain that it is
my intention that generally members
should hold office for at least four years.
It is very desirable that some continuity
of policy should be maintained, and with
this object I propose that of the twelve
"non-officials" on the Committee three
should retire annually in the fourth and
subsequent years. I intend, of course,
that retiring members shall be eligible
for reappointment, and I suppose that
normally the retiring members will be reappointed.
The functions of the Youth Committee
proposed by this Bill include, in respect
of youth welfare work, all those exercised
under the Physical Training and Recreation Act by both the Advisory Council
and the Grants Committee set up under
that Act; and there is no reason for
retaining these bodies in existence once
the Youth Committee to be established
by this Bill is set up. It is therefore proposed that Sections 1 and 2 of the Act
should be repealed.
The remaining parts of the measure
are largely self-explanatory and do not
appear to me to require elucidation. I
beg to move.
"That the Bill be
Question proposed,
now read a Second time..
Mr. BROWNE: I should like to
congratulate the Minister of Education
and also the Government on the introduction of this Measure. It is apparently intended as an improvement upon the
Physical Training and Recreation Act of
1938. The Minister has stated that that
Act has not been as great a success as was
expected. I suppose I may read that into
his observations. It would be interesting
to know to what extent the local authorities, especially rural authorities, had
made use of the Act, and how much
money has been paid to them; also how
much has been paid to voluntary organisations.
Perhaps an annual report giving a list
of the grants made has been issued, but
if so I have not seen it.
The White Paper is satisfactory so far
as it goes, but could an estimate of the
expenditure under this Bill not have been
given. The disbursements may be large,
yet we are in the dark as to the probable
amount involved.
The Act of 1938 brought into existence,
as we have been told by the Minister, an
Advisory Council and Grants Committee.
These are now to be wiped out in favour of
a permanent Youth Welfare Committee.

The Grants Committee had power to distribute grants up to a maximum of
£65,000. Has all that money been expended?
I notice that the members of the Youth
Committee shall hold office for such term
and upon such conditions as the Minister
of Education may prescribe.
I was
thinking it would have been better to include the terms of appointment in the
Bill, but after what the Minister has said
I do not think it is necessary that that
should be done. He has explained in
outline the conditions he intends to apply.
Then is the position of Chairman to be a
paid office and what is the remuneration?
It would have been interesting to have
been told that. We are told that this Act
shall be construed as one with the 1938
Act. The 1938 Act has been radically
altered, and would it not have been better
to have repealed the whole Act and introduced an entirely new Bill? Legislation by
reference is not satisfactory, at least to the
ordinary layman. It is hard to understand the full implications of a Measure
unless you have the complete text before
you. There is far too much legislation by
reference. I do not know whether the
right hon. Senator Leslie, who is an
authority on drafting Bills, will agree
with me or not on this subject.
I think when the Youth Committee
gets into working order it should submit
an annual report of its activities to the
Ministry of Education for submission
afterwards to Parliament. As I have
said, I support the Bill, though I should
like to have seen the Ministry adopt
more of the recommendations of the Committee set up by Mr. Robb. I do not
think anyone can reasonably object to
this Bill. It is most important that the
rising generation should have vigour of
body as well as vigour of mind, full of
vitality and energy and buoyant spirit.
We want our young people to be of high
moral and Christian character and usefully to occupy their spare time.
The Prime Minister in a recent speech
said:
Youth has been trained efficiently
for battle, it must now be trained efficiently for citizenship.
We can no
longer allow our youth to be like so
many Topsies, who had just "growed."
We must realise that the adventurous
spirit that may land a boy in the Police
Court is not so very different from the
adventurous spirit that sends him to
the R.A.F.
I think it is our duty to divert that
adventurous spirit into right channels.
It is deplorable to see so many young
persons standing idly at street corners
when they might be engaged in wholesome recreation. We want a higher
standard of physical fitness generally. It
is to the young people we must look to
see that the inheritance handed down to
them is handed on to their successors
unsullied, and anything that can be done
towards improving their health and
making them strong men and women will
be handsomely repaid.
The Government is, therefore, to be
commended for what it has done and
intends to do in this connection, so that
people may not only be better able to do
their work, but by possession of good
health add to the. happiness and wellbeing of the whole community.

would be an obstacle to leaders being sent
to these countries. At the same time I
agree that we could possibly benefit by an
examination of the systems of youth welfare in these different countries, and I
have no doubt this new Committee will
take that matter into consideration, and
will make itself wise as to the methods of
work that have been successfully adopted
elsewhere.
The difficulties of establishing such a
scheme have been referred to, particularly
the financial difficulties, because voluntary
organisations will have to raise at least
25 per cent. of the cost, and even that
may be considerable. I hope they will
realise that this expenditure is well worth
that effort, and the Government will back
them up if their clubs are efficiently run.
The difficulty in regard to securing leadership is also real, particularly at the
present time when so many of our potential leaders are not available, but I have
no doubt that suitable men can be found.
I am told that in Londonderry they have
been successful in securing an admirable
leader for the new club that has been
established there.
It has been suggested that this new
Committee has been overweighted by
officials, but they represent only onefourth of the total, and the other twelve
members will not be worth much if they
allow themselves to be brow-beaten on all
occasions by the four official members.
Perhaps if they get their way every other
time it will not be so bad. Mark Twain
tells about Tom Sawyer's legs. One particular leg said to the other, "If you let
me pass this time I will let you pass
the next time," and they could not get
locomotion by any other method.
The Committee will be representative,
as far as possible, of all classes and creeds
in the community, and I shall be glad to
supply Senator McLaughlin with a list of
the members of the two committees established under the old Act. These committees did not function very well, but I
should like to help the hon. Senator to
add to his interesting collection of committees. These are all the remarks which,
I think, it is necessary to make on the
Second Reading of this Bill.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Adjournment Motion.
THE LATE SIR EDWARD
ARCHDALE.

until Tuesday."
Mr. LESLIE: I do not think this

of us have heard of the death of Sir
Edward Archdale, which occurred, I
understand, on All Saints' Day. We
should like to pay a humble tribute of
admiration to a life well spent in public
service. We remember him in this House
appearing very many times to support
before us various measures of the Ministry of Agriculture-measures which did
not invariably receive undivided support,
but which were always presented in the
most heartfelt manner in order to obtain
the good of Ulster and the prosperity
of agriculture. We are poorer by the
loss of Sir Edward Archdale.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I should
like to associate myself with the suggestion made by the. hon. and learned
Senator. Sir Edward Archdale was a
grand old man and an able administrator
of the affairs of this country for many
years, a man who had a very reasonable
mind and great public spirit, and a most
equable temper, a man with whom anyone
could have worked. I beg to associate
myself with the observations that have
been made. I do not know the procedure, but perhaps you, Mr. Speaker,
would suggest that we stand for a moment
in token of respect.

Expiring Laws Continuance Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE
(Rev. Professor Corkey): I beg to move,
"That this Bill be now read a second
time." As hon. Members know, every
year a number of Acts have to be continued in this way. On our Statute
Book at present there are a number of
such Acts, and they are found in the
Schedule of the Bill, which consists of
two parts. It is not necessary for me
to give any extended account of these
Acts, as most of them are familiar to
hon. Members already. The first part
of the Schedule deals with Imperial
Acts, the operation of which is continued
for another year by this Bill. Only one
Act is referred to, the Harbours, Docks,
and Piers (Temporary Increase of
Charges) Act, 1920. This Act enables
harbour authorities to raise their dues
to meet increased costs. The second
part of the Schedule deals with Acts
which have been enacted in Northern
Ireland, and the usefulness which has
not yet come to an end. The first of
these is the Local Authorities and Jurors
Book (Temporary Provision) Act, 1940,
which enables the postponement of elections for local authorities owing to the
existence of the war and the war situation.
It is felt that that Act should continue
for at least another year. The next Act
referred to is the Housing (Temporary
Accommodation) Act, 1942. Certain
temporary provisions were enacted relaxing the requirements governing the building of houses owing again to the difficulties created by the war, and it is proposed
that this law also should be continued for
another year.
The third Act of this sort referred to
is the Agricultural Wages (Flax Workers)
Act adopted last year. This gives power
to the Agricultural Wages Board of Northern Ireland to fix the standard rates of
wages for workers. Another Act mentioned in this schedule is one referring to
ice cream, which suspends the statutory
requirements in regard to the use of milk
fats in ice cream. The necessity for that
requirement is still in existence, and it is
proposed to continue that Act in operation
for another year. There is also the Local
Government (Emergency Powers) Act,
1921, which gives the Government authority to suspend local authorities which
have refused or failed or may be unable
to perform all or any of their Statutory
functions. That Act also gives the Ministry power to enable local authorities to
resume their functions when it is felt in
the opinion of the Government that the
time has arrived to do so. The time has
not yet arived when the Government feel
that these Acts should expire, and so it
is proposed to keep them in force for
another year.
Question proposed.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I propose to vote
against this Bill because in one of these
Acts the Government, to my mind, have
abused their powers. The city of Armagh,
where I come from, has had a commissioner for the past 10 years, and the
people of Armagh have been deprived of
the franchise for that time. I think it is
an undemocratic state of affairs that such
a thing is in existence. Surely to goodness it is time the citizens of Armagh at
least got the opportunity of appointing
their own town council. The Minister
stated that in one of these Acts the Government have power to suspend and also
power to reinstate. I suggest to the
Minister that if the Government are
honest and wish to do what is right
towards the citizens of Armagh, they will
immediately restore the franchise to the
people of that city and permit them to
appoint their own council. In Belfast
advantage has been taken of these powers
and the Administrators have been practically done away with, and I do not see
any reason why the same should not be
done as far as the city of Armagh is concerned.
Expiring Laws Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE

Appropriation (No. 2) Bill

Finance (No. 2) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time." This short one-clause Bill
is concerned with a concession made to
farmers in June last regarding the use of
their lorries. As hon. Members know, if
a vehicle of this type is taxed at a reduced
rate the owner can only use it for the conveyance of his agricultural produce or
articles required for his own land. However, it has been thought that during the
harvest time, owing to the pressure of
work, a concession ought to be made to
farmers possessing lorries, and they ought
to be given the right to use their lorries
for the transport of goods belonging to
their neighbours or even to lend their to
their neighbours for such a purpose. The
purpose of the Bill is to empower farmers
so to make use of their vehicles. The
time during which this relaxation is permissible is not referred to in the Bill, but
the Minister of Finance has announced
that it is not his intention to extend the
concession to other times of the year than
the harvest time, as it is felt that it is
only when the crops are being brought in.
that the case for transport assumes
sufficient urgency to demand this exceptional measure.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Moved, "That this Bill be now committed." (Rev. Professor Corkey.)
On Question, Motion negatived.
Ordered, That the Bill be read the
Third time on Tuesday next.

CLAUSE 2 (Establishment and functions of Youth Committee).
The following Amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Sir Roland
Nugent:-
Clause 2, sub-clause (1), after paragraph (ii), to add:-
(iii) To promote and administer any
scheme or schemes for youth welfare
which may appear to the Minister, after
consultation with the Committee, to be
of such a character that they could most
efficiently be dealt with under the direct
control of the Committee and to be of
sufficient importance to justify a departure from the normal procedure under
this Act.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The amendment standing in my name is, as the
Committee will realise, purely permissive..
It seemed to me that an occasion might
arise when it would be desirable for one
Youth Welfare Bill [Senate]-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
reason or another that an activity for
the encouragement of youth welfare
might need to be run directly by the
Youth Committee. I agree that normally
it is much better that they should confine themselves to encouraging and promoting youth welfare, but you might get
a case in one particular locality, or under
some special circumstances
easy to
imagine, when rather more direct control
and direction by the Committee would be
desirable. I thought it would be. a pity
that, if such an occasion did arise, the
Ministry should not have power to
authorise the Youth Committee to deal
with it. Really it is a sort of insurance
measure, SO that in case the matter
arises the Ministry should have the
necessary power.
Question put, That those words be
there added."
Rev. Professor CORKEY: This amendment has an objective appearance, and
I may say I did my best to persuade
myself that it was innocuous and would
be a useful addition to the Bill, but
after considering it with the greatest/
care, I came to the conclusion that it
gives to the Ministry a power which
would be in many possible situations.
inconvenient There. are some powers
which a Ministry had better not have.
I may first point out that the amendment contravenes the general principle
of the Bill, which is that this youth
welfare work should be carried on by
co-operation between the Ministry and
the local authorities on the one hand,
or between the Ministry and voluntary
organisations on the other, and all the
clubs and organisations which have so
far been established have been of that
co-operative nature.
This possibility of the Ministry being
able to finance and run an organisation
of its own would open a door, and it
might not be very easy to keep that
door even ajar. There would be
appeals continually made. to the Ministry to take over more and more of this
youth work, and the time does not seem
to be ripe for that yet. It would be
difficult for the Ministry to differentiate
between an organisation on behalf of
which an appeal of this sort is made.
For these reasons I am reluctantly compelled to resist this amendment.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I think
the. Minister is making a mistake in
refusing a handsome offer, but in view
of what he has said, I ask leave to withdraw the amendment.

committee which we are going to establish
the services of members directly representing three of the Departments will be
really valuable; whether they will not
perhaps be a hindrance rather than a
help. Let us look at the members suggested in the sub-clause, and whom I suggest should not be asked to serve on this
Youth Committee. They are officials of
the Ministry of Home Affairs, the Ministry of Labour, and the Ministry of Education. That is all three of them. The
Thompson Committee suggested there
should be four, the fourth being a representative of the Ministry of Finance.
think I am correct in saying that in the
Grants Committee which was established

the Ministries of Home Affairs, Labour,

It was not in the last Act, nor was it recommended by the Thompson Committee,
but I have left it in because it seems to
me that the Assistance Board may possibly have a knowledge of domestic circumstances which might make representation a possibly valuable adjunct to the
Committee. I am not very sure about it
myself, and if the Committee accepts the
main amendment I should not resist an
amendment at a later stage to leave out
the member of the Assistance Board.
What good are these three gentlemen
going to do? They cannot help us in
finance, because there is no representative
of the Ministry of Finance, nor is there
a representative of the Ministry of Agriculture.
We have a representative of
the Home Office, which is more concerned
with youthful delinquents, and that, although it may be of some interest to the
new Youth Committee, is really rather a
side show, because the object of this Committee is not SO much to deal with
youthful delinquency but youth as a

good he could possibly

the Ministry of Education, the supreme

Ministry of Education will either propose
something the Ministry does not like or

is an obstruction for the Ministry in the
final stage. and for the other members.

Rev. PROFESSOR CORKEY: The
effect of this amendment is to eliminate
from this Committee the three individuals

361 Youth Welfare Bill [Senate]-
THE SENATE

Committee
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
Committee. First of all, the Ministry of
Home Affairs deals not only with juvenile

under its care certain institutional schools,
and is concerned with public health and
medical services, and it is thought that

these grounds be useful members of this
Committee. The Ministry of Labour is
concerned with the employment of young
people and with their insurance, and, to
a certain extent, with their health, and
these grounds would seem to justify a
member of this Ministry being a unit in
the Committee.
As regards the Ministry of Education I

this Ministry. He fears that anyone
from this Ministry will be a positive nuisance and a source of mischief. I have
not formed such a bad opinion of the
staff officers whom I have met in the
Ministry, and I think it quite likely that
they will be useful and will bring a
fund of information and experience which
will prove useful to the Committee.
Senator Leslie has referred to the fact
that the Committee which sat under the
chairmanship of Judge Thompson recommended that these civil servants, along
with a representative of the Ministry of
Finance, should be members of the Committee. I may explain that the Ministry
of Finance requested that they should not
be represented on the Committee for some
reason or other they did not express, and
at their wish it is not proposed to appoint
a member from that Ministry to the Committee.
The special reason we ask for these
officials to be appointed members of the
Committee is that the Committee will
have difficult and practical problems to
deal with, and we think they will have
experience and wisdom to give the Committee, although the Committee may be
prepared to set aside their judgment.
Further, the grants that have been made
for youth work hitherto have been made
by a committee composed entirely of civil
servants, and these men have already a
certain amount of experience in this
matter which will be helpful, and it
would be a pity in setting up this new
Committee to lose the chance of the assistance they will be able to give. Perhaps
I may mention that up to this point Dr.
Garrett has been chairman of the Grants
Committee and has done valuable work in
guiding its affairs. It was a quite small
committee, consisting of four or five civil
servants. We think that on the ground
of the experience and wisdom they will
bring to bear, these individuals should be
allowed to remain members of this Committee. Therefore the Government cannot accept the Amendment.

Bill would be best served if I withdrew
the Amendment and put down on the
Report stage an Amendment providing
for a representative of the Ministry of
Agriculture, and so giving the Committee a touch of symmetry which, I
think, it lacks at the moment. On the
Report stage also the question raised by
the hon. and gallant Senator opposite
might be considered; that is to say,
whether these Government representatives
should attend in an advisory capacity
without power to vote. There is plenty
of precedent for that.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: This is going
to be a Committee controlled by civil
servants. None of us know what the
quorum will be. Many of the twelve
unofficial members to be appointed may
come from the country, and some day
they may be absent and the civil servants
may have the majority vote. I quite agree
with the hon. and gallant Senator
opposite that if these gentlemen are to
be on the Committee they should not
have power to vote. If this Committee
is to benefit the youth of the country, I
would suggest to the Minister that it
should be representative of public
bodies. I could have understood it if
the Minister had selected public men
instead of civil servants. If the Committee had included representatives of
the Association of County Councils and
the Association of Urban District Councils instead of civil servants, it would
have been more representative of the
public and would have given the public
a say in the matter. If the Government
is going to persist in having four or five
civil servants on this Committee with
voting powers, then I say the Bill will
be a failure from the start, because the
Committee will practically be governed
by civil servants.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: There was
a desire to have this Committee as small
as possible, and for this reason we were
quite glad to drop the representative of
the Ministry of Finance when that
desire was expressed by the Ministry.
With regard to the proposal put forward by the hon. and gallant Senator
behind me, it is interesting and will be
considered when it is brought forward
at a later stage. So far as the point
made by Senator McLaughlin is concerned, I hope the Committee will be
representative of the various local
authorities.
Mr. LESLIE: And I hope the question of a representative of the Ministry

CLAUSE 3 (Training of organisers,
etc., of youth welfare physical training
and recreation).
(1) The Ministry may make grants
towards the expenses of training at
an approved college or institution, as
organisers, wardens, instructors or
leaders of youth welfare, physical
training and recreation, such persons
as the Ministry may, on the advice of
the Youth Committee, accept as suitable for such training.
A grant under this section shall not
exceed three-fourths of the expenses
to be incurred in the training of any
such person as aforesaid.
(2) In this section the expression
"approved college or institution"
means any college or institution,
youth club or centre in Great Britain
or Northern Ireland approved by the
Ministry for the purposes of this
section.
(3) Section six of the principal Act
is hereby repealed.
The following Amendments stood on
the Order Paper in the name of Sir
Roland Nugent:-

words:
but the Ministry may make a grant for
the full expenses incurred in any case
in which it appears to them, after consultation with the Committee, that an
increased grant is necessary to secure
that a sufficient number of persons present themselves for training, or to
ensure that the training is fully effective.
New Clause.-Page 4, line 2, at the end
add the following new Clause:
cause
4. (1) The Minister shall
periodic study to be made of the
methods for the promotion of youth
welfare employed in the Dominions and
foreign countries and may make such
grants as may be necessary to defray
the expenses of or to remunerate the
persons employed on such study.
(2) If the Minister decides on the
•
advice of the Committee, that it is expedient to introduce any of the methods
in use in other countries into Northern
Ireland, he may arrange that suitable
persons shall visit those countries to
acquire practical experience of such

or part of the expenses of such

Mr. SPEAKER: I am afraid that these
Amendments by the hon. and gallant
Member will have to be ruled out of order.
They deal with finance.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I was afraid
of that being so, but I had some hope
that they might be permitted.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in Clause
3, sub-clause (2), to leave out the words
"in Great Britain or Northern Ireland."
I referred to this matter on the Second
Reading. What I am afraid of here is
that the Ministry may inadvertently put
in something which might be inconvenient.
The proposal is, in effect, to confine the
various proceedings to Great Britain and
Northern Ireland. I think that on the
whole even in Northern Ireland we have
to accept it that the expression "Britain"
includes Wales. Wales is legally a part
of England as it was always one of the
Britains. On those rare occasions when
we do refer to Great Britain I do not
think there is any special reference to
Wales, but if Great Britain includes Wales
it certainly does not include the Isle of
Man, and why should we leave out the
Isle of Man. There is also the broader
question of the possibility of deriving information of what is being done in the
United States, and possibly in some of the
Scandinavian countries and in Eire. One
of the most valuable witnesses at the
Thompson Committee came from Eire.
Hon. Members have not read the evidence
of that Committee as it was not published,
but there is repeated reference in the
Thompson Report to that gentleman's
evidence. I think it will not do any harm
to leave out these words, which might
possibly be unduly restrictive.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I have been
impressed by the facts brought forward
by Senator Leslie. I think it might be
an advantage in extending the possibility
of training in the direction which he has
suggested, and I think that this Amendment should be accepted.

The LEADER OF THE HOUSE
(Rev. Professor Corkey): I beg to move,
"That this Report be now received."
Mr.
JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
was going to suggest that the Report
stage of this Bill might be deferred. It
is evident that the longer this Bill is
before the public, the more interest it
creates. The churches, which carried on
this youth welfare work for years before
the Government intervened, are very
anxious to give this Bill more, consideration, and to help, if possible, in improving
the phraseology of the Bill. I have had
with me several representatives of the
churches, and they suggest the Bill
should be deferred for another week to
give them an opportunity to carefully
consider the measure and submit any
amendments they may think desirable.
They would rather that was done before
the Bill went to another place.
However, if the Minister cannot see his
way to further adjourn the Bill I shall
have to submit, but the churches deserve
consideration in this matter, because they
have done a tremendous amount of good
work in years past. The Boys' Brigade
and the Girl Guides have been carried on
by the churches, and now, when we have
a Bill to put this work under the control
of the Government, I think the representatives of the churches should be
given every consideration. If it is
possible to defer the Report stage for
another week, I think it would be a nice
gesture to the representatives of the
churches.
Mr. LESLIE: With due deference to
what the hon. Member has just said,
surely, the House having received the
Report of the Amendments, the proper
course would be on the Third Reading to
move that the Bill be referred back to the
Committee. If a Motion of that sort is
made a case might perhaps be made for
it, but at the moment we cannot very
well do it, as we have received the Report
of the Amendments. I think it would be
better to finish the Amendments on the
Paper, and on the Third Reading a
Motion can then be made to defer the
Third Reading.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I presume
that the suggestion of the hon. Member
opposite is in order-that the Bill be
deferred-but I do not think it should
be. The Bill has already been on the
way quite a long time. There is a very
heavy legislative programme on hand,
and it would appear that the Senate will

Rev. Professor CORKEY: I beg to
move, in Clause 2, sub-clause (2), to
leave out from the word "of" in line 22
to the end of the sub-clause, and to
insert instead thereof, "twelve persons
(not being officers of a Government department) appointed by the Minister of
Education. This is an Amendment
which has been circulated in manuscript
form, for which I ask the consideration of
the House. I trust hon. Members will
raise no objection to our considering it,
although it has been brought before them
very hurriedly. It is a very simple
matter, and there is no difficulty in
understanding it. Judging from the
sentiments expressed in the House at an
earlier stage, I think it is an Amendment
on which there will be general agreement.
On the Committee stage the right hon.
and learned Senator opposite proposed
that certain civil servants should sit on
the Committee, but should not have
votes. I have discovered that the
Ministries were unwilling to nominate
civil servants on a Committee on those
conditions. Consequently we have been
forced, out of consideration for the views
of the Senate and owing to this further
fact, to revert to the right hon. and
learned Member's first form of Amendment. Therefore I beg to move the
Amendment, the significance of which is
that all reference to civil servants is
deleted from the Bill.
Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: I should like to welcome
the proposal of the right hon. Gentleman.
I am quite satisfied in my own mind that
it represents the real feeling of the House
as expressed on the Second Reading and,
to some extent, carried out on the Committee stage. It will be remembered that
the original proposal was to put in the
representatives of some Ministries and
leave out the Ministry of Agriculture and
so on, and there was also a proposal
not to include any representatives
of the Ministries at all. Now the
Amendment has got rid of all official
representatives, and I think the House
will be very well advised to accept
it. The effect of that will be that my
two Amendments on the Paper will not
be moved.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: While I
welcome the Amendment, I must admit
that I am not impressed by the reasons
given for it. This House was considering putting in the Bill a direction to a
375 Youth Welfare Bill [Senate]-
23 NOVEMBER 1943

Report
Government Department. In other words,
they were considering issuing an order to
their servants. Those servants now chose
to say they will not put their representatives on the Committee on those conditions. Therefore, although I approve
of the Amendment, I am going to express
my very strong disapproval of the reason
given for it by voting against the Amendment.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Apart from what
the last speaker has said, I think the
Government have taken a wise course in
this particular instance. They have undoubtedly acceded to public opinion, because the criticism of this Bill was rightly
directed chiefly against the fact that the
Government had taken far too much
control over this Bill and its administration. They appointed a committee
with absolutely no control so far as the
administrative matters under the Bill are
concerned. I hold a very strong objection in principle to civil servants being
appointed on these committees and I
believe that in holding that opinion I
am supported by the great majority of
the thinking and senior members of the
Civil Service, who have devoted their
energies and talents to trying to make
themselves first-class whole-time civil
servants. As regards what the hon.
and gallant Member opposite has said, I
am not prepared to say anything at the
moment, because we have not sufficient
data before us. At the same time, if I
thought Members of the Government
were going to exercise undue influence to
deprive civil servants from occupying
positions which this House intended them
to occupy, no Member of this House would
oppose that more than I should.
Now, that they have proposed this
Amendment, I would suggest to the
Government that it has been brought in
in answer to the views expressed in this
House and the general will of the people.
The general objection to this Bill is that
the Government have maintained far too
much control over the functions of this
Committee, and not left it free and elastic
to carry out what, in their opinion, might
be required under the Bill. Further, I
would suggest to the Leader of the House
-and he will understand that I am not
criticising him personally, but only in his
position as Minister of Education-that
he should explain to the House why, in
the first place, he appoints this committee, and why he should determine how
long any member is to sit on the committee. If an individual member differs
from the views of the Minister of Education for the time being, it is open to the
Minister to dismiss him without rhyme
or reason.
Not only that, but I would like the
Minister to justify his decision that the
committee when appointed, should not
have the right to elect its own chairman,
and why the Minister of Education should
retain that appointment in his own hands.
Why should the Minister be in a better
position to exercise a choice, than the
collective members of the committee. I
think these are questions to which the
right hon. Gentleman should address himself and see if they cannot be remedied
before this Bill finally passes, because
there is a feeling that, in this Bill in
particular, the Ministry of Education has
taken unto itself so much control that it
is really a Government Department Bill.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: The last
speaker has covered a good deal of
ground, and I am afraid I cannot give
him an answer to all the details he has
raised, surveying as he did every part of
the Bill. He suggested that the. Government have exercised too much control
over this Youth Welfare Committee, but
the fact is that the committee is only
coming into existence. now. Therefore,
it cannot be said that the Government
have exercised too much control over the
committee. The effect of the amendment
I have just moved is that the Government has taken away any possible excuse
for making a charge of that sort. In
regard to the appointment of the chairman of the committee, I understand it
is customary for the Government to take
power to appoint the chairman of a committee of this sort.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Why?
Rev. Professor CORKEY: That would
be a long story.
Amendment agreed to.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in
Clause 2, sub-clause (5), after the word
"Committee" in line 9, to insert the
words, "who, in case of equality of votes,

is on the whole more usual than not,

should be exercised, to give direct power
for the exercise of that vote. There are
numerous precedents. There was one
the other day in the Rent Restrictions
Act, and an older one is the Privy Council Order attached to the Local Government Act of 1898. I think the words
ought to go in, and therefore I beg to
move.

been made by the right hon. and learned
Senator, and the Government therefore
accepts this amendment.

The following Amendment stood
the Order Paper in the name of Sir
Roland Nugent:-
Page 4, line 2, at the end add the
following new clause:-
NEW CLAUSE (Methods of Promoting Youth Welfare).
(1) The Minister shall cause periodic
study to be made of the methods for the
promotion of youth welfare employed in
the Dominions and foreign countries.
(2) If the Minister decides on the
advice of the Committee, that it is expedient to introduce any of the methods
in use in other countries into Northern
Ireland, he may arrange that suitable
persons shall visit those countries to
acquire practical experience of such
methods.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I think
the amendment standing in my name is
practically self-explanatory. I said on
the Second Reading that I attached very
great importance to the very interesting
experiments which have been carried out
in other countries in regard to youth
379 Youth Welfare Bill [Senate]-

23 NOVEMBER 1943
Report
welfare being closely followed. I believe
that if from time to time the Minister of
Education follows those developments in
other countries he will find some aspects
of them which it might be desirable to
try here. I therefore want, first of all,
to ensure that it shall be the duty of
the Minister to watch developments in
other countries. Secondly, if, as a
result of such study he thinks it desirable to arrange that suitable persons
should visit those countries and obtain
practical knowledge of the
of the way the
authorities there are working, I want
him to have the power to do so.
I expect this is the last time I shall
speak on this Bill, and I should like to
congratulate the Minister on his Bill. I
very much hope it will have a peaceful
passage in another place, and not suffer
too drastic amendment.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: The hon.
and gallant Senator, who moved this
Amendment, said it was self-explanatory.
I would suggest also that it is supererogatory. The Ministry does make a study
of the methods for promoting youth welfare in the Dominions and foreign
countries. That is being done all the
time. We get magazines and books and
reviews about educational work and youth
welfare work in other countries. New
Zealand, in particular, has had a number
of interesting experiments of late, and
also Denmark and Norway. In fact there
are gentlemen associated with educational
and youth welfare work visiting Northern Ireland at the present time in order
to share with us their information in
regard to these subjects. The new Clause,
therefore, does not seem to the Government to add anything to the responsibilities or duties of the Ministry, as these
already exist. The Clause would really
be superfluous in the Bill, and for these
reasons the Government do not think it
should be accepted.

various schemes actually being carried
I most strongly support the new

383 Workmen's Compensation Bill-
THE SENATE.

Thursday, 25th November, 1943.
The Senate met at a quarter before
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Workmen's Compensation (Temporary
Increases) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Rev.
Professor Corkey): The Bill to which
to-day I ask the Senate to give a second
reading is very short, but notwithstanding
that, I believe it will commend itself to
the House.
There
The purposes of the Bill are simple. It
is common knowledge that wage levels.
have risen, and what the Bill proposes is
to raise the maximum amounts payable
in cases of accidents to workpeople so as
to lessen the hardship caused to a high
wage earner when he is disabled.
has been no increase in the amounts payable as workmen's compensation since
pre-war days except the supplementary
allowances granted in 1940, but
amounts were small and were intended
merely to cover the increased cost of living.
the
At the present time, for example, a
man with a wife and three children earning £5 per week would, if disabled, receive only 46s per week compensation.
Under the Bill the weekly compensation
payable to him becomes 55s rising to 65s
after thirteen weeks.
I think that is a
proposal which will commend itself to the
House. I assure hon. Members that there
is nothing in the Bill which prejudices
consideration of the Beveridge proposals,
and, indeed, the amounts which it is now
intended to provide bring workmen's compensation in its present framework closer
The increases proto those proposals.
posed are temporary and will cease after
31st December, 1946, by which time, no
doubt, a new scheme for workmen's compensation will have come into being.
Turning now to the provisions of the
Bill itself the effect of Clause 1 is to leave
the single man or woman on the present
weekly maximum of 35s except in the
more serious cases where disablement continues after 13 weeks, when another 5s
will be payable, making a maximum
weekly payment of 40s. The man with
a wife gets a further 5s in respect of her,
raising his maximum to 40s as against
35s at present, and after 13 weeks' disablement he gets a further 10s, making
50s in all as against the present 35s. This
is subject to conditions which I need not
elaborate.
An important addition is made to the
scale of allowances in respect of children.
These at present are 4s for each of the first
two children under 15 and 3s for every
other such child, and it is now proposed
that the allowance shall be 5s per child.
This 5s allowance moreover will now be
payable after 15 years of age up to the
31st July following the sixteenth birthday provided the child continues at school
in receipt of full time instruction. The
maximum weekly payments, including
children's allowances, must not, however,
exceed seven-eighths of the average weekly
earnings.
In the cases of partial incapacity, that
is to say where the workman is able to
perform light work, the amounts payable
will be in proportion to the difference between his pre-accident and post-accident
I know that sounds rather
earnings.
complicated and it is a difficult matter to
explain, but it does mean that he gets the
full proportion of compensation opposite
his loss of earning power.
Clause 2 allows an increase from £600
to £700 in the lump sum payable in fatal
cases where children are left, and from
£300 to £400 where an adult wholly de-
Where weekly
pendent person is left.
payments have been made prior to death,
they can at present be deducted from the
lump sum payable to an adult dependant
so long as they do not reduce it below
£200. Under the Bill this minimum
amount is raised to £300.
Clause 3 merely makes certain necessary
alterations in the other provisions of the
1940 Act consequent upon the increases
given in this Bill, and I think the remaining Clauses call for no comment.
I beg
to move, "That the Bill be now read a
Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.

385 Youth Welfare Bill [Senate]-

Third Reading

Rev. Professor CORKEY: I beg to

CLAUSE 4 (Methods of promoting
youth welfare).
(1) The Minister shall cause periodic
study to be made of the methods for
the promotion of youth welfare employed in the Dominions and foreign
countries.
(2) If the Minister decides on the
advice of the Committee, that it is expedient to introduce any of the methods
in use in other countries into Northern
Ireland, he may arrange that suitable
persons shall visit those countries to
acquire practical experience of such
methods.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Before
moving the Amendment to Clause 4
standing in my name, I would like, if I
may, to make a short personal explanation. I understand that something I
said on an Amendment to Clause 2 on the
Report stage was construed as being a
reflection or attack on the Civil Service.
I need hardly say that nothing was
further from my mind. I have not had
an opportunity of seeing Hansard, but
I do not think that what I said would
have borne that construction. I hasten
to say that my attack was directed, as
usual, to Ministers. It would be most
improper that I should attack civil
servants, and, in this instance, most unfair, as they are not to blame. I was
merely pointing out that when Bills are
passing through this House, for Ministers
to announce that if certain provisions did
pass they proposed to disregard them, was
obviously something I could not agree to.
To come to the six Amendments I have
tabled, they really comprise one Amendment directed to correct certain ambiguities in the wording of a Clause we passed
when last we met. I am indebted to the
Parliamentary Draughtsman for pointing
out that the original wording might have
been misleading. The point is, first of
all, that in the Schedule of the Bill as it
stands, while the word "Ministry is
defined as being the Ministry of Education, the word "Minister" is not defined,
so that it is necessary to insert the words
"of Education." Apart from that, the
wording of the original Clause might have
been held to have excluded the
Colonies or the remainder of the
United Kingdom, because they were
not specifically mentioned, and it is
sought to alter the wording accordingly.
I think the best thing would be to read
out Clause 4. It will read when
amended: :-
(1) The Minister of Education shall
cause periodic study to be made of the
methods for the promotion of youth
welfare employed in countries outside
Northern Ireland.
(2) If the said Minister decides on
the advice of the Committee that it is
expedient to adopt any of the methods
in use in any country outside Northern
Ireland, he may arrange that suitable
persons shall visit that country to
acquire practical experience of such
methods.
As the House will see, the amendments are, in essence, the same as the
original Clause, but they make quite sure
that we can visit all countries, and that
the Minister who will act is the Minister
of Education. I beg to move the
amendments.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: The Government accept the amendments.
Amendments made:
Clause 4-
,
Page 3, line 34, after the word
"Minister" insert the words "of Education."
Page 3, line 36, leave out the. words,
"the Dominions and foreign countries,"
and insert the words "countries outside
Northern Ireland."
Page 3, line 37, after the first "the"
insert the word "said."
Page 3, line 38, leave out the word
""'introduce" and insert the word
"adopt."
""
Page 3, line 39, leave out the words
"other countries into,' and insert the
words "any country outside."
""
Page 3, line 40, leave out from the
word "visit" to the word to" in line
41, and insert the words "that country."

Exchequer and Financial Provisions
Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison).
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time." As an explanatory
memorandum has been circulated to hon.
Senators it is unnecessary for me to do
more than refer very briefly to the
various Clauses of this short Bill.
The purpose of Clause 1-despite its
technical language is simple and comprehensive. In fulfilment of guarantees
given to shipowners under the Loans
Guarantee Acts the Government became
indebted to the extent of some
£4,500,000. Over £2,000,000 of this
sum was recovered, but in respect of the
remainder only securities were received.
At present day prices, however, these
securities are estimated to be more than
sufficient to cover the debts incurred, and
a partial realisation of them has already
taken place. The debts, of course, have,
as far as the Exchequer is concerned,
been discharged long ago, and the moneys
now received on account of the realisation
of these securities are regarded rather in
the nature of windfalls.
Under the Exchequer and Financial
Provisions Act, 1942, the Government
were authorised to use these windfalls to
help pay off the Suspense Debt on the
Northern Ireland Unemployment Fund.
The extinguishment of the Suspense Debt
is in sight, and a conservative valuation
of the securities as yet unrealised shows
that there is every probability of there
being a substantial surplus in hand. It
has again been agreed that this surplus
when it emerges should be used for other
of our own capital purposes, including
the repayment of Exchequer debt. Subsection 1 accordingly gives effect to this.
Apart from the satisfactory position
regarding the liabilities actually incurred, contingent liabilities under the
Loans Guarantee Acts have been reduced
to so low a level as to make unnecessary
the special statutory provisions which
exist for meeting such liabilities, when
they arise. Sub-section 2, therefore, repeals these provisions.
Financial Provisions Bill
Milk Bill
THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
unto our
This Clause thus reflects in no
certain way one aspect of the very
pleasing outcome of the Government's
activities in connection with loans
guarantees. These guarantees have resulted in approximately 100 modern
vessels being made available
Merchant Navy in a very crucial period
of our nation's history, and even if they
had involved the Government in
pecuniary loss, their value is established
beyond dispute. Nevertheless it is
gratifying to know that there does not
even now remain the slur that this has
been achieved at the expense of the
Exchequer.
a
Clause 2 authorises the Government to
increase the sums which may be advanced
to the Ministry of Commerce for purposes under the Electricity Supplies Act
(Northern Ireland), 1942, by £1,500,000.
Though the initial programme carried
out under this Act attracted a grant of
50 per cent. from the Imperial
Treasury, the further work envisaged
cannot really be said to be entirely of an
emergency stand-by nature, and the full
amount of the cost will, therefore, be
recoverable from the industry.
Clause 3 gives the Government power
to pay to the Government Loans Fund a
premium when it is desirable to redeem
a loan made to the Exchequer, or to vary
the terms as to the rate of interest of
such a loan. Substantial sums have been
borrowed by the Exchequer from the
Government Loans Fund for different
purposes, including the reconstruction of
R.U.C. barracks and the Bann Drainage
scheme, and the payment of such premiums is but one of the capital purposes
to which might be put the Loans
Guarantee windfalls referred to in
Clause 1.
Clause 4 extends the period of office of
the present Comptroller and Auditor-
General for a further year. Legislation
is necessary, as his age exceeds the statutory limit of 65 years. I beg to move.

Milk Bill.
Rev. PROFESSOR CORKEY: I hope
the hon. and learned Senator will not
think I am ungracious in making no
reference to his appeal, but in these
matters I am under the direction of the
Officers of the House. I rise now to move
the Second Reading of the Milk Bill.
The principal objects of this Bill may
be summarised as firstly, improving the
conditions under which milk is produced
on farms, and, secondly, improving the
quality of the milk that is distributed
to the consumer.
The war has brought about a
revolutionary change in the conditions of
milk production and distribution in Northern Ireland, and has led to the
necessity for introducing amendments in
the code of legislation dealing with the
production and marketing of milk that
was in existence prior to 1939.
Before the war there was a considerable surplus of Grade A, B and C milk,
for the production of which a licence was
required by the producer from the
Ministry of Agriculture. No licence was
required for the production and sale of
Grade D milk. The principal problem
of the Ministry of Agriculture before the
war was how to deal with the surplus of
high quality milk that was available
which had to be manufactured into butter
at a low price because no other outlet
could be found for it. That position has
now completely changed. There has been
an increase from 15 to 29 million gallons
in our home consumption of liquid milk.
In addition, we are shipping about 2
million gallons each winter to Scotland.
Instead of there being more Grade A. B
and C milk than required for human
consumption, as was the case before the
war, we are now in the position that
almost every drop of milk we produce is
required for liquid use at certain times
of the year. In these circumstances hon.
Senators will, I am sure, agree that the
time has come when every milk producer
in Northern Ireland should be made subject to control and inspection by the
Ministry of Agriculture. That is secured
by Clause 2 of the Bill.
Another great change that has been
occasioned by the war is in milk distribu-
Milk Bill-
Second Reading
14 DECEMBER 1943
T
tion. Before the war movements of milk
from place to place in Northern Ireland
were at a minimum. Over a large area
of the country farmers sold their milk
direct to consumers. Even in Belfast a
considerable part of the milk supply was
distributed in this way, while even the
milk sold by distributors was obtained
direct each morning from a comparatively
small number of farmers within a short
distance of the city. This is no longer
possible. A large portion of the milk
supply of Belfast is now bulked milk
obtained from milk depots established by
the Ministry of Agriculture a considerable distance from the city.
In order to safeguard the interests of
consumers in the new conditions of milk
distribution which have been created by
the war the Ministry of Agriculture propose to take power to require all milk
sold in certain areas to be either Grade
A milk, Grade B milk produced from a
single herd, or pasteurised milk. This
enabling power is given by Clause 1, and
the intention is to apply these powers in
the first instance to Belfast.
The great value of pasteurisation is
now generally recognised by the medical
profession. It greatly increases the safety
of milk without appreciably affecting its
nutritive qualities, and I trust that this
House will commend the efforts being
made by the Ministry to improve the
purity and standard of our milk supply.
There is only one other portion of the
Bill to which I need refer. Clause 3 deals
with the adulteration or impoverishment
of milk. Everyone will agree that this
is a matter of the greatest seriousness,
and there will be no sympathy with any
farmer or other person who is guilty of
such an offence. Considerable discussion
arose in another place as to the most
appropriate method of dealing with this
evil, and the Clause in its present form
represents an attempt on the part of the
Ministry to satisfy all interests, while at
same time ensuring that this abuse is
adequately controlled.
This is a Bill, the Second Reading of
which I move with the greatest satisfaction and confidence that it will commend
itself generally to the House. It is
evidence that the Ministry of Agriculture
are anxious to keep in line with the foremost thought in regard not only to the
production of milk, but also to the maintenance of the health of the community.
It is with that end primarily in view that
the Ministry are establishing these changes
in the code governing the production and
distribution of milk. I might also say
that the Bill commends itself in this way,
that it is not a very extreme measure.
It
is an illustration of what has been called
the inevitability of gradualness. The
Ministry desires to go slowly in a big
matter of this kind. This is a step forward, but, as hon. Members, I think, will
agree on careful reading of the Bill, it
involves only a very small step forward
in actual operation.
Question proposed.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I am sure we all
agree with the Leader of the House that
we all wish to see the best and the purest
possible milk supply for the people of
Northern Ireland. Whilst doing that we
have also another duty, and that is to
see that no injustice is allowed to be perpetrated on milk producers or on farmers
generally in Northern Ireland. We who
are not of the medical profession are put
in a very difficult position by this Bill,
because most eminent members of the
medical profession seem to differ, and
seem to differ to a very substantial degree,
as to the merit or otherwise of pasteurised
milk. On that point I am not prepared
to express any opinion, but it occurred to
me that the chief object in this Bill is to
provide a new system of pasteurisation of
milk in Northern Ireland. That is, I
think the House will agree, the main
object and the real object of this Bill,
but so far as I can see-and I have looked
through the Bill from the first Clause to
the last the word "pasteurisation" is
never mentioned.
My submission is this. If I am right
and that is the object, we are only passing
phraseology in this Bill, and we are delegating power to the officials in the Ministry of Agriculture to carry out the objects
and aims to which we are now supposed
to address ourselves. There is no question about pasteurisation of milk, and it
is not left in our hands. We are asked to
delegate that to officials in the Ministry
who will work it out how they choose in
a room in this building. I submit we are
doing a dangerous thing by continuing
day by day and week by week to delegate
what I suggest are our functions and
Milk Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
trusts to other people, no matter how high
their ideals may be.
There is another matter on which I
would speak with very great earnestness.
It has been the sacred law in England and
Ireland from time immemorial, a law that
has been treasured by the legal profession
and by the community, a law that has
been sacrosanct in England and Ireland,
that no man has the right to be punished
twice for the one offence. Not only in
this Bill is a milk producer to be punished
twice for the same offence, but he is to be
punished by two separate tribunals.
When this Bill was passing through another place this Clause was very seriously,
and, I think, rightly attacked, and when
it was in danger and jeopardy the
Attorney-General-for whom as a lawyer
I have the highest possible regard, and
as an advocate I would place on a very
high pinnacle-was set up to try to save
the situation.
I submit with all respect that the
chief criticism against this Bill was that
the suspension of the licence and the
confiscation of a man's livelihood, by
which he supports himself and his wife
and his family, was left to an individual,
however efficient, in an office in this
building-an official who did not hear
the evidence and who did not see the
demeanour of the defendant and the
witnesses in the witness-box. That is a
serious proposition, and it was rightly
seriously challenged in another place. I
say that the Attorney-General was put
up to save the situation, and though I
am speaking from recollection, for I
have not read the Acts of Parliament for
years, I without hesitation, and I
say
defy challenge,
challenge, that the Attorney-
General only partially stated the law,
and that he saved the Clause by a partial
statement of the law in another place,
instead of stating the whole, the complete, and the full law.
The Attorney-General, in order to get
the ear of the House and try to win
support for this Bill, contrasted the procedure under this Bill with the Licensing
and Motor Acts. He said that under the
Licensing Act or under the Motor Acts.
the magistrates of a summary court of
jurisdiction were bound to suspend the
licence. I challenge that. I say it is
not so at all. I say that these Acts of
Parliament expressly contain a proviso.
The Attorney-General quoted the Clause,
but not the proviso. Under the proviso,
if there are extenuating circumstances,
circumstances which condone the offence
and which show there was no malice and
no evil intent, that it was merely a
technical breach of the law, the court of
summary jurisdiction had power under
the Licensing Acts and the Motor Acts to
preserve that man's means of livelihood
and not confiscate his licence.
The Attorney-General is a very able
advocate, but he got this Clause through
in another place when it was seriously
challenged, and was likely to be lost
by stating only in part the law as it
stands with regard to the Licensing Act
and the Motor Acts. I say that one of
the great safeguards of the administration of the law both in England and in
Ireland has been that a person should be
tried in public, that there should be
public knowledge of what is going on,
and there is no principle of constitutional
law on which the English and the Irish
High Courts have been so jealous and so
anxious to preserve as freedom from the
suspicion that the law is carried out
privately thereby destroying the confidence of the people in the courts.
Surely, as a matter of common
sense, this is a matter for the
magistrates, for apart from their experience of the law, they are an efficient
tribunal of well qualified lawyers, daily
sifting, weighing, and measuring evidence, which makes them experts. They
see the witness in the box. They see the
defendant in the box, and they see him
cross-examined. They see his demeanour,
and they hear the pros and cons of the
matter. Surely they have a better opportunity of doing justice than a civil servant, who has not the necessary training
in the law and has no experience of the
sifting and measuring of evidence, and
no opportunity of seeing the witness in
the box. Surely the courts are competent. Surely we have sufficient confidence
in them not to deprive them of that
right.
On this point I would seriously press
for an amendment to this Bill to make
sure that the courts, in hearing these
offences, have the power to take any
extenuating circumstances into consideration on the question of the suspension of
a licence, which means perhaps the ruination of a man. A milk producer may
14 DECEMBER 1943
