Second Reading
of their rightful privilege and the duty
for which they are trained, and hand
over the liberty of persons and their
means of livelihood to an official of our
civil service, no matter how honourably
he may act, because he has no opportunity
of hearing a case and seeing the witnesses,
and of forming a conclusion as an expert
court would undoubtedly do.
have a farm of grass land which is very
suitable for producing milk, but absolutely unsuitable for tillage or arable purposes. If he is deprived of his licence.
his whole livelihood goes. In farming he
has no other alternative. I suggest that
the Bill be so altered in Committee that
where these tribunals find there are
extenuating circumstances, and where
there is no deliberate malpractice, but
only a technical offence, they should have
the power not to suspend a man's licence.
In case I should be misconstrued in
another place, let me say, that when I say
that the Attorney-General did not state
the whole of the law under the Licensing
Acts, I know that the magistrates are
bound to endorse a licence in certain convictions. They have no alternative except
under a proviso to the Section, which
provides that if there are extenuating
circumstances relating to the prosecution.
the magistrates need not endorse the
licence. At the same time I believeand I am speaking in the hearing of a
very experienced, able, and efficient
magistrate, Senator Leslie that under
the motoring laws the magistrates have
also a discretion, where a man's means
of livelihood is at stake, to safeguard his
licence and allow him to carry on his
means of livelihood.
I remember well appearing before
tribunals, of which Senator Leslie, a very
desirable magistrate, was an able, useful
and efficient member, and in saying this
I can gain no advantages from him now,
because his functions have been taken
away from him. I remember defending
a case under this very Act, the Food and
Drugs Act, in which the milk was not
up to the standard. The Bench heard
the case. They knew, as everyone knew,
that there was no tampering with the
milk, and obviously what had taken place
was that the milk in the can had not
been stirred. The tap was at the bottom,
and all the good milk went to the top.
When the policeman came along and took
a sample, he very naturally got a sample
of a pint or a half pint, or whatever he
took, which was below standard. The
magistrates in that case had the right to
act, and they did not convict.
I am very earnest about this, because
I think it would be a very serious thing
in Northern Ireland if we deprived the
existing courts of summary jurisdiction
B
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I am afraid
that I, like many Members of the House,
feel a very grave doubt about this pasteurisation business. I do think it is
possible that we may find ourselves taking an attractive short cut which may
land us in a very muddy field at the end
of it. I cannot help thinking that it is
a laudable ambition on the part of the
Ministry to keep the liquid milk market.
which they have found in Scotland, and
which has led them to feel that they
must keep step by step with the, to my
mind, rather rash and premature legislation which has been passed across the
Channel.
Like the previous speaker, I hesitate
to be dogmatic on such a highly technical
matter, but there is undoubtedly a very
wide difference of opinion among medical
and scientific men as to whether pasteurisation does not, in fact, destroy some
of the essential food qualities of the milk.
I would remind the House that the
medical profession changed its mind.
about these things. If we had been sitting 30 years ago and a similar measure
had been before us in such terms that it
would have destroyed every vitamin in
milk, there would not have been a
nutrition expert who would not have said
that it was perfectly safe to go on with
the Bill because they had not then discovered vitamins, and it may be the
same again.
One should be very reluctant to interfere by artificial means with essential
foodstuffs, and I still believe that the
right road is the road we were on. It is
a longer and more difficult road, but if
you go ahead, keep your herds clear of
disease, and keep your byres clean, you
will have the full value of your milk and
everything will be just as safe as it would
be the other way. I know it is a slower
and more difficult road, but I still feel
that it is the safer and better way. I
do not wish to put myself up against the
experts, except to point out that experts
do differ.
Milk Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
I should like now to refer to two
points to which the hon. Member opposite
(Mr. McAllister) alluded. The first is
the constitutional issue. The previous
speaker stressed very strongly and I
entirely agree with him-the great
danger of giving judicial functions to an
Executive Department. I know that
Parliament has given such powers in
previous cases; for instance in the 1931
Act. It has also been done in other
Acts, and even more extensively so on
the other side of the Channel. But the
mere fact that you have done it before,
in rather different circumstances, does
not necessarily justify you continuing
what is undoubtedly an unsound principle. Those of us who had a chance of
seeing fairly intimately the work of
officials in the Civil Services in continental countries have come back with the
greatest possible admiration and thankfulness for our own Civil Service. As
the previous speaker has stressed the
other side I wish to stress this side; that
undoubtedly after the war there will
have to be much more Government control, and the high quality of our Civil
Service will be even more important then
than it is now.
To give judicial functions to an
executive body is slowly but inevitably to
wreck that body. It has done it in the
Continental Civil Services, where they are
allowed very considerable judicial powers.
This may be a very small point in this
Bill, but it is the small leak in the dam
that pulls the dam down in the end, and
I think the time has come when we in
this House must set our face like flint
against courses of action which are contrary to the principles of the British
Constitution, and may have in the long
run the most deplorable effect on our
Civil Service, to whose honesty and impartiality we owe so much. I do not
believe that it will be difficult to draft
words into this Bill which will enable
the decision as to whether a licence should
be taken away or not to be made either by
a court of law or by some special tribunal,
if it is thought to be a technical
character, independent of the Ministry,
but do not start putting responsibilities
on your Ministry which it is impossible
for them to shoulder and which, in the long
run, will lead to their being less
efficient.
There is just one more point. It is
contained in sub-clause (2) of Clause 3,
a sub-clause which, I think, required
amendment in another place and which,
possibly in the heat of debate, was not
sufficiently considered. That sub-clause
lays it down that if a person is convicted
under the Sale of Food and Drugs Act,
or under the various sections of this Bill,
for the adulteration of milk, the Ministry
may revoke or suspend his licence after
conviction. That creates a very curious
state of affairs, and an embarrassing position for the magistrate who is trying the
The case is tried, the magistrate
case.
is satisfied that an offence has been committed, and he would normally proceed
to impose the penalty provided by the
law, but he does it without knowing that.
after he has done so, the Ministry will
not step in and inflict an extra, and in
most cases, much more severe penalty.
The natural effect on the mind of most
magistrates, and certainly on mine, would
be to be very reluctant to convict at all,
and very, very reluctant to impose any very
heavy penalty, because I should not know
what would happen to my victim when
the Ministry got hold of him. This would.
be set right if there was a special
tribunal, because it would be the tribunal
and not the Ministry that would make
the decision. That tribunal could take
into consideration what had happened in
the court of summary jurisdiction, and
take it into consideration in a proper
judicial manner.
I would ask the Minister if he would,
between now and the Committee stage,
consider whether on these two points the
Bill could be amended in some way, and
to facilitate discussion I will try to put
down an Amendment. Might I ask the
Minister that he should not, as Ministers
so often do, try to defend the departure
from a sound principle by saying that
it is only a little departure. To say it is
only a little departure is no excuse for
his illegitimate offspring, nor is it any
excuse to say that it has been done
before. It is an even less good excuse to
have done it before.
Mr. LESLIE: By leave of the House,

the Bill itself naturally awakens our sympathy. The Bill as designed, and so

must be lost. I do not see how you can
You cannot get over it.
help it.
expressed in its Title, is to make provision for improving the quality of the
milk supply, and that is a matter that
appeals to all of us. Everybody would
like our milk supply to be as good as it
can possibly be made. There are, it seems
to me, four points to consider as regards
milk.
First of all there is the quality of the
milk. That means, to take a convenient
example, the content of butter fat in the
milk as produced by the cow. How you
are going to attain that is a matter I need
not go into. It was said in another place,
in the course of a debate rather more
vigorous than usually takes place in another place, that you can add water to the
milk before the cow is milked. That is
worth consideration, because the speaker
showed that you could add water to milk
before the cow is milked by giving the
cow an excess of water or something containing water, and therefore that cow so
fed or not fed is not properly nourished
and does not produce milk of the proper
quality and with the proper amount of
butter fat. So much for quality.
Attempts to prevent tampering with
milk have been with us through many
years. Some of them have been successful, and others have not been so successful.
Attempts have been made by officers
operating under the Sale of Food and
Drugs Act, and by some under the Ministry of Agriculture. Complete success has
not yet been attained. Whether it will
be is another story. I have dealt with
the quality and purity of the milk.
There is the simple matter of cleanliness,
and that is a matter for the police.
There remains the fourth point, and
that is the freshness of the milk. I am
not posing as an authority on milk, but
I think it will be generally agreed that
one of the most desirable qualities
of milk, from the point of view of
consumption by young people, is that
the milk shall be fresh. Fresh milk
can be obtained in the country and
in the smaller towns, but, taking the
thing as a whole, it cannot be obtained in
Belfast. If you are going to bring your
milk, as you do bring it to Belfast, a

freshness must go, and again if you are

Apparently an attempt is to be made,

on the one side or the other. At the
moment pasteurised milk is sold, and
those of us who are not keen on it will
perhaps be told that we have been reading the wrong books about it. That may
be so.
One set of books may be right and
the other set wrong. For myself I cannot say that I am in favour of pasteurisation.

based on the infringement of the person.
The moment you provide that a man shall
not hold a licence then you are infringing
the liberty of the person. I think these
things have been accepted by Parliament.

illuminating. The Minister of Agriculture should tell us exactly where he
stands with regard to pasteurisation. If
he does not, I hope this House will realise
what it is doing if it passes this Bill. I
do not say it should not be passed, but
hon. Members should realise what they
are lending themselves to if they do they
are lending themselves to pasteurisation.
Paragraph 2 of the Memorandum deals
with Clause 1 of the Bill. It says:
Under the powers conferred by this
clause the Ministry proposes to make
regulations requiring all milk sold in
the Belfast area to be either (1) Grade
A milk; (2) Grade B milk from the
cows of a single herd; or (3) Pasteurised
milk.
That is very definitely in favour of
pasteurisation. They go further than
than that. In paragraph 5 of the Memorandum they say:-
At the outset there will be two exceptions to the policy of pasteurisation.
These two exceptions are Grade A and
Grade B, but that is only at the outset.
I take it that in a short time we shall
have full pasteurisation of Grade A,
Grade B, and every other grade of milk.
Pasteurisation will come gradually, but I
take it that eventually all grades of milk
will be pasteurised. It will only come
gradually because, in the first instance,
this Bill is going to be confined to the
Belfast area. I think we should make
up our minds as to whether or not we
are prepared to have pasteurisation. I
took a deep interest in the question some
years ago, and I came to the conclusion
that pasteurisation was not a success,
because by pasteurisation the nutritive
qualities of the milk were lost. Now

The MINISTER OF AGRICULTURE
(Rev. Robert Moore): I have been quite
interested in the course of the debate on
this Bill. I would like, if I may, to point
out that it is being brought before Parliament to enable my Ministry to deal
with an entirely new situation. As things
stand at the moment my Ministry buys
all the milk that is offered for sale in
the Six Counties, and that brings in a
very large class of producers who have
not been in the liquid milk trade
previously. Many of those producers are
'very small, and their byres and general
arrangements are not very satisfactory.
One of the main parts of this Bill-
Clause 2 is simply to give my Ministry
power through advice and consultation,
in the first instance at any rate, and later
on, if such advice is not sufficient, to
enable my Ministry to improve the conditions under which the milk is produced
on those farms. The only practicable way
we could see of doing this was to bring
these people under a similar system of
licensing to what holds in regard
to producers of Grade A, Grade B, and
Grade C milk. A certain flexibility, I
think, ought to be discernible in the
phraseology of that Clause, because my
Ministry is quite well aware that it will
be very difficult for some of these small
producers under war-time conditions to
comply with hard and fast regulations as
to the air-space of their byres and matters
of that kind which have been insisted
upon in regard to these other graded producers. If my Ministry is to have any
administrative authority at all I cannot
see how it can be appointed a licensing
authority to issue licences under even the
most flexible conditions and regulations
and have no power left to it for the
enforcement of those conditions and
regulations.
Mr. MCALLISTER: There are the
courts.
Rev. ROBERT MOORE: I do not
speak with any legal knowledge, but
purely as a layman. It seems to me that
this is purely an administrative function
and that we have to keep the judicial side
apart from what is a purely administrative side. Therefore, my Ministry
request that we should be given for purely
administrative purposes the right not only
to issue licences to those people, but if,

14 DECEMBER 1943
after conference with them and visits
from our inspectors and such other
friendly means as we can use in a particular case a producer absolutely fails to
comply with the most reasonable conditions for the production of milk for
liquid consumption, we should have the

Mr. McLAUGHLIN:
It could be
worked as in the case of the publicans.
Rev. ROBERT MOORE: There may

there is to be this power of revoking a
licence it should be in the hands of the
Ministry. I submit that it is only nonsense to issue a licence if there is no
means whatever of seeing that the terms
of the licence are complied with. I am
satisfied that 99 per cent. of the producers would prefer that authority should
be left in the hands of the Ministry whom
they can approach in the easiest fashion,
and whom they are approaching in connection with all sorts of matters every
day of the week.

i
Rev. ROBERT MOORE: The suggestion has been made that once a licence
has been issued it should be put under
the authority of the courts.
Mr. McALLISTER: Do you suggest

Rev. ROBERT MOORE: It is not a
matter of that at all. I have as much
confidence in the courts, and I think
everyone else has, as Members of this
honourable House seem to have in my
Ministry. I am perfectly satisfied having heard the views of representatives of
the producers and having met in conference the members of the Farmers'
Union and gone into all the provisions of
this Bill that the producers themselves if
this matter were put to them, whether
it meets with the views of hon. Senators
or not, would prefer to have it as it is
in the Bill.
With regard to the general question of
pasteurisation again I only come here as
a layman and I am not competent to
judge as between the parties who take
one view and parties who take another
view on this highly technical question.
But I would like this honourable House
to realise that the goal that my Ministry
has before it and towards which it is
hoped that this Bill will take us part of
the way, is not simply the production of
milk according to slipshod ways and then
to cure all its faults by pasteurisation.
That is not what is in the minds of my
Ministry at all. My Ministry is out to
lift the quality of milk production in
Northern Ireland to a point at least as
high as that in any other part of the
world, not even leaving out the muchboosted United States of America.
But for the duration of the emergency
a demand has arisen for milk. There is
a very large circulation of money. There
have been propaganda campaigns to induce the population to use more milk.
Schemes have been introduced for the
supply of free milk to children and to
others in the community, and there is
a demand from across the Channel for a
very large quantity of milk for liquid
All these factors
consumption there.
taken together have thrown a responsibility and burden on my Ministry to
secure a tremendously increased supply of
milk, and under the círcumstances it is
impossible in the time at our disposal to
grade up all that milk that is demanded
by circumstances at the moment to a
point where without pasteurisation we
would be satisfied that it was
a safe
source of supply for the community. I
would ask hon. Senators not to overlook
the fact that we are not proposing as was
suggested and urged very strongly in
another place that we should pasteurise
all milk. We are proposing to leave two
alternative sources of supply to grade
"A" milk, and we give every encouragement as a Ministry to the production of
that grade and quality of milk.
I would like to say, speaking generally,
that the primary function of my Ministry
is not what this emergency seems largely
to have converted it into, namely, a vast
trading concern. The primary function.
of, my Ministry is to help and encourage
the producer and to encourage production. I hope at least while I hold the
office I will never lost sight of that in
the snowdrift of accumulated legislation
Milk Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Rev. Robert Moore]
which has been steadily forced upon us
by the emergency. But we have to find
that increased supply somehow for the
community. It is impossible for us to
find it along the channels that have been
in existence for supplying the liquid
market. They are no longer able to meet
the whole demand. In order that that
other part of the supply that is necessary
may be made safe for the population, we
propose in this Bill to take powers to
schedule a certain area beginning at least
with the city and surroundings of Belfast,
and require that the milk sold in that
consuming area shall consist of one or
other of three grades-grade "A,"
grade "B" milk from a single herd, or
pasteurised milk. I do not think for one
moment that pasteurisation is going to
affect producers in any way-I think some
hon. Senators have that suspicion-because my Ministry buys all the milk, in
the first instance, from the producer, so
that his price or anything else is not to
be affected. It will be after the milk has
been delivered by the producer to my
Ministry that whatever else is done to it
will be done, and the producer whether
he is grade "C" or grade "B" or even
grade "A" producer will not be affected
at all.
Mr. LESLIE: During the war?
Rev. ROBERT MOORE: This is for
the duration of the war. The Emergency
Order enables us and requires us to purchase all milk.

limited to the duration of the war?
Rev. ROBERT MOORE: This Bill is
not limited to the duration of the war.
The trouble is that none of us can see
what will be the shape of things after the
emergency-
Mr. McALLISTER: Or the Government.
Rev. ROBERT MOORE: I am not

Parliament that passes this Bill can annul

producers who deliberately go out of their

by the removal of part of the milk or by

commodity-

the number of people who will be

are buying milk all over the Province,
and that milk is being sampled, and
tested, and examined from day to day at
our central depots, and there is no wholesale adulteration of milk. It is only a
very small number of producers who ever
resort to this, and I would respectfully
submit that the person who persists in
that sort of thing is not a desirable
person. It may be taking away his liveli
hood, or whatever construction we may
put on it, but he is not a very desirable
person to have in the business of milk
production. I would further point out
that the penalty suggested is only a permissive one. It says the Ministry may,
but not the Ministry shall. I would ask

creameries. We all regret very much the
position in which the creameries found
themselves before the present arrangements were brought into operation. It may
be some gain from a purely theoretical
point of view, and probably from a practical point of view to consider the future
position of the creameries, but I think
the position that agitates the mind of the
individual producer is what price he is
going to get for his milk. I do not think
the hon. Senator would for a moment
suggest there is likely to be any comparison at all between the price a producer would receive for his milk which
he sent to a creamery and the price he
would obtain for that milk if sold for
liquid consumption.

Question to Minister
16 DECEMBER 1943
THE SENATE.

Thursday, 16th December, 1943.
The Senate met at a Quarter before
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
QUESTION TO MINISTER.
Customs Examinations at Border
Stations.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to ask
the Leader of the House whether the
Government will make representations to
the Imperial Government with a view to
expediting the customs examination at
the border stations, and, where practicable, to have this examination carried out
on trains while in motion and thus save
valuable time and fuel.
At the present time there is a great deal
of time lost at these border stations between Belfast and Dublin with the
customs examination and the hold-up at
Goraghwood. I travelled from Dublin to
Belfast a very short time ago and I would
say we were held up almost an hour at
Goraghwood station by the customs
examination. I calculate that on three
journeys from Belfast to Dublin and
back, there are at least four hours lost
each day, and I think it is time there
was something done with regard to it.
And I think it is time there were very
strong representations made to the
Imperial Parliament to make arrangements whereby so much valuable time
will not be lost and particularly from
the point of view of saving fuel. You
can just imagine how much fuel is being
wasted by these engine furnaces going all
the time at these stations whilst the
examination of customs is going on.
In passing I may say I cast no reflections on the officials who carry out the
work. They do it to the best of their
ability. They have an unpleasant job
and they really do it the best they can.
I do say it is too bad that in winter time
people going from Dublin to Belfast
should be held up in dark trains for
almost an hour. If the Imperial Parlia
ment were really taking an interest in
the people of the Six Counties here I
think they would very soon devise ways
and means to expedite this examination.
The Eire Government when representations were made to them with regard to
the customs examination at the junctions
arranged to have it carried out in the
train at Dundalk and Drogheda and vice
versa. I suggest that the customs examination could very easily be carried out
from Portadown to Goraghwood or from
Goraghwood to Portadown, and that
where passengers got off at such stations
the customs authorities could easily
arrange for a table to be at that junction
for the examination of passengers getting off the train at that junction, and
carry on the examination of passengers
to Belfast whilst the train is in motion,
thus saving valuable time and fuel as
well. I sincerely trust we will get a
very favourable answer from the Leader
of the House and that this Government
on behalf of the citizens will make very
strong representations to the Imperial
Parliament to have this scandal-I can
call it nothing else-stopped.
I am
certain that if the same thing occurred in
England it would not last a week because the English people would immediately rise and ask the Government to
facilitate them.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I would just like
to supplement the remarks of the hon.
Senator McLaughlin. He has referred
to the fact that people are detained on
the journey from Belfast to Dublin and
vice versa. That is a very substantial
grievance, but what about those people
who travel from urban and rural areas?
If the last train into Belfast is an hour
late they are not only delayed an hour,
but they have no means of getting to
their destination that night at all. They
are held up in Belfast. That is a very
serious grievance and one of which we
hear complaints. Unless there is a very
substantial reason given by the Minister
why this examination could not be carried
on on the train I think the Government
in the interests of the people should
make the strongest possible representations to the Imperial authorities in
England.
I would like the Minister to say if the
reply is not favourable why, if it is possible for the Free State Government to
make this examination while the train
is in progress between Goraghwood and
Dundalk or wherever it is made-if it is
possible for them to convenience not only
their own citizens but the citizens of
Northern Ireland travelling on their railway, why could not the Imperial Government take some steps to meet this very
distinct grievance.
Financial Provisions Bill 422
Question to Minister
THE SENATE
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE
(Rev. Professor Corkey): I have been
asked by the Minister of Commerce to
make the following reply to this question:
The possibility of expediting examination by H.M. Customs on cross-Border
trains has already been the subject of
discussion between the Ministry of
Commerce and the Customs authorities,
who take the view that the present system
does not lead to undue delays, and that a
thorough search is not practicable on
trains in motion between Belfast and
Goraghwood and still less practicable
between Portadown and Goraghwood as
suggested by the hon. Senator. The
trains are usually crowded, the distance is
short, and there are no facilities in the
trains for personal searches.
The Government regrets any inconvenience caused to passengers, but shares the
view of the Customs authorities that the
prevention of smuggling of commodities.
in short supply is a matter of first importance in time of war.
Mr. McALLISTER: May I ask the
Leader of the House if he will place on
the Table of the House and before Members of Parliament any correspondence or
representations that have passed between
the Minister of Commerce and the
Imperial authorities?
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I quite agree
with what has been stated, but I do say
there is plenty of time between Belfast
and Goraghwood to carry out the customs
examination and if necessary they could
have a table at the Junction
suggestedas I
Mr. SPEAKER: The hon. Senator
can only ask a question.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I am doing that.
He should take into consideration the
fact that Belfast passengers anxious to
get home that night wonder whether they
will be able to get their connection. On
the particular train I was on last week
people from the North were most uneasy,
fearing that the train would not get into
Belfast in time to get their connection
home and that they might have to stay
in Belfast all night. I think something
should be done to facilitate them.

am not sure whether that will be practicable or not. In any case the suggestion
made by the hon. Senator will be passed

Youth Welfare Bill [Senate].

Order for consideration of Commons
Amendment read.
CLAUSE 2 (Establishment and functions of Youth Committee).
shall
(2) The Youth Committee
consist of twelve persons (not being
officers of a government department)
appointed by the Minister of Education.
(3) The persons to be appointed as
members of the Youth Committee shall
be persons specially interested in the
objects of this Act and the principal
Act, whether as being members of
education authorities and other local
authorities and of voluntary youth
organisations, or otherwise.
(4) Persons appointed as members of
the Youth Committee shall hold office
for such terms, and upon such conditions as to retirement, ceasing to hold
office, and reappointment, as the
Minister of Education may from time
to time prescribe.
Commons Amendment:
Page 2, line 23, leave out the words.
"not being officers of a government
department.
""
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I beg to
move, That the House do agree with
the Commons in the said Amendment."
I may say that this little Amendment,
emanating from the Commons, is really
consequential on an Amendment which
was passed by this House. These words
form a very proper part of the original
Bill where civil servants were mentioned
in it. This reference to them is rather
out of place and altogether uncalled for.
The effect of the Amendment is that a
civil servant now appointed on the Youth
Committee will be appointed in his own
personal capacity and not as the representative of any particular Ministry. I
think it would be very undesirable that
there should be any sort of legislative
ban put on the good work of civil servants
who happen to be keenly interested in
child welfare or any other sort of social
effort. I am sure that this Amendment
will commend itself to the House.
Mr. LESLIE: I think that this parnot
ticular phase of the question was
considered when the matter was before
the House. It was never the intention
of the House to exclude a man from being
appointed on the Committee just because
he was a civil servant. If he sits there, as
I understand it, he will sit purely in his
personal capacity, as a man acquainted
with the problems of youth, a man who
happens to be a civil servant, but will not
sit there because he is a civil servant. I
do not see that there is the smallest reason
why this House should not agree with the
House of Commons. It is very encouraging to this House to know that those who
sit elsewhere are. beginning to take a little
practical interest in legislation. This
particular Bill is an example of the kind
of awareness on the part of back benchers
which, I feel, will be of benefit in the
future.
Mr. McALLISTER: My criticism of
the original clause was directed against
the fact that civil servants were selected
from a particular Ministry on the committee because they were civil servants.
If a man, be he civil servant or not, is
giving good service towards the welfare
of youth, I would be the last to oppose
the appointment of that man who is
willing to contribute his services to the
public.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I think the.
amendment does, in a far more effective
manner, what I was attempting to do in
the compromise I suggested to the House
when the Bill was before it. Therefore,
I have very great pleasure in supporting
the amendment.
Amendment agreed to.
Commons' amendment:
Page 2, line. 30, at end add "and the

to secure that one half of the members

are, under forty years of age.'

Commons in the said amendment."

of the Ministry to have a fair represen-

tation of young men and women on the

to the
Clause. I do not think any serious

It is a rather unusual type of wording to

Rev. Professor CORKEY--he would

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I have a
great deal of sympathy with the Amendment. I think we must have a considerable fusion of youth in a committee of
this nature. At the same time we should
remember that the number of responsible
persons with a really intimate knowledge
of youth welfare problems is not very
large. To enact that the half of the
committee should fall into an age group,
some of whom will not possess very long
experience of youth problems, is perhaps
stretching the point too far. Will a man

Order for resuming debate on Second
Reading read.
Question again proposed, "That the
Bill be now read a Second time."
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: On the last day
this Bill was debated the suggestion was
made that the criticism offered showed
want of confidence in the Ministry of
Agriculture. As far as I am concerned.
-and I think I speak for the vast
majority-it is not want of confidence in
the Ministry of Agriculture. From my
experience I consider the Ministry are
doing excellent work. I have found the
officials to be courteous and anxious to
carry out the work as it should be carried
out, and at times they have a very difficult
task to perform. Sometimes they have
very thankless jobs to do, but they carry
them out as best they can. They always
see to it that things are done judiciously
and fairly. In all matters the Ministry
have tried to be most helpful.
It has always been held that under our
Constitution each citizen should have the
right to defend himself in open court.
The handing of power to the Ministry to
revoke or suspend licences behind closed
doors is, to my mind, not democratic. It
savours a little of Hitlerism. If we look
at the licensing laws we find that a
publican may be fined and cautioned. He
may be convicted a second time and
cautioned, but he knows quite well that
if he is convicted a third time the court
has power automatically to revoke his
licence. If, however, it is revoked he has
Milk Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. McLaughlin]
power to go before the Court of Appeal
and place his case before it.
I hold that if a man has been penalised
in a court it is not just for the Ministry
to have power to revoke or suspend his
licence. I do not think it is fair that,
behind closed doors, any Ministry should
have power to decide the fate of a man
earning his living by a particular trade.
If a court has power to deal with a milk
vendor and can fine or inflict a penalty
on him, it should also have power to
revoke his licence, if necessary. I think
when the case is stated and the facts are
known to the judicial authority, that the
magistrate will know the type of man he
has to deal with. If the man is criminally or illegally tampering with the milk,
the magistrate will know how to deal with
him, and he should have the power to
revoke the licence instead of it being left
to the Ministry to decide behind closed
doors. From that point of view, I suggest that the Ministry should not have
power to revoke any man's licence behind
closed doors.
We have heard that what is called the
Farmers' Union-I understand it gets a
subsidy of £900 a year-has no objection
to the Ministry having power to revoke
licences. I often ask myself what is this
Farmers' Union, and what it really represents. I know that in a great many areas
in my part of the country it has no franchise at all. The Farmers' Union cannot
take upon itself to speak for the farmers
throughout the country. I am quite
satisfied that if the farmers were asked
they would be quite prepared and anxious
that the matter should be left to the
courts, and that they would prefer to be
tried publicly. If a man deserves his
licence to be suspended, it should certainly be suspended by the court, and, if
necessary, room left for him to appeal,
just the same as is done in reference to
the licensing trade.
Take the case of motor cars. People
have to apply for a driving licence, but
no one can take a licence away from any
person execept the courts. The courts
have the right not alone to fine or sentence a person, but to revoke, his licence.
I have never heard anyone objecting to
that procedure, but a person would object
if, after a penalty was imposed by the
court, the Ministry, behind closed doors,
took away the licence.
The question of creameries was raised
on the last occasion on which this matter
was debated. It is only right that the
public should get an assurance-I ask for
such an assurance-that under this Bill
creameries will not be affected. I think
it is only right we should ask for that
assurance..
Mr. STEVENSON: I welcome this
Bill, and if it is firmly carried out it will
cause a revolution with regard to milk.
Very clever arguments have been put forward in regard to the revoking of licences,
but I cannot see the point of them at all.
The magistrates, as I understand the
position, decide a case purely on the question of the quality and purity of the milk,
but they do not know the conditions
under which it was produced or handled.
On the other hand the Minister, through
his inspector, knows all about the herds
and the conditions under which the cows
are milked. He also knows whether a man
is genuinely trying to do his best. I think
the Minister is the fit and proper person
to decide whether or not a licence should
be revoked.
We have heard about motoring licences
and the licences of publicans, but, on the
other hand, we have the Minister of
Food who issues licences. In such cases
the magistrates impose a fine, but the
Minister has the right to revoke the
licence, if necessary. That is a similar
case to what we have here. The Minister
of Agriculture has the power to revoke.
licences in reference to the sale of
poultry, bacon and other commodities.
As I see the position, the trouble with
this Bill is that the Government may not
deal firmly enough with the milk producer. In many districts the grading
of milk is a perfect farce. The milk is
handled under filthy conditions, and no
attempt is made to keep it to the one
herd. It is simply filled up at the
nearest farm house or creamery, as the
case may be. Another matter that
strikes me forcibly with regard to the
handling of milk is the present custom
of sending milk to the creameries. I
live in the depths of the country and
these things are brought under my notice
every day. In the first place the cans
are put where there is filth and then
they are wheeled to the county road and
set in filth. A lorry then comes along
and the cans are lifted on to it. When
the first layer of cans is on, the second

is filth adhering to the bottom of the
cans.

not see why the same custom should not

QUESTION TO MINISTER.
Issue of Residence Permits.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether he has seen
a report of a case the Crown v. Matthew
Henderson heard at Enniskillen Petty
Sessions on Monday, 6th December, in
respect of the claim of the police that the
defendant had failed to apply for a
Temporary Residence Permit; whether the
Resident Magistrate held that Mr.
Henderson had been a resident of Northern Ireland before January, 1940, and
had held the usual Identity Certificate,
and was entitled to a renewal of it
recently, and if the facts are as stated,
whether it is now proposed to issue an
Identity Certificate in this case.
I should have requested that the
Ministry give the police instructions not
to hinder the issue of an identity certificate forthwith in view of the legal proceedings. This man Henderson has, I
understand, been deprived of that
advantage, and recently at the Petty
Sessions at Enniskillen he was summoned
for not having one. The report of the
proceedings shows that the police summons alleged that Matthew Henderson,
of 4 Market Street, Enniskillen, not being
a resident of Northern Ireland immediately prior to the first of January, 1940,
required a residence permit and had not
one. Major Dickie said it was hard on
people who were natives of Northern Ireland to have to register, but it was the
law, and his immediate concern was
whether defendant had been resident on
the required date. Defendant, in his
evidence, stated he was a native of Enniskillen. He first left it at Easter, 1940,
and was away on and off about two years.
Previous to that he always resided in the
country. In December, 1939, he promoted an entertainment in the town, and
so forth. In view of the fact that this
man has been resident in Northern Ireland and is practically a native of
Northern Ireland, I suggest he should
not be deprived of this identity certificate,
and that the Government should issue
instructions that an identity certificate.
should be issued to him as a native of the
Six Counties.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Rev.
Professor Corkey): The following is a
statement of the facts that has been
given to me in regard to this case. I may
say I have seen the newspaper report of
the case against Matthew Henderson for
not being in possession of a Residence
Permit, and am satisfied that the police
acted properly in bringing the matter
before the Court.
The resident magistrate who heard the
case was reasonably satisfied that Henderson had been normally resident in Northern Ireland before the 1st January,
1940, and he dismissed the case on the
merits.
With regard to the last part of the
question, Henderson is now in possession
of a temporary National Registration
Identity Card, and in view of the Court
decision, the question of issuing a
standard card will be considered by the
National Registration Authorities if
application is made to the National
Registration Officer of the district in
which Henderson resides.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Thank you very
much.

Planning Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
Planning (Interim Development) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I intimated
to the Senate that it was intended that
the Minister of Home Affairs should be
here when this Bill came before the
Senate for consideration. Unfortunately
it is impossible for Mr. Lowry to be here
to-day, but as in all probability the
principle of this Bill will commend itself
to the House, and as we hope that Mr.
Lowry will be able to be here to defend
the details of the Bill in the Committee
stage it will probably be the wish of the
House that we should proceed with the
business and take the Second Reading of
the Bill to-day.
The Bill, I may say in a word, proposes
to do two things: first of all, to prevent
erratic and ill-considered development
during the interim period and, in the
second place, to make possible well considered development during that period
and to see that such development would
harmonise with the plans of the local
authorities when these plans are forthcoming. It is not, I think, necessary for
me in such an assembly as this to make
town and
any elaborate defence of
country planning. The principles of such
a policy were accepted at Westminster as
long ago as 1909, and by the Northern
Ireland Parliament over 12 years ago.
Since 1931, the strength of the case for
requiring land to be utilised in accordance with a plan which shall have regard
to the health and well-being of the community has steadily increased. To-day,
with the prospect of post-war building
and reconstruction, there is an
whelming case for taking every precaution we
that future
development will be on sound lines, and
that building will not take place in the
haphazard manner which has so often
characterised it in the past.
an almost
We are faced with
exampled opportunity, which may not
recur, and we should be guilty of a grave
dereliction of duty if we were not to seize
it. If the natural beauties of our Province are impaired, we suffer an injury
which, once wrought, cannot easily be
remedied. Prevention is of literally
vital importance, if cure is impossible, or
nearly impossible. Planning is also, I
believe, a sound financial proposition
from the point of view of the authorities
responsible for such services as roads,
can
to
ensure
overunwater supply and sewerage.
Moreover,
if our people are to be properly housed,,
attention must be paid not merely to the
construction of the houses themselves,
but the environment in which they are
placed.
This Bill deals with interim development, that is to say, development which
takes place during the interval between
the passing of a resolution to adopt or
prepare a planning scheme, and the time
when such a scheme can be brought into
operation. It will be evident to Members
of this House that the preparation of a
scheme, dealing, as it must, with matters
of some considerable complexity and
affecting many interests, is bound to take
a certain amount of time, though the
period should not, of course, be unduly
prolonged. During this period, pending
the final approval of a scheme, development must be controlled: otherwise, by
the time the scheme has come into operation, it may be too late for the planning
authority to take effective action in preventing undesirable development without
incurring considerable expenditure by
It is, moreover,
way of compensation.
to the advantage of persons contemplating the development of their property
that they should be in a position to
obtain from the planning authority some
indication as to whether the proposed
development is likely to contravene the
scheme which the authority has in mind.
That, briefly, is the case for the control
of interim development.
Under the 1931 Act, interim development can be controlled only where the
local authority has passed a resolution to
prepare or adopt a planning scheme.
Only a few of our authorities have passed
such resolutions, though many of them
have employed planning officers, and
considerable progress has been made in
the preparation of skeleton plans for
One
particular areas of the Province.
effect of this Bill, if passed, will be to
bring the whole Province under interim
development control after the lapse of a
period of three months from the commencement of the Act.
Once an
area has become subject to
interim development control, development can proceed only in accordance with
an Interim Development Order which
will be made by the Ministry of Home
Affairs. Such an Order "may itself permit the development of land either unconditionally or subject to such condi-
Planning Bill—
Second Reading
21 DECEMBER 1943
tions as may be prescribed in the order,
or may empower any local authority
specified in the order to permit the development of land in accordance with"
the provisions now set out in Clause 2 of
the Bill. Normally, the practice will be
to empower the, local authority to grant
or withhold permission or to grant permission subject to conditions. There
will be a statutory obligation on the local
authority, in certain cases, to consult the
Planning Commission, which is an expert
body in possession of the information
necessary to enable it to view the planning of the Province as a whole, in
proper perspective. The Government
believe that the advice of this body will
be of the greatest value to local authorities, and it is hoped that they will pay
due regard to its recommendations.
The two cases in which consultation
must take place are set out in paragraph
(2) of Clause 2. The first is where the
plan of one local authority must be coordinated with that of another authority,
and the other is where the locality to be
planned is one to which special architectural, historic or artistic value attaches. It is undesirable, for example,
that one local authority should encourage
industrial development in an area adjoining that which another authority proposes to reserve as a purely residential
area. As regards the other type of case
I need hardly impress upon the House
the importance of ensuring that in planning-for example, the Coast Road area
or an historic town like Carrickfergus
the local authority should have the
advantage of the expert advice of such a
body as the Planning Commission.
Where an application for permission
to develop is received by a local authority,
the authority may either give a decision
upon it, or may, unless the applicant can
show that he can carry out the proposed
development immediately, postpone the
application. If they adopt the latter
course, the applicant has the right of an
.appeal against the postponement, to a
court of summary jurisdiction.
If, however, within two months, an
applicant has not had his application
determined or referred to the Ministry
under Clause 5, or has not received a
notice of postponement, his application.
is deemed to have been refused. This
provision is of considerable value to the
applicant in that it not only acts as an
inducement to local authorities to determine applications as expeditiously as
possible, but also enables the applicant to
know where he stands in the matter of
development. The provision ensures also
that where permission is granted, the
applicant will have documentary proof,
and will not have to rely on the absence
of a document of refusal, as hitherto he
has had to do.
Where an applicant is aggrieved by a
decision of the local authority on an
interim development application, he may
appeal either to the Ministry of Home
Affairs or to an independent person
appointed by the Ministry, according as
he may himself elect.
Clause 4 of the Bill relates to the
enforcement of interim development
control. If, in defiance of an Interim
Development Order, development takes
place, the local authority may take such
measures as may be necessary to deal
with such development; that is, by
removing buildings erected otherwise than
in accordance with permission granted
under an order. Experience in Great
Britain has proved that a provision on
these lines is essential. There is an appeal
to a court of summary jurisdiction against
any action proposed to be taken by a
local authority under Clause 4.
It is
Clause 5 of the Bill proposes that the
Ministry should be enabled to require
interim development applications to be
referred to it for decision where it is
expedient, having regard to the public
interest, that applications should be so
referred. As I mentioned earlier, considerations may arise in connexion with
an interim development application which
transcend purely local interests.
desirable that applications of that kind
should be determined in such a way as
not to prejudice the master plan of the
Province as a whole which will gradually
be built up from the plans of the
individual local authorities. The right of
an appeal of an independent person is
provided against any decision of the
Ministry under this provision.

Clause 6 is one of the most important
as well as the most complicated Clauses of
the Bill, and I have no doubt that Members of the House will very properly
scrutinise its provisions with the utmost
care. An important clue to the understanding of the. clause lies in the distinction between loss or damage which has
Planning Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
been suffered, and prospective loss or
damage. Paragraphs (1) to (5) deal with
loss or damage which has been suffered,
and paragraph (6) with prospective
damage. In the former paragraphs, the
payment of compensation is made mandatory; for prospective damage which a
person is likely to suffer, payment is left
to the discretion of the local authority.
The extent of prospective damage, cannot,
in the nature of the things, be accurately
determined, and it would, therefore, be
inequitable to make. payment of compensation in respect of it mandatory. It must
be remembered, however, that compensation in respect of most kinds of damage
will be payable as a matter of right under
a planning scheme, and it will, therefore,
be open to persons whose damage was prospective at the interim development stage,
to make a claim when the damage has
actually materialised, and can, therefore,
be assessed with reasonable accuracy by
an arbitrator.
Hardship may arise from either of two
causes at the interim development stage.
First, the refusal of permission or the
imposition of conditions; and secondly,
the revocation or modification of a permission already granted. The former is.
dealt with in paragraph (1), (5) and (6)
of Clause 5, and the latter in paragraphs
(2), (5) and (6). Paragraphs (1) and
(2) relate to abortive expenditure and
abortive contracts. Under each paragraph the person affected is entitled to
recover from the local authority an
amount equal to the abortive expenditure incurred, or such sums as may reasonably have been paid by him in discharge
of any liability arising as the result of
an abortive contract.
Paragraph (5) gives a right to compensation, fixed by an arbitrator, to persons whose land has been injuriously
affected as the result of a decision of a
local authority in relation to interim
development. Faragraph (6), as I have
explained, authorises the payment, at the
discretion of the local authority, of contributions in respect of prospective loss
or damage. Paragraph (8) is designed
to prevent the payment of compensation
twice in respect of the same matter or
thing.
I hope that this brief summary may
assist the House in the understanding of
the somewhat complicated provisions of
this Clause. It is proposed, on the Committee stage of the Bill, to move a number
of amendments, mainly of a drafting
nature, with the object of simplifying the
provisions of this Clause.
I believe this Bill will prove a serviceable instrument for enabling a proper
control to be exercised over the land
during the critical period which must
inevitably elapse between the commencement of control and the approval of
planning schemes. At the same time, the
Bill, as I have been at pains to point out,
contains adequate safeguards against any
arbitrary action on the part either of the
local authorities or of the. Ministry of
Home Affairs. As I have said, we are
facing a time of unexampled opportunity.
The rebuilding of those parts of our city
which have been destroyed by the ravages
of war, and the very considerable building
development which is bound to follow the
cessation of hostilities, alike afford us a
chance which may never recur-at least
in our lifetime-of ensuring that prudence, foresight and informed intelligence
are applied to the planning of our towns
and countryside. Let us not be slow to
seize the opportunity, and so to earn the
gratitude and respect of those who will
come after us. I beg to move, "That
the Bill be now read a Second time."

Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: It
is right to point out that this Bill has a
very respectable precedent, because in
1580, Queen Elizabeth by proclamation
attempted to stop any further building
in London. The last time I was in
London it did not appear to me that the
proclamation had had much effect. I do
not think that any legislation introduced
in any Parliament will stop houses being
built in any particular place if a sufficient
number of people wish to build them
there. I must confess that I view with
some apprehension a rush to build houses
after this war and before the effects of
the war have worn off; that is to say,
before the Government and the local
authorities have really settled down to
peace-time conditions. One must remember that there are large numbers of people
of importance in this country who are
serving in the Forces. They will not
return immediately to civil life, and it
may be that their views should be heard
when the eventual planning of the
country is being decided.
Then, again, it seems to me that our
industrial position will, to say the
least of it, be uncertain. We shall not
know to what extent we have lost our
export trade, and it will be some time
before we can tell what factories we
should have or how we should plan our
existence. I view with great apprehension
the future of this country and the world
for some time after this war. I must
confess that these facts alone give me
some reason for believing that a planning
Bill is necessary, even though it may
only have the effect of temporarily
causing a certain amount of slowing up
of the process of rebuilding. When I use
the words "slowing up of the process of
rebuilding" I do not mean that the
building of houses which are urgently
required for housing the population now
should be slowed up. If this Bill has
that effect it will be most injurious. I
think there are certain types of building
which should be slowed up.
We must also consider the question of
drainage, the utilisation of agricultural
land to the best purpose, and so on.
These are matters which would restrict
ribbon development. I think that ribbon
development is often spoken of in the
wrong sense. A few houses adjoining a
road would be a very narrow ribbon.
What the right hon. Gentleman (Mr.
Leslie) said is true to some extent. You
must have ribbon development unless you
are going to have development which is
entirely central. If that is done you
block the main arteries of a city, or its
main breathing space. The question is,
where is the ribbon going to be? Is it a
ribbon running round the perimeter of
the whole city and, therefore, making use
of all available civic facilities, or is it to
be a ribbon stretching far into the
country, not just one running along a
road but a deep one? If that happened
new facilities such as drainage and so on
would have to be pushed into the country
and they might not be an economic
proposition.
It is essential that local authorities
should get together and from their point
of view alone decide what form of development building will take in the
future. There are other points of view
to be considered because the local authorities are not the only people concerned
with building. As the right hon.
Gentleman (Mr. Leslie) stated it is
important to consider the point of view
of the man who is going to live in the
house. It is also important to consider the
point of view of the man who owns the
land, because until land is nationalised
in the sense that it is removed from
private control altogether-I hope it will
never be removed from private control
altogether, because I do not think it
would be economically used in any other
Planning Bill-
Second Reading
21 DECEMBER 1943
way-you must consider the economic
factors which affect the holding of land.
If the Government are determined to
remove land from private ownership they
should say so straight out. I believe
that it is important from the point of
view of agriculture, as well as from the
point of view of general economics, that
capital should be attracted back into the
land and not driven away from it. It
seems to me that the danger of these
planning Bills is that by throwing everything into the hands of the local authorities I have the greatest respect for
these bodies in another sphere-you are
practically telling a man who has capital
to invest that it would be better for him,
to keep his money in his pocket, to put
it into stocks and shares or something
else out of which he can get it quickly,
rather than that he should put it into
land which later he will have no control
over. That is a very dangerous attitude
of mind for any Government to adopt.
I am afraid this Bill goes very near the
danger mark when it assumes, as I think
it does, that after the war most building
will be placed in the hands of the local
authorities. That is a very dangerous
attitude of mind because as costs come
down I think that as in the past the
private owner of land will play a very
big and a very disinterested part in
building houses for the people to live in.
In the course of my duties as chairman
of the local Employment Assistance
Board I have not found that the small
houses owned by the private individual
are any more excessive in rent than those
owned by the local authority.
impression is that it is the other way
round. The Rent Restrictions Actperhaps I am exceeding the liberty

me to have done a great deal in the past.
to prevent the building of small houses.
My
There are other economic questions
which affect the erection of houses. It is
no use asking a private individual to build
a house if he knows he will get a rent
which will only pay for the cost of repairs.
So far as the local authorities are concerned, you may say that it does not
matter to them whether a house is an
economic proposition or not. If you say
that, then you must say that it does not
matter to the ratepayers what amount of
rates they have to pay. It is essential
that the local authorities should finance
their undertakings in a reasonable way,
and that they are reasonable to the ratepayers. That being so, I do not see that
the local authorities are really in a much
better position than the land owner in so
far as the building of a house is concerned.
I know many houses belonging to land
owners for which the rent charged is £1
per month, whereas the local authorities
are getting 7s per week. If one reviews
the whole problem, I do not think it can
be said that the function of the private
land owner as a builder has ceased.
What are we to do? We must have
some Interim Bill which after the war
will, without prejudice to the future
development of the country, stop expansion in certain directions. I should raise
very little objection to this Bill if it were
not for the fact that it does not compel
local authorities to produce a scheme of
any kind. It may be that this Bill is
going to put a permanent fetter on building development in the Province. If that
is so, then I should be very much against
the Bill, and I would support the right
hon. Gentleman in his observations.
At the beginning of this Bill it is
assumed that every local authority has
passed some planning scheme, but we
know perfectly well that this assumption
is unfounded in fact, and that it may be
many years not only before there is any
question of interim development but
before there is any plan preceding the
interim development. What is going to
happen if the local authorities produce
no plans at all? Nothing. It may be
assumed that the local authorities which
have not acted under the principal Act
for many years, and have had no desire
and no reason to produce a plan, will see
no more reason to produce plans under
this Bill than before.
What position are we going to be in?
For years and years, perhaps for 20 or
30 years, after the war other countries
will be going ahead with development
and taking steps which are not being
taken in Northern Ireland where we are
going to have the greatest difficulty in
producing adequate building. If the
Government were going to introduce some
amendment in the Bill which would
compel local authorities to produce a plan
of some sort within six months after the
war I would support the Bill. It would
then be an interim measure, but there is

they possess, should get the right people
to help them, and they should produce a

much speculation. They should encourage
capital to flow back to the land and not
away from it. They should treat the
country as if they were partners in this
matter. They should realise that building
is a question of partnership between the
local authorities, the man who owns the
land and the man who lives in the house.
Each partner should have some form of
support shown by the others. If the
Government do these things then I think
they will have the support not only of
the House but the country as well.
The LORD MAYOR OF BELFAST
(Sir Crawford McCullagh): I can find
fault in some directions with this Bill.
It is, after all, a mild Bill. In some
directions it goes too far, and in other
directions, in my opinion, not far enough.
I would not go so far as to oppose this
measure or support the amendment proposed. There are many good points about
it. The Government have met the Corporation of Belfast in what they asked
for, and I can say that the Belfast Corporation are perfectly satisfied with the
Bill. I wish to make that perfectly clear.
The hon. and gallant Senator behind me
has laid great stress on private enterprise.
He seems to think that this Bill is designed to allow local authorities to build
houses. Nothing of the sort. No sane
man to-day would suggest that in the
future only local authorities should build
houses. You have to encourage private
enterprise, and the Bill in no degree stops
or prevents private individuals and
builders from erecting houses.
As far as Belfast is concerned, there is
no doubt it has a problem before it. The
Corporation will be faced with great difficulties. We all know the number of
houses that have been demolished and
are aware of the vacant spaces all over
the city. In the matter of reconstruction all this will have to be taken into
consideration, and possibly open spaces
will be left where houses stood. The
biggest problem we have to face is where
are we going to build houses? Belfast is
too confined. You cannot possibly get
sufficient ground within the boundary to
build houses. Land will be required
when the war is over and victory won,
we hope at an early date. If you go outside the city you meet with opposition.
You have the opposition of the individual
who has deliberately gone out with the
object of evading payment of his fair
share of the rates and taxes of the city
while he enjoys all the amenities-gas,
electric light, transport, and sewerage.
He possibly earns his living in the city,
and by residing outside escapes all his
responsibilities.
Again, some of the industries must be
removed outside, and houses will have to
be provided for the workpeople. The
Planning Commission and the authorities
concerned should come together and see
how far they can harmonise in the spreading out of Belfast and the acquiring of
additional land in surrounding districts.
It would be a tremendous mistake at this
Planning Bill
Adjournment
21 DECEMBER 1943
hstage to postpone the Bill.
錄
There may be
amendments in the Committee stage, and
I am confident that the objections from
the two previous speakers will be met.
All I have got to say is that as far as
the city of Belfast is concerned, we are
perfectly satisfied. I do not know that
we will move any amendments; possibly
we will, but I am certain that in the
Committee stage all the objection that
could possibly be raised will be met by
the Government. Therefore, I am sorry
I cannot support the right hon. Gentleman's amendment, and I intend to vote
for the Bill.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I am sorry
that the mantle of the Minister of Home
Affairs seems to have descended upon me.
I do not feel in a position to cover the
ground which has been canvassed by the
speakers who have addressed themselves
to the Bill. The Lord Mayor, I think,
has put the case very fairly and very well.
I think it would be a mistake to turn
down the Bill at this point. Several
points have been made with regard to
the Bill which will come up again for
reconsideration. I think it would
hardly be the place for me to take them
up in detail, and I am satisfied with
commending the Bill again to the House.
In the opinion of the Government there
ought to be planning. That is the great.
principle of every government and every
country in the world to-day. This
Interim Bill is a reasonable measure and
I commend it to the House and ask that
it be given a Second Reading.
Mr. LESLIE : Can the right hon.
Gentleman say would the Government
agree with an Amendment which would
require the production of a scheme within a certain time after the passing of the
Bill? Would that be acceptable to the
Government?
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I am informed that it would be difficult for the
Government at the moment to give a
definite answer to that question. I may
point out, however, that this same point
was made by the hon. and gallant
Senator behind me that the production
of the plan should not be indefinitely
delayed. If the right hon. Gentleman
would put his question at a later date I
am sure he will get an answer to it.
Mr. LESLIE: That, of course, puts
me in a difficulty. The House will remember that my main point was that
there was no intimation given as to the
extent of the interim period and that
was laid stress on and amplified more
correctly than by me by the hon. and
gallant Gentleman on the other side.
Whether the House really wants to give
the measure a chance or not, I confess
I do not feel myself entitled to assume the
mantle of Gordius, and do not like tying
a knot on which the citizens might break
their fingers for the next ten years while
schemes are prepared until a new
Alexander comes forward to cut that knot.
Although the House may be misled by
the Lord Mayor of Belfast's implication
that the Belfast Corporation approves the
Bill, I am inclined to think that the
House, as a whole, would prefer to give
the Bill a chance, and, therefore, I ask
leave to withdraw the Amendment. Anybody can object if he wants to do so.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
On Question, Motion, "That the Bill
be now read a Second time," agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday, 4th January.
Milk Bill.
Order for the House to be put into
Committee read.
Ordered, That the House be put into
Committee on Tuesday, 4th January.
Adjournment.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I beg to
move, "That this House do now
adjourn." We will meet again at halfpast two o'clock on 4th January.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Adjourned accordingly at Four
o'clock until

Tuesday, 23rd February, 1943) of the

the Eighth year of the Reign of His
Majesty King George the Sixth.
